The RED PAGE
Queed —Henry Sydnor Harrison (Con-
stable). An American sentimental story
of considerable, interest. Queed, \a
brusque little man absorbed in a work
on Evolutionary Sociology, is gradually
humanised, becomes a good journalist,
develops his muscles, and evolves from
consuming egoism to heroic altruism.
He is impossible, of course, but if you
read it as a fairy tale for grown-ups the
book will do you good.
The Girondin —Hilaire Belloc (Dymock’s).
Belloc opens with the Revolution in
progress at Bordeaux. The duel and
escape of the Girondin are well done,
though the general features are familiar.
The account of his subsequent career
in the bedraggled skirts of the revolu-
tionary army, of the tramp in the rain,
and the battle of Valmy is a masterly
one. The book is the best of Belloc’s
clever novels.
Wells Brothers —Andy Adams (Constable).
A plain, straightforward narrative of
the lives of two boys who became cattle
kings in Kansas. It is a more telling
picture of the backblocks of America
than the majority of their highly-colored
.romances.
Table D’Hote —W. Pett Ridge (Roycroft
Libi’ary). An entertaining assortment
of hors-d’oeuvres, joints and sweets
made in Pett Ridge’s best style.
Nonsense Novels —Stephen Leacock (Dym-
ock’s). The author of Literary Lapses
is a professor of political economy in
Canada, which does not prevent him
from being extremely humorous. These
“condensed novels” are burlesques
rather than parodies, and will amuse
the reader whether he spots the origi-
nals or not.
AUSTRALIAN WRITERS.—3
Thomas Bailey Clegg
Amongst the half dozen or so who have
written acceptable novels of Australian life,
T. B. Clegg has an honorable place. As will
be seen from his owm story, which follows,
his career as a novelist did not begin until
he had reached mature years, and had gradu-
ated in journalism. The four novels which
stand to his credit are carefully written;
there are no slovenly sentences in them, and
no artificial “flowers.” There are romance,
adventure, excitement, ingenious plots, quiet
humor and the philosophy of an optimist
who believes that though all’s not right with
the world, there is a soul of goodness even in
things evil, which will keep man on the up-
grade. Clegg is an admirer of the sturdy
bushman and the self-reliant, prolific bush-
woman. He is a lover of the country, with-
out despising the town, and all his books are
pervaded by Australian atmosphere, which is
as unobtrusive as that which drifts softly
through The Bulletin office.
The theme of The Love Child (1906) is a
delicate one for a writer in English, and it is
handled delicately. The race must flourish
at all hazards, as the good bush priest of the
story saiys:—
“Strong sons and buxom girls are more
to the world than all the advance in science
and the arts. I’ll back Molly Malone against
the musical glasses any day. It does my
eyes good to see a decent woman with a
clutch of downy-headed rapscallions round
her skirts. Begad, I often think that if I
wasn’t a father I’d be a mother. ’Deed an’
I do.”
“And a good mother you’d be, sir.”
l’d be prolific, anyhow, Vaughan,
an’ that’s getting fast to be an old-fashioned
virtue;.”
The heroine, a healthy bush-bred woman,
marries for ambition, and her unsatisfied
craving for motherhood drives her to adul-
tery. Whether lovely woman stoops to
folly or obeys a natural law, she must
suffer; so Mary dies. It was hardly neces-
sary, however, that her cold-blooded law-
yer husband should be made a Judge just in
time to try the love-child’s father for the
murder of a ruffian who held their secret.
The melodramatic note is stronger in The
Wilderness (1907). The powerful revenge
motive is worked out relentlessly to a pain-
fully tragic end; but a number of twisted
relationships are dragged in for no better
reason than the rewarding of some minor
persons.
The Bishop's Scapegoat (1908) is better
built. The story moves naturally from Eng-
land to Paris, to New Caledonia (where
some wild fighting, and Cacalouch, a fine ad-
venturer, are introduced),then by open boat
to the Queensland coast, on to Sydney, and
finally back to the convict island, where the
question whether the Bishop or his scapegoat
committed the murder in Paris is satisfac-
torily settled.
Joan of the Hills (1909) depends upon its
love interest and admirably-drawn Australian
characters.-' It is less exciting than the
other three; but, on the whole, truer and
better work. All four stories are worth
anyone’s reading now, and' will be as well
worth reading by the next generation. They
are not great, but they are good.
Here is the author’s story of his career:—
Not long ago) in the common round of
that daily task which so rarely furnishes
“all we need to ask,” I came across a link
with my boyhood—an old man, obviously
well educated, at one time intended for the
army, but whose vagrant instinct had led
him abroad. Time had not dealt kindly
with him, and in old age he had to succumb
to its buffets, and seek the relief of the
State. He was a nephew of George Eliot,
though he did not bear the family name of
Evans. Of course I questioned him about his
aunt, but, such is human nature, the one preg-
nant, ineffaceable memory that remained with
him was that she, “tipped” him handsomely
when he was a boy at school. Truly, to
quote the words on her tombstone at High-
gate Cemetery, though in a more materialis-
tic sense, “She lives again in lives made
happier by her presence.” With old mem-
ories stirred, on returning home I looked up
the author who, with Dickens and Thack-
eray, had done so much to brighten the days
of my youth. Turning over the- pages of
The Mill on the Floss this passage cropped
up: “Our instructed vagrancy, which has
hardly time to linger by the hedgerows, but
runs away early to the tropics, and is at
home with palms and banyans—which is
nourished on books of travel, and stretches
the theatre of its imagination to the Zam-
besi.”
T. B. CLEGG.
It took me straight back to a little lend-
ing library in the goldfields town where I
was born. We were too intent on gold get-
ting, in those days, to give much time to
forms of wealth that could not be floated on
the stock market, but we had, nevertheless,
a few score books of what were then known
as “standard authors” on the shelves of our
local library. I can still remember as viv-
idly, as though it had happened yesterday,
the joy of anticipation that filled my young
heart as my father made me free to delve
in this wealth of lettered matter. He put
in my hand a volume of one of Harrison
Ainsworth’s romances, I forget which, for
I read all that were on the shelves in
rapid succession. So I began my “in-
structed vagrancy,” wandering here and
there from Occident to orient, high and low
among the treasures of this little library.
I essayed much with that valiancy of spirit,
which is the precious gift of youth. How
I ploughed through Paradise Lost, and
quailed at the contemplation of Paradise
Regained, fumbled it, and left it as food for
old age, where it still remains! Macaulay,
Scott, every scrap of Thackeray I could lay
my hands on, and every morsel of Dickens!
As I write, the memory of an illustration,
showing a vacant chair before the writing-
table at Gad’s Hill, flashes across my mind.
I grieved for him with many thousand
others, on goldfield, in camp and city, the
world-wide, as one but little less than kin
and more i ban kind. I wonder if our
children will find the same joy in the
genial papers of Charles Dickens that we
found ; or is humor a malleable thing, beaten
out of the environment of the time, needing
to be re-beaten into new forms with new
manners, new conditions ? Only the other
day a friend said to me, “My little chap
has been reading Pickwick lately, and do
you know that he said in sheer innocent
bewilderment, ‘Daddy, were they all mad ?’ ”
My parents, seeing life through glasses
untinted by any rose of romance, placed
me first in their own store on the gold-
fields of Ballarat, and afterwards in a
warehouse in Melbourne, but though I ac-
quired, by striving, some knowledge of
“buying cheap and selling dear,” the heart
was not there to reach success in trade;
so, after some years, having in the mean-
time attained manhood, I reversed the pro-
cess by “selling cheap” what little I had to
sell, and became “a newspaper man.” I
enjoyed being “a newspaper man” at that
time, for life had not yet any clearly-defined
perspective. When, later on, that perspec-
tive became less nebulous, it showed that
for most “newspaper men” the vanishing
Poin.t was not at all a halo of glory set in
a golden shower. I served the Press, an
exacting master, for seven years, the greater
portion of the time as a member of the
Sydney Morning Herald staff. As special
commissioner for that newspaper I visited
New Caledonia and Northern Queensland.
From the vicinity of Cairns down to the
Richmond River, I worked my way by
horse, timber craft, and any other means of
conveyance that offered itself. The exper-
ience thus gained in the French settlement
and the sugar plantations of the Queens-
land coast, furnished, later on, local color
for The Bishop’s Scapegoat.
, At the close of the ’Bo’s, I determined to
make a trip to England, with the dual pur-
pose of seeing the Old Land and qualifying
for the Bar. Landed there, the necessity
for some remunerative work to eke out the
small stock of cash at my command at
once presented itself. It would be possible
to write much about the difficulties which
an unknown and friendless man has to
battle against before he can wrest a crust
from the grimy old city of London. How-
ever, ends were made to meet, and I burned
my boats when the heavy initial fee for
entrance to an Inn of Court was paid. Get
that money back by hook or by crook I
must. Eventually, by means of a scholar-
ship and prizes, it all came back with a
few guineas added. One turned one’s hand
to anything that offered, from reporting
financial meetings in the city to writing
up short stories to catchy titles in the maga-
zine press. There was a constant ebb and
flow of “copy’ ; to and from my diminutive
chambers, under the slanting roof of Staples
Inn, not far from my own Inn of Court-
Grey’s—on the other side of Holborn. With
some other men from across the water I
did a share of “devilling” for Sir Charles
Dilke, who was then writing his “Problems
of Greater Britain.” We found Sir Charles
a generous employer, who paid on effort, not
result —a fact of which I was happily con-
scious at my first business transaction with
him. His method was to send me a note
or card', with a list of subjects he wanted
to be informed upon. What we knew from
our own colonial experience cost little effort
in supplying, but then he so frequently
wanted to know details of matters with
which the “devil” had but the most super-
ficial acquaintance. This meant looking up
files of colonial papers—perhaps for years
back; visits to the Agents-General’s offices
and so on. When the information was
gathered we waited on him. He would, blue
pencil fin hand, devour—for it was a sort of
eating-up process—the slips of “copy” we
brought him. Sheet after sheet of manu-
script would be thrust relentlessly into the
waste-paper basket, here and there one blue
pencilled and put aside, and so the process
would go on, with such tremors on the part
of the “devil” as may be well imagined.
Then, when all was read, the fragments
would be gathered up, and a cheque be
handed over, such a fair and reasonable
cheque as filled the “devil” with pleasant
anticipations of feasts, tobacco and other
good things.
It was in London at this time that I first
got on the bookstalls. A rather instructive
experience. In reply to an advertisement in
one of the daily papers, I sent to the address
given a “shilling shocker”-—works of this
kind were much in evidence at that time;
the vogue being started by Hugh Conway’s
Called Back. 1 endeavored to< shock for all
I was worth; not Mrs. Grundy, of course,
that field went to the lady novelists of a few 1
years later; my shocks were of great ad-
ventures of the respectable do-and-derring
kind. Months went by, and I could get no
account of my wandering shocker; so, con-
cluding that it had gone the way of much
other grist from my mill, I took its loss with
the profound philosophy that only your scrib-
bler acquires. One morning passing a book-
stall —it was that rare thing a sunny morn-
ing in London —I saw, with full amaze, my
lost shocker in an illustrated paper cover. Such
a cover! It seemed to hit me square in the
face; first the yellow, then the red, then an
awful combination of the two, with inter-
mingled blobs of green or blue. Truly I had
reached the bookstalls—but in such motley.
As I lean back in my chair and smoke, the
satire of the thing comes home. I pursued
the wretched publisher, and with my newly-
acquired store of law so held him up that in
the end he yielded to me in good currency of
the country a sum greater than I have re-
ceived in later days for a novel that has
cost me months of evenings filched from the
company of the home circle.
Having qualified in what was to have
been my profession, I returned to Australia,
put my name on chambers, made a few
assaults on the courts, and then came the
financial crisis. As somebody once remarked :
“The crisis has come, and brought its things
with it.” What it brought is an old tale. It
changed many lives from their currents, my
own among the number, and now I am a
steady-going public servant, with all thoughts
of the woolsack vanished years ago. But
the titillation of the scribbler’s itch never
wholly ceases ; and this perhaps accounts for
the four novels to my name — The Love
Child, The Wilderness, The Bishop’s Scape-
goat, and Joan of the Hills. They were pub-
lished in England, and to those who contem-
plate doing anything in the same way, I
would, in passing, remark that England is a
long way off. Ido not know much about the
ways of publishers, but have often felt that I
would like to know a great deal more. Why,
for example, they should continue to lose
such vast sums of money in exploiting the
works of unknown authors. Perhaps Bar-
abbas was also a philanthropist. I have no
fault to find with my own publishers, they
have, at all events, paid me something—and
that is better than nothing, which, I under-
stand, has in other cases with other pub-
lishers been, if we set aside that virtue of
authorship which may be its own reward,
the sole guerdon of aspiring fame. The
writing of qovels is nowadays as much a
trade as the selling of any other kind of soft-
goods, and to do business, you must be a
business-man, and must be on the spot. True,
you can w r ork through agents, but agents,
like all commercial men, find it more profit-
able to sell well-known and well-advertised
brands. ’ How to become well-known and
well-advertised is a secret —the secret which
allied, and in the end it must be so allied,
with something of real merit, spells success
whether in fictional literature or Blowser’s
Pills.
Yet if to be ‘‘one of the best sellers,” and,
consequently, fill one’s purse, is not the sole
object of your author, he may gain some re-
ward from his act of creation. He has
brought a new life into the world. It may
be a poor thing, but it is his own; and he
cherishes this often maimed, misshapen bit
of creation with the self-satisfied affection of
a parent—if the world buffets it, what mat-
ter, it is the way of the world, says Sir
Egoist, and they could not appreciate all
that lies in my beautiful child. He has his
consolations, truly!
More Confessions of a Beach-
comber
E. J. Banfield’s first book, The Confes-
sioris of a Beachcomber, of which a new edi-
tion has been issued at 3s.- 6d., nas met with
well-deserved success. The writer happened
to be just the man for a unique opportunity.
Other men have secluded themselves, or have
been marooned on more or less lonely
islands without much profit to anyone else.
Banfield is a voluntary Orusoe on a little
island within-sight of the Queensland coast,
who has studied every phase of life upon it,
and has set down the most interesting oi
his results in a bright style. He knows the
virtue of “not too much.” His details are
always sufficient to make a clear picture of
the episode in hand, but never enough to
bore. One book could not hold all the good
things he had to tell about his life on Dunk
Island, where he has lived with his wife
since 1897; and, in response to several sug-
gestions he has published another volume —
My Tropic Isle (Fisher Unwin).
In the new book Banfield has filled in a
few blanks in the first, especially with re-
gard to the building of a house by his own
inexperienced hands, the food, cost of living,
etc.; and then passed on to fresh anecdotes of
the cunning worms and cute fishes, the play
and reasoning power of certain birds, the
habits of the sea-cucumber or beche-de-mer,
the. curious bivalves and other small deer
which frequent his island ; besides the antics
of his solitary horse, and the amazing skill
and superstition of his black neglibors.
Was there ever a white man who rejoiced
so exuberantly as Banfield does in the magi-
cal power of the tropical sun? Here is a
paean to His Majesty:—
~ great and glorious and mighty sun!
Uh, commanding majestical sun! Superfine
invigorator; bold illuminator of the dim
spaces of the brain; originator of the glow
which distills its rarest attars! Am I not
thy true, thy joyful knight? Hast thou not
touched my toughened unflinching shoulders
with the flat.of thy burnished sword? Do I
not behold its. jew'elled hilt flashing with
pearls and precious stones as thou sheathest
it for the night among the purple western
lulls? Do I not hail its golden gleams
among the fair-barked trees what time each
scented morn I milk my skittish goats?
That outburst followed some eloquent re-
marks upon the foolishness of clothes, and
the virtue and delight of bhthing the body in
sunlight and ocean when the thermometer is
at 112 deg. The life changed him from a
pale weakling of Bst. 41b. to a tough brown
man of lOst. 21b., which is a good ad. for the
island, even if it were not otherwise made to
appear like an earthly paradise. An exten-
sive circulation of Banfield’s'books will cer-
tainly; start many people on the quest for
other similarly delectable spots, without re-
flecting that the successful Crusoe is
born, not made. A woman like Mrs. Gunn
found roses and incense in the unpromising-
desert of the Never Never.
Readers of the Confessions will be glad to
extend their knowledge of Dunk Island and
the unprofessional beachcomber; but My
Tropic Isle is complete in itself. The reader
will be inclined to agree with the author
that “the whole world might be brighter and
better if coral reefs were more generously
distributed.”

Edward Dyson’s Fact’ry ’Ands is one of
the most humorous books written in Aus-
tralia ; and readers will welcome a further
collection of stories about the ’Ands pub-
lished by the N.S.W. Bookstall Co. as Benno
and Some of the Push. Most of the yarns
have appeared in The Bulletin; but Norman
Lindsay’s illustrations were made specially
for the book.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Song of the Engines.
Have you heard the engines singing when we’re home- !
ward bound at sea ?
Have you heard their anthem ringing with a beat
that’s true and 'free? :
Have you heard them . loudly pounding when each
turn we’re nearer home
And her bows the seas are crushing into rolls of
sparkling foam ? ,
Twelve knots, by God! Twelve knots point two,
And she’s only a ten-knot ship !
Shows you what the old tank can do!
We’re ahead of our time this trip.
Sixty-one, sixty-two, sixty-three, sixty-four!
(With a wake like a white ramrod).
Hark to her voice, with a rattle and a roar,
“Twelve point two, by God!”
Have you heard the engines swearing when a head-
wind comes along ?
Have you heard them madly tearing in a harsh and
angry song ?
Have you ever heard them racing like an eight-day
Yankee clock
As she brings up like a live thing with a shudd’ring,
pulsing shock ?
Oh, curse this wind ! We’re down to seven!
(Listen to that shafting swear!)
With decent luck we’d do eleven!
(It’s grunting like a beast down there!)
We’re going to miss our tide for a cert this trip,
For our wake’s like a wriggling snake.
Our Sunday’s gone! Oh, it gives a man the pip!
(Gurr-rump !) How they shake!
Have you heard the engines laughing when the wind
has fallen light?
Have you heard them lightly chaffing when the home
port is in sight?
Have you heard them gaily chanting in their sta-
tions dark and drear,
When she’s headed for the entrance, and the end of
toil is near?
We’ll soon be there! We’ll soon be there
(By the Lord! But we’ve caught our tide!
She’s measuring her beat with care.
Half an hour, and we’ll be inside)—
Sixty-one, sixty-two, sixty-three, sixty-four!
(With a wake like a white ramrod).
Hark to her voice with a rattle and a roar !
Twelve point two, by God!”
N.S.W.
A. FOSTER BAYNES.
En Menage.
I thought I should have died, indeed,
When first my Nesta made a cake.
Dislike of sweets 1 dared not plead . . .
I thought I should have died indeed.
For hours and hours I watched her knead,
For days I throbbed, I was one ache. ...
I thought I should have died indeed,
When first my Nesta made a cake!
I had some very feverish weeks
When first my Nesta joined a club.
Her nattire ran to vivid streaks;
I had some very feverish weeks.
Of these things she no longer speaks,
But deems me heartless —there’s the rub !
I had some very feverish .weeks
When first my Nesta joined a club.
I tore my hair and hid myself
When first my Nesta’s, baby came.
It scared me frightfully,, that elf;
I tore my hair and hid’ myself.
I lay unnoticed on the shelf,
The merest pawn in that new game.
I tore mv hair and hid myself
When first my Nesta’s baby came.
I was delighted—yes, but shy—
When Nesta’s room was mine once more.
I held her close, I heard her sigh;
I was delighted—yes! . . . but shy.
A sacred sweetness whispered by
From that small cradle on the floor.
I was delighted, yes; but shy
When. .. Nesta’s room was mine once more.
MAORI LAND.
SELWYN RIDER.
The Bellman.
Old Bellman Time is going his rounds;
Far down the street his warning sounds,
A cry monotonous and dread —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
The least is set, and hearts beat strong
With wine and revelry and song.
But, hark! a clanging sounds ahead —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
The fatal sign is on each door
The grim old Bellman stops before;
So leave your work and board and bed ■
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Sweet child, with lips and eyes of light,
The world was fair and good and bright;
How soon was all its radiance shed !
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Brave youth, true maiden, dreaming dreams
Of endless bliss by shining streams;
The vision fades, the dream is sped—
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Proud man, play on your little hour
Your play of pride and wealth and power,
By Avarice and Ambition led
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead I
Priest, round whose altar ever clings
The shade of dark and devilish things;
The old gods go; their faiths are fled —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Kings, rulers, warriors and lords,
Put up, put up your useless swords!
No longer shall the earth run red —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Bring out, and place upon the cart
Each perished fancy of the heart,
Each idol dwindled to a shred —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead.
Reluctance and regret were vain;
Naught may return, and naught remain;
Then, follow in the Bellman’s tread —
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead /
At yonder all-consuming pyre
Goes up in flame each heart’s desire;
Nor any Phoenix-wings are spread
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
Old Time, the Bellman, goes his rounds;
Far down the years his clanging sounds:
The pyre of Change must still be fed
Bring out your Dead! Bring out your Dead!
N.S.W.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for ‘'copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 31, 1911.
FREEDOM-AND FUDGE.
Thebe is a Freetrade society in Sydney, and
the other night Alfred Conroy, ex-M.H.R.,
lectured to its members on the Lost Cause,
and, incidentally, on the deplorable restric-
tions on Liberty—social, military and in-
dustrial—which prevail in Australia. The
fustian which Conroy of the Yap indulged
in—vague stuff concerning the “inestimable
blessings of freedom of commerce,” “the in-
alienable liberty of the British subject,” the
Englishman’s “home” being “his castle,” and
so forth —had a certain 'vogue as lately as
naif a dozen years ago. To-day it appears
as out-of-date as velocipedes or bustles. Eng-
land is menaced with Revolution, owing to an
overdose of the very article which the Free-
trade society wants to saddle on this coun-
try. For years Britain’s industries have
been battered and shelled and undermined
by foreign rivals. The effect of that relent-
less war has been felt in an increasing degree
during the past decade or so. It is rapidly
becoming intolerable. The average English
industry finds it hard, if not impossible, to
pay more than starvation wages, and, as a
result, the wage-earners are rising in a des-
perate frenzy of revolt, and millionaire earls
like Lord Derby have been constrained to
insure their ancestral halls against mob-
violence on extravagant terms, and obscure
citizens, like tbe late Leonard Worsey, have
been shot dead by their own countrymen.
These things are happening solely because
the country is swamped with over-produced
foreign goods—articles which may be cheap
at the price, but which millions of English-
men cannot buy because they cannot earn
the money to buy them with. The nation is
paying for freedom of trade —which is to
say, for undefended trade—with such an
army of unemployed and unemployables as
the world has not hitherto seen ; and, seem-
ingly, the end is not yet.
* * * *
The cranks who, in the teeth of all the
evidence, continue barracking for this fatal
system are—logically, no doubt —in favor
of complete freedom in other walks of life.
They look on our alien restriction laws as
immoral and uneconomic. Their ideal is
freedom of ingress, the time-dishonored Brit-
ish fetich which nas made England in gen-
eral, and London in particular, refuges for
the world’s desperadoes and outcasts. This
unrestricted freetrade in human beings, in
which Britain has engaged for so long, has
not only brought the Gold Country into bad
odor among ioreign nations, which resent
sanctuary being given to their law-breakers ;
it has also dragged thousands of British
workers down to the level of their dirt-cheap
foreign competitors. And it is likewise re-
sponsible for an increasing exodus of all that
is strongest and most enterprising among its
working classes. Thus the Cold Country, under
this system, is continuously giving new
lamps for old —exchanging healthy, reput-
able workers for insanitary and often crimi-.
nal wasters. The Freetrade enthusiasts who
gathered at the feet of Conroy the other
night reviled Arbitration and Conciliation
legislation when it was first mooted in Aus-
tralia as a grotesque attempt to restrict the
free operation of the laws of supply and de-
mand. People like Bruce Smith found the
proposal more insupportable to the temper
of free Britons even than a tariff. They
urged that the worker should be suffered to
sell his labor, and the employer be left to
buy it, without the intervention of any third
party, whether the same was a Judge or a
Board or the State; and they had the
matchless effrontery to urge it in the sacred
name of Freedom. And so with Compulsory
Service and a dozen other democratic and
defensive measures that might be named.
The point that requires to be driven
firmly home every time a Tory, masquerad-
ing under the guise of Liberalism, attempts
to justify his opposition to progressive legis-
lation by means of cant about Freedom, is
that without laws, and rigid ones, there can
be no freedom for anyone. One of the essen-
tials of real freedom in the world as it is con-
stituted to-day is money. The worker who
is hanging on to an ill-paid job by his finger-
nails, unable to put by anything week by
week, and in chronic dread of the sack, does
not know freedom. He is not free to go
away and rest if phthisis smites him; he
cannot see the world and its beauties ; nine-
tenths of the amenities of life are denied
him. In England, as elsewhere, Freetrade is
responsible indirectly for the fact that mil-
lions of workers who, after they have swal-
lowed a few beers, are ready and willing to
howl “Britons never shall be slaves,” are, in
reality, in a hopeless condition of bondage.
A tariff, as America and Germany have
demonstrated, provides regular jobs, and jobs
provide wages, lacking which a vast section
of every nation must be in a worse position,
for all practical purposes, than many of the
nigger slaves of eighteenth-century America.
Here in Australia we have got a tariff, in-
adequate in spots, but still a useful article,
take it for all in all. Yet, left to itself, it will
not procure true freedom. It must be but-
tressed by countless devices—laws to force
manufacturers to pay fair wages and pro-
vide sanitary workshops; laws to compel
workers to insure against invalidity; laws
to prevent the formation of injurious Trusts
and to destroy those which are already in
existence. The ultimate end of all these
measures is not to fleece or ruin the em-
ployer, or rob him of one iota of his lawful
freedom, but simply to provide the classes
that hitherto have not known such things
with (1) living wages, (2) facilities to get
a measure of physical and intellectual enjoy-
ment out of life, (3) absolute security
against want. With those advantages, the
humblest citizen is free —not so free, possi-
bly. as the millionaire whose time is his own,
and whose playground is the world, but
still free enough. Without them he is no
freer than a plough-horse.
* * * *
There is another form of freedom to which
those who ask for license to import cheap
goods and labor, lay claim; namely, the free-
dom to leave their native land undefended.
The deadliest shaft these folks levelled at
the Fisiier Government’s Compulsory Ser-
vice Bill was that it “interfered with the
liberty of the subject.” Yet these same people
are only enabled to eat their meals in their
own homes, without loaded firearms beside
their plates, because the liberty of the sub-
ject is interfered with by the State, in so far
as burglary is concerned. We are free to
walk abroad in security for no other reason
than that restrictions are placed, by law, on
highway robbery. Life would indeed be an
intolerably narrow and comfortless and
furtive business if checks were not placed on
people’s freedom to restrict the freedom of
others —from the jostling of passers in the
street, or accosting their womenfolk, up to
the license to shoot strangers on sight.
In this way compulsory military ser-
vice, the policy which the advocates
of undefended trade and undefended la-
bor quite consistently assail, stands really
for the highest possible form of freedom —
national freedom. The “conscripted” Ger-
man knows it. The Korean doesn’t; and
though for it he would probably be prepared
to face the bondage of many, drills, he never
will know it now.
* * * *
Our modern whole-hog individualists are
the heirs (unconsciously, in many cases) of
the robber barons who raged impotently
when Society first organised itself sufficiently
to be able to put an embargo on murder,
theft and kindred crimes. They want the
nation to be vitiated by a Freetrade tariff for
the enrichment of a tiny aristocracy of
agents, capitalists and importers; they hold
that so long as there are workers ready to
submit to the exactions of sweating employ-
ers, the State has no right to interfere with
the process; and, above all, they hold that
no one should be forced to defend anything
—not even his country’s honor—because
compulsion is un-English, and the negation
of that Freedom Avhich is the Briton’s in-
alienable birtli-riglit. It is a grotesque and
horrible misuse of an inspiring Avord. To
believe that this is Freedom is to credit
Freedom Avith more than half the Avorld’s
misery and crime.
ON THE ADVANTAGES OF AN ELOCU-
TIONARY TRAINING.
The training of the speaking voice is a
thing which counts. Dr. John Watson
once declared that Avhen church people de-
scribed a sermon as good, they usually only
meant that they had heard it. Clear enun-
ciation, fine modulation of the voice, a care-
ful study of the devices by which pathos
and humor -can be simulated, and a skill in
gesture acquired by long practice, make up
a general stock-in-trade Avhich may very
readily enable the oAvner to pass for a saint,
an orator, or a doAvnright earnest man, as
occasion may demand. Among the rank and
file of churchmen, politicians, or workers for
any social reform the trained elocutionist has
an enormous advantage; and lack of per-
sonal force, real grip of vital questions, and
conscientious convictions can be more than
balanced by a studied use of words and
phrases which tickle the public ear, and
SAvay an audience even against its better
judgment. The oratory of the Greeks was
largely a matter of elocutionary skill, and it
Avas a recognised axiom of the Hellenic
Avorld that the thinker who could not ex-
press his thought with grace and force
might as well have saved himself the trouble
of thinking at all. It is true that in mod-
ern times virile and rugged spirits have
triumphed over their lack of the cultivated
voice and modulated utterance by the sheer
power of their big manhood. An Abraham
Lincoln or a Richard Seddon might win
his triumphs by the tremendous personal
appeal of his own natural strength; but
smaller men are well advised if they study
the talker’s art Avith infinite attention to
detail. And the smaller they may happen
to be, the greater the height of popular
esteem to which a complete knowledge of
elocution may carry them.
That notorious person, William Shaks-
peare, who is reputed to have knoAvn quite
a number of things about human life, elab-
orated the triumph of the elocutionist over
the mere natural orator in a melodrama
which turned upon the central historical
fact of several unpleasantly-disposed people
sticking their knives into Julius
After the puncturing, Brutus, the honor-
able leader of the band of patriotic cut-
throats, addressed the Roman mob. In big,
natural, rolling periods he declared his
motive, and excused his action; and the
crowd, swayed by his personality, recognised
him as a genuinely honest man, and shouted
their applause. Mark Antony followed.
He said he was no orator as Brutus was;
but he refrained from remarking that every
trick of the trained elocutionist and skilled
actor was his. With tears in his cultured
voice, and with shallow Avords Avell-measured
to the capacity of his hearers, he sAvayed the
throng at his Avill; and, as a result, Brutus
Avas soon running for his life, and the whole
Roman Republic had petered out before a
few stage tricks and cleverly-spoken phrases.
It was the triumph of easily-modulated
words over that passion for truth and right
which had been furnaced in the flames of
knowledge and experience. In a word, the
little man who knew how to use voice and
gesture to suit the occasion, made himself
for a moment greater than the greatest man
in Rome.
The value of the study of elocution cannot
be over-estimated. The ambitious Australian
who would figure largely on the passing stage
should get to work at once. Good masters
can teach him much ; and by constant prac-
tice along well-defined lines something like
perfection may be achieved. A suitable ar-
rangement of mirrors is a great help to a
mastery of proper and dignified gesture, and
—if the suburban landlady will tolerate it —
an hour’s recitation of showy selections
from poets and prose writers may be in-
dulged in before breakfast. People who decry
the art as one of a trifling and unimportant
kind, to be rated with step-dancing and
tight-rope-walking, are hopelessly overborne
by the stern logic of experience. It is the
one and only method by which a person of
the most moderate talent and the most slen-
der endowment in the way of ordinary intel-
ligence may be made to appear as if possessed
of transcendent genius. For the very reason
that his thought is scanty, and his grasp of
affairs weak, the man who knows how to
make the most of every syllable he utters is
bound, when he addresses a crowd whose in-
tellectual level is about equal to his own, to
duly impress them and win their highest re-
gard. He decks the shop-front with tinsel,
and the glitter readily passes for silver and
gold. In the case, however, of the aspirant
to honors who may really be possessed of
something special in the way of brains, the
tricks and graces of the elocutionist must be
used somewhat sparingly; for were these
used to their fullest advantage by a man of
real ability and weight, there would be no
saying to what dangerous heights of reform
and improved conditions he might not lead
the people. To the painstaking, mediocre
person who desires to shine with all the bril-
liance of a Chinese lantern or an actress’s
teeth, the way is wide open. Though in its
nature a small art, elocution may be pro-
ductße of much applause and no little profit.
The most prominent man in New South
Wales to-day is W. A. Holman, elocutionist.
Only a fortnight ago the air was full of
acrimonious aspersions on his political char-
acter. Wild men asserted that he w y as a
traitor to his party, and an enemy to the
greater Australian national cause. To-day
he walks abroad, cleansed, perfumed, ab-
solved pf all guilt, and wearing what old
Chaucer called a “spiced conscience.” Yet
Mr. Holman did oppose the Referenda pro-
posals of his party, did help to put back the
clock of Australian progress, did aid in pre-
venting that which would have made effect-
ive the New Protection, whereby the workers
would have received the full advantage of
every increase in wages, did obstruct the
machinery by which the Commonwealth
could have readily shaken off the Trusts and
Combines that have her by the throat, did
use his weight and influence against the
granting of powers to the Federation which
would have enabled it to deal effectively with
larger industrial matters, did prove Bmself
in the eyes of all men to be a small States
Spiter of thoroughly parochial mind. These
are plain facts Avhich no P.L.L. conference
can dispose of or deny. Moreover, the posi-
tion remains unchanged; for the man has
given no hint of repentance, nor made any
promise of more worthy action in the future.
Yet Mr. Holman goes forth with the marks
of the whitewash brush all over him. A
huge majority of his party in the State has
decided to ignore all his sins, and allow him
to continue on his doubtful way. Why?
Reviewing his Avhole career, and endeavoring
to find in him any virtues which may coun-
terbalance in the smallest degree his flagrant
offences, one is forced to a direct conclusion.
Mr. Holman is upheld by the party because
he is a good elocutionist. It is his sole out-
standing merit; and it Avould seem that in
the deliberate vieAv of the New South Wales
Labor Leagues the elocutionary skill of the
man who knoAvs lioav to handle words and
phrases, and to cleverly simulate emotion, is
of more importance than the larger Aus-
tralianism, and the good of the people of a
continent. Henceforth, earnestness of pur-
pose, deep conviction, higher patriotism and
all that made the Labor party the hope of
sturdy Australian men and women are
things of the past as far as one State is con-
cerned. Brutus is doAvn, and Mark ax-
tony holds the mob at his will.
THE FRUSTRATING OF SOME IMPU-
DENT POLITICAL JOBBERS.
During most of last week there was Parlia-
mentary anarchy in N. S. Wales, and the un-
savory spectacle of elderly politicians bark-
ing inarticulately at one another after the
manner of undisciplined dogs. The basic
cause of the uproar lay in the fact that there
happened to be 45 Laborites in the'Assem-
bly and likewise 45 “Liberals.” Our ridiculous
system of Party Government insists 1 on the
constituencies returning a majority for one
side or another if Parliament is to be work-
able. The electors of Mudgee and Liver-
pool Plains naturally didn’t take this
into consideration when they went to
the polls the other day, just as the electors
of the State at large will certainly decline to
take it into consideration at the next general
election ; and, consequently, failing the pro-
vision of a Speaker by one side or the other,
business stood to be held up indefinitely.
Now, it is more than usually desirable that
certain Parliamentary business shall be put
through without delay. The recent census
indicated that there must be a redistribution
of seats before a proper reflex of the State’s
mind can be gained at a general election;
and, anyhow, the law insists on it. So no
one is opposed to redistribution. A further
matter on which all parties are agreed is
the necessity of amending the Electoral Act.
The Labor party found in Henry Willis a
“Liberal” who was willing to take the chair
for the purpose of carrying tli'ese two needed
measures, and these only. The “Liberals”
had no Labor Speaker offering; and it is cer-
tain that if they strove from now till the
crack of doom they wouldn’t- get one. The
prospect of their gaining office Avas, accord-
ingly, rotten. And so were their proposals
for dealing with, the situation. Put in the
baldest terms, they urged that Parliament
should immediately go, under the old Elec-
toral Law, to the constituencies (the same
constituencies which had quite recently re-
turned 45 “Liberals” and 45 Laborites —a pro-
cedure which, as above mentioned, rendered
government an impossibility) and see if.
haply, the constituencies mightn’t this time
return more “Liberals” and feAver Laborites,
or vice versa. If they didn’t, Parliament
would presumably go to the country all over
again.
* * * *
Labor objected to this course. And the
most enthusiastic anti-Laborite Avill hardly
deny that in objecting it was acting in the
best interests of the taxpayers, however much
the party incidentally benefited itself or pre-
judiced C. G. Wade and Go. General elec-
tions are not things to lightly impose on a
community Avhich has its living to earn.
Apart from the dislocation of business, and
the waste of time that they <ause, each one
of them costs some £50,000. The Wade fol-
lowers viewed with frenzy the acceptance of
the Speakership by Henry Willis, not be-
cause they cared a threepenny malediction
who was Speaker, but because they recog-
nised that in an immediate general elec-
tion lay “Liberalism’s” last faint hope of
office. One explanation of this is that, to
borrow the exulting phrase of a “Liberal”
daily, “the ‘Liberals’ have never been better
organised than they are now.” Money has
been poured into the party’s exchequer, and
it has been dealt out with a free hand in the
places where it was likely to do most good.
State Labor’s funds are low; the party is far
from united (owing chiefly to the attitude of its
leaders on the Referenda questions), and it
will take some months to regain its old
solidity. The Electoral Law, as it stands,
disfranchises the large nomadic class
(shearers and the like), 95 per cent, of
whom are Laborites —a disability which the
projected provision tor voting by post will
remedy. And, finally, and above everything,
each day the Labor Administration stays in
office means further kudos for it, and, as a
result, additional support. A party is judged,
largely, on its administration, and as regards
the administrative capacity of the Labor
team, the anti-Labor papers are the most
eloquent witnesses. Taking all these factors
into consideration, it is understandable that
“Liberalism” should be enraged at the pros-
pect of the Labor party carrying on. In
the present condition of the Electoral Law,
and with the constituencies apportioned in
the unrepresentative manner they are, there
would be. at any rate, a chance of the
“Liberals” making good if a general elec-
tion were to occur at once. It is true that
they would be forced themselves to bring m
a Redistribution of Seats Bill and a Bill
amending the Electoral Law, and they
would have to follow up these measures
with another general election. But, anyhow,
they would enjoy a further term in office;
and they would then face the country with
the 5 per cent, advantage that the part.v •“
lower is supposed to hold when campaign-
ing.
* * *
Last week’s position, therefore, was that
the Laborites, since they were able to find a
Speaker, were in the position of being; an
to offer N. S. Wales the legislation it in-
quires, at the price of one general election.
The best that the “Liberals” could do was to
offer the same legislation at the price ofat
least two general elections—a bargain tnai
a reasonable State would obviously decl •
Recognising the logic of this, anti-Labont


who have sufficient decency to be ashamed of
the outrageous outburst of “Liberal” larrikin-
ism that defiled last week’s proceedings in
Parliament, are now contending that, if C. G.
Wade had only been “approached properly”
by Holman, he would have agreed to some
unspecified “arrangement,” whereby the same
result could have been attained peaceably.
But the yarn cuts no ice. Before the Labor-
ites secured Willis’s assurance that he
would accept the Speakership, the “Liberal”
leaders and papers breathed fire and slaughter
against any adherent of the party who
dreamed of such a betrayal of the cause — i.e.,
who dreamed of doing anything to avert an
instant dissolution. After Willis had com-
mitted himself, he was treated, both in the
“Liberal” press and by “Liberals” in Parlia-
ment, as :he uncleanest breed of pariah.
Later, during2oweary hours, “Liberalism” ex-
hausted the vocabulary of the gutter respect-
ing the infamy o’ the Labor party’s efforts
to get a Speaker from the Opposition side of
the House — i.e., to overt a dissolution. At
the dead finish of the disreputable display
Willis was suddenly placed in the Chair, as
provided for by the Orders of the House.
Then chaos and blasphemy broke loose afresh
in the ranks of “Liberalism,” to be followed
swiftly by the preposterous yarn that, at the
time of the elevation of Willis, “Liberal-
ism” was on the verge of proposing McCourt
for the job. No one believed this story; it
is unlikely that anyone was expected to
believe it. The sole object of the crusade of
bluster, vituperation, blasphemy and slander
in which the Wade party plunged neck-deep
last week, was to force on the country an
immediate and wholly unnecessary general
election, and thus advantage “Liberalism” at
the second and necessary election. In other
words, the party which claims to stand for
law and order has made a discreditable hog
of itself in a fruitless effort to bleed the State
of £50,000 towards the party’s election ex-
penses. It was an audacious attempt at day-
light robbery, which the Labor party, in con-
junction with Willis, did well to frustrate.
A DREAM OF “BROKE” HOSPITALS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was Lunatic Asylum Saturday. From 8
a.m. some hundreds of ladies, none the less
earnest for the fact that the publicity and
advertisement of the street-collection game
keenly appealed to them, had been shaming
small coins out of the jeans of the multitude.
The daily papers helped on the good work
for all they were worth. They printed the
collectors’ names, the streets where they
were stationed, and mendacious statements
concerning their powers of persuasion and
all-round allurement. Young men with cam-
eras were to be seen on every side. From
time to time they would pose a stout gentle-
man in a plug hat in the act of dropping a
coin into a money-box held by a female in a
race-frock and a galvanised smile. At dif-
ferent points, along the street, similar
females were being snapped acting similarly
with policemen, Chows, hod-carriers, par-
sons, and other units of our great demo-
cracy. The results were destined to appear
in one or other of the agricultural weeklies
labelled “Wealth —and the Charmer,” “The
Mongol’s Mite,” “The Law Unbends,” or
some such breezy caption. Everyone, bar-
ring the collectors and a comparatively
small band of erotomaniacal youths, avid for
flirtation at any price, loathed Lunatic Asy-
lum Saturdays more than a hot northerly
in a dusty thoroughfare. But, as the papers
pointed out, the hospitals for the insane
were in a bad way. The public, on whom the
entirely consistent Anti-Socialist Govern-
ment of the day relied for their upkeep, had
not come forward with subscriptions in the
manner expected of it. As a result, the
mad-houses which were not frankly insol-
vent were so near it as not to matter. De-
mented persons were being turned away
daily by the asylum authorities, for the
sound reason that there were no beds or
attendants or padded cells or other conveni-
ences for them. Many households had con-
sequently to house their own lunatics, and
the howls of the afflicted ones disturbed the
neighbors’ rest, and proved a constant source
of worry and humiliation to their relatives.
Occasionally a homicidal maniac escaped from
his amateur warders, and it mostly mean!, a
horrible death for someone. Hence Lunatic
Asylum Saturday. It was a well-meaning
institution, but it did little real good, and
year by year the asylums got broker and
broker, and less and less fitted to cope with
the demands on them.
* * * *
As a matter of fact, the insignificant sec-
tion of the public which gives voluntarily to
institutions of this sort had too many calls
on its generosity. For example, there were
the constantly-recurring Gaol Balls. These
grim festivities were organised by the leaders
of Sydney society. Tickets cost from half
a guinea to a guinea apiece. For this sum
a dancing hall, a perfectly rotten band, and
a beer-and-mineral-water supper were pro-
vided. The profits went to the upkeep of
the gaols. But they were hopelessly inade-
quate. There were always two prisoners
offering for every available cell, and the pay
of a gaol official was as precarious as that
of a private in a South American regiment.
Despite the efforts of the Society ladies, the
prison authorities had not the funds either
to extend the gaol premises as need arose, or
to support the malefactors they already had
in hand. Up country, the old-time method
of chaining felons by the legs to the trunks
of trees was reverted to —an unsatisfactory
arrangement at best. The people whose cash
and energy kept the asylums and gaols stag-
gering on their uncertain way were also
levied upon by means of bazaars, raffles,
socials, house-to-house collections, and pretty
well every other means short of highway
robbery, for the upkeep of the State’s refor-
matories, penitentiaries, benevolent homes,
morgues and so forth. They gave according
to their means, albeit blasphemously in many
cases. Meanwhile the hardy economists who
lacked the public spirit, or idiocy, or weak-
mindedness, or whatever it is that impels
people to disburse liard-won coin in a foolish
cause, sat back and smiled. And, year by
year, these hospitals for the morally or men-
tally unfit, which should have been sup-
ported by taxes evenly distributed over the
whole ot the money-earners and money-own-
ers of the community, went from bad to
worse.
* . * * *
The writer had got thus far -when he sud-
denly woke up and recollected that, as a
matter of fact, the State takes the entire
burden of supporting its gaols, morgues, asy-
lums, reformatories, etc., on its own shoul-
ders, and has never left any part of that
duty to the doubtful mercies of Private En-
terprise. The State looks after the mentally-
afflicted in its midst, and does the job well;
is only the physically-afflicted whom it
allows to be turned away from its semi-
bankrupt hospitals. The gaols are run by
the State on lavish lines. But the hospi-
tals, on whose proper conduct the health of
the community depends, are kept in a condi-
tion of chronic starvation. The money is
cheerfully provided out of the consolidated
revenue for the upkeep of dozens of resorts
where decrepit old people and unruly young
ones are attended to; for tne upkeep of those
institutions wherein the lives of those most
useful to the community are lost or won, as
the case may be, reliance is largely placed on
charity.
Dazedly tlie writer drops his pen and
broods on what is perhaps the most rankly
illogical situation of modern times.
H. SINGEMOINE.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Tlie Deadly Results of Federation and
Protection.
“The ghastly error of Federation,” as “Lib-
eral” politicians of the mental calibre of J.
C. L. Fitzparrot are fond of squawking,
may be observed with the naked eye in the
last official publication issued by the Ma
State’s Department of Labor and Industry.
In the metropolitan district, which covers an
area of 1530 square miles, it is shown that
since Federation arrived in 1901 the number
of factories has increased by 1761, and the
workers employed by 36,850. A compari-
son of the figures in periods of five
years discovers the fact that although
the increase in hands employed from
1900 to 1905 was 10,080, from 1905 to
1910 it was no less than 21,901, and, more
satisfactory still, male employment practi-
cally held its own in that large increase. The
most conspicuous increase was in the metal
and machinery trades; there in the past
five years alone the register of employees
ran up from 4867 to 9376, an increase of
nearly 100 per cent. In the clothing and
textile fabrics industry the hands emploved
increased from 15,793 to 21,354 —also in five
years. The year before Federation started
in to “blight the Mother State’s great
future,” there were in the Sydney metro-
politan district 2047 factories Oja the regis-
ter, employing 39,104 persons; at the end of
last year there were 3670 registered estab-
lishments providing a living for 71,085 per-
sons and those depending upon them; while
the horse-power of machinery used in the
same period pulled up from 16,179 to
56,909. Out upon this Protection!

The Dawn of a Great Reform.
Speaking to 200 clergymen in Mel-
bourne last week, the Victorian Acting-
Minister for Health announced the State
Government’s policy regarding the sup-
pression of syphilis. He remarked, in effect,
that the committee which was recently ap-
pointed to enquire into the ravages of this
disease, had found it to be, in every respect,
the unspeakable horror it had been repre-
sented. And he asked the churches to co-
operate in an educational campaign which
the Government proposes shortly to insti-
tute. That is all to the good, so far as it
goes. Indeed, the clamant need of some-
thing of the sort is strikingly illustrated in
the very issue of the Age which handles the
meeting. Neither in the big daily’s report,
nor in the leading article which it published
on the matter, did it bluntly name this com-
plaint. concerning which there is as little
need for genteel periphrasis as there is in
regard to phthisis or appendicitis. If the
syphilis curse is to be grappled with effect-
ively, it will have to be dragged out of the
atmosphere of mvstery and suggestiveness
in which it has flourished for so long, and
be paraded in the cleansing light of open
discussion. No one’s morals are going to
be injured in the process. In fact, if young
people—tlie future fathers and mothers of
Australia —were thoroughly instructed in
the nature and possibilities of this affliction,
the knowledge would do more to anchor them
to the Narrow Path than many sermons.
Their clergy, the newspapers, their parents
and their teachers might tell them, as oc-
casion arose, that syphilis is responsible for
very nearly all cases of general paralysis of
the insane, locomotor ataxia, brain soften-
ing, etc.; that most nervous troubles proceed
from it, directly or indirectly; that the
victim of it is a loathsome pariah for years
and, perhaps, a wreck for life. As to its
effects on the innocent, let the Doctor in
Brieux’s play, “Les Avaries,” speak :—-
Above all else, syphilis is a child-murderer. Every
year produces a fresh massacre of the innocents.
Herod still reigns in France and all the world over.
And though it is my duty to preserve life, I tell
you that those who perish are the luckiest. If you
want to see the children of syphilitic parents, go to
the children’s hospitals. We know the type: it has
become classical. Any doctor can pick them out
from the rest; little monsters, old from birth,
stamped with the marks of decay and every human
ill. You shall find children who are hump-backed,
twisted, club-footed, hare-lipped, rickety, with heads
too big, bodies too small and hips diseased.
The mention of these things may revolt the
msthetic, but there should be no protests
from the moralist, and there certainly will
be none from the mere pagan whose creed is
the happiness of mankind. A campaign of
education, honestly—-which is to say ruth-
lessly—carried out, is going to do a lot of
good, but it doesn’t go far enough. N. S.
Wales promises to give the other States the
further lead that is required in this matter.
The Ministry decided last week, provided it
remains in office, to instruct every subsi-
dised hospital in the State that, if it desires
to retain its subsidy, it must immediately
set aside a ward for the treatment of syphi-
lis cases, which ward shall be designated in
the ordinary way (“A” or “B” Ward, or
what not), instead of “Lock” or “Magdalen”
Ward, as hitherto. This means that Sydney
Hospital (say), which for many years has
shied at these cases, will in future be forced to
treat them, and that it will do it, moreover,
without heaping unnecessary shame on the
sufferers. When in every State in the Com-
monwealth there is an active movement in
the direction of teaching the ignorant what
this horror really is, and with it a serious
effort on the part of the authorities to
stamp it out, Australia will be some distance
on the road to that splendid consummation
aspired after by The Bulletin six
months ago—namely, the reputation of being
not only White, but Clean.

Water and Waste—with a Word About
a Tiger.
From G. IT. Blakemore, a good Australian,
who spent many years out West (N.S.W.)
as general manager of Great Cobar :
In a busy three months I have been able to see
something of 12 of the States of the U.S.A. ; and the
great fact which has burnt, itself into my mind is
that the prosperity of 10 of the 12 is due almost
wholly to irrigation. I have been in Washington,
Oregon, California, Nevada, Utah, Arizona, New
Mexico, Texas, Louisiana, Colorado, Idaho, and Wyo-
ming. In all of these there is some irrigation. In
Louisiana it is used to give an increased prosperity
over and above what the rich soil and fairly-abundant
rainfall would give without assistance; but in the
whole of lower California, a great deal of Nevada,
all of Utah, Colorado, Arizona and New Mexico, and
a considerable portion of Texas, the prosperity is
absolutely and entirely due to irrigation. Of late
years the Federal Government has been taking a
hand, and lately has put in a huge dam across the
Colorado in Arizona, and has built another one to
collect the snow-waters of the Truckee, to irrigate
large areas of Nevada ; and several immense ditches
have been dug and mountains pierced with five- and
six-mile tunnels to Colorado, to bring water to the
rich but arid district about Montrose on the D. and
R. S. railway.
The last six or seven States mentioned were
deserts until irrigation was tried; and the success
reached by the irrigators in what is apparently
clean sand is simply marvellous. There is a 4000-
acre vineyard about 20 miles from Los Angeles,
whose soil is sand as dean as that of the dunes at
Bondi. But it is irrigated, and the wonder is ex-
plained. The owners of land like this sand vineyard
will ask you SIOOO to SISOO an acre if water rights
go with it. In almost all the States there are magni-
ficent mountain ranges, with peaks thrusting their
snow-enpoed heads up into the blue, scores of them
over 10,000 ft. Even in the sun-smitten deserts of
Arizona there are rivers from these snow ranges,
and the water only has to be used. Such things
make an Australian a little sad; nevertheless, when
we get educated, and so arrange matters that none
of the fresh water in the few rivers that we do
possess ever gets to the sea except through the
clouds, we will know what land will produce. We
only think we know at present. Along the banks of
the river Darling there is soil from 200 ft. to 800 ft.
deep. If the water in the Darling which now flows
to waste were used for irrigating land only to
grow lucerne, I will swear, on my Australian know-
ledge, backed by my American observations, that
we could maintain more sheep on the banks of that
one river than the whole State now owns. Do you
wonder that from henceforward, in season and out of
season, I shall shout “Conservation of water with
irrigation” ?
I write this in Lower California, where, before
irrigation, a few miserable Mexicans made a scant
living. Now there is a succession of cities, and the
country which can be irrigated by gravity is one
huge garden. The folks in this part of America
have got over “trusting in Providence,” and with a
good irrigation ditch available, the farmer doesn’t
care if it rains one inch or 20 inches in a year—his
crop is sure. Hence there is not a poor farmer in
any of the irrigation colonies.
Of course it is of no use growing crops if there is
not a means of communication with the markets.
But in that respect Australia has an advantage over
these States, which are in the grip of railway cos.
that squeeze the last possible drop of blood out of
their customers. Such things as this the irrigators
have had to struggle against. Cabbages were scarce
in a citv, and prices went up S2O a ton; whereupon
those who were fortunate enough to have a cabbage
patch found the freight put up sls, so that the
railwavs scooped 75 per cent, of the increased price.
Australia doesn’t know what it escaped when it so
early took its railways out of the hands of private
cos., and it doesn’t know what a ravenous jaw it is
putting its head into if it lets the Trusts get be-
yond the nowers of the State.
Not only have my few months here absolutely
convinced me of the great benefit of State owner-
ship of railways (with a policy of building railways
of a development character), but I have seen so
much of “State rights” that my views on the sub-
ject have received a very severe shaking. In fact,
to myself I almost have got so far as to admit the
far-reaching wisdom of Federal control. Here State
rights have led to a condition of absolute disor-
ganisation. Towns now arrogate to themselves the
rights of States, and the spectacle of the police
officials of one town refusing to act on the warrant
of the police of another ihas become so common
that it scarcely excites a remark.
Maoriland Fills ’Em Up Again.
With the approach of-the liquor noli in Novem-
ber, the Maorilard Cold-water Dervishes are
tuning up the old, cracked No-License hurdy-
gurdy for something 'extra in the way of a
fling. The great hope this time is that
every pub in louland will be nut out of ex-
istence per medium of the “Dominion op-
tion” issue which will he presented for the
consideration of the free and enlightened for
the first time; and judging from the results
that have followed in the wake of the wiping
out of the beer palaces in various districts
since 1895, Messrs. Barrel and Bottle
may reasonably be suspected of subsidising
the movement. Since that year, when only
one no-license district was on the list, and
the cost of liquor per head amounted to
£3 Os. 5d., the inhabitants have been rolling
the old water chariot along amidst the- loud
“hie, hie, hurrah” of the procession, until in
1909, the year of the last poll, 12 districts
had turned good, comfortable hotels into
second-quality hash-factories or imported rag
emporiums, and the thirst barometer had
jumped to £3lls. Old. per head of population.
As usual, the whirling Dervishes charge the
increase against the “wave of prosperity”
which has swept the Ricketty Islands, leaving
behind it those prolific deposits from which
luxuries spring. The argument might be
right if the facts were right; but the truth
is that in the past two or three years there
has been a bad slump, bringing Civil Service
retrenchments, slackness of work, and a gen-
eral fear that the mortgage may ring the
bell any morning. Between 1901 and 1910
the national debt had swollen from £62 16s.
lOd. to £72 13s. 9d. per head of white popu-
lation, and the cost of living had also put on
weight. But if the Maorilander tightened
his belt at all it wasn’t when there was
liquor in the vicinity. On those occasions,
which are all day and every night, he merely
said: “Sufiicient unto the I O U is the day
of reckoning, JLetsaveadrink.” So he leaned
over the bar more than ever, and took home
more demijohns than ever, and became
drunker than ever, and got run-in more than
ever. In 1901, 8032 of him was convicted
for overloading himself with alcohol, and in
1910 no less than 11,718 of him had been
fined or sent to knuckle oakum. It is a
cheerful advertisement for No-License, and
proves that if the Maorilander gives all his
attention to “striking out the top line,” the
Plimsoll line will look after itself.”

Another Call for a Public Defender.
A few days ago a young man named Brit-
ton was released from a Victorian prison, on
the ground that “police inquiries had estab-
lished his innocence of the crime.” He
had been sentenced to two years’ gaol for an
offence against a girl under the" age of 16;
and he had served three months of the term.
The history of the case is almost identical
with that of many others recorded during
the past few years : —-
The conviction was due chiefly to the sworn evi-
dence of the girl. Detective Sullivan, who was
afterwards sent to investigate the affair [owing to a
local petition], found that the young man was 11
miles away at the time of the alleged offence, and
could not possibly have committed it. The girl her-
self then admitted that her evidence was a tissue of
perjuries. She had also sworn that she had never
misconducted herself before; and this also, she now
admitted, was untrue.
Upon receiving the detective’s report, Attor-
ney-General Brown recommended a free par-
don, and the victim of the girl’s perjuries
and the law’s blunder was released. And,
apparently, the brilliant Attorney-General
proposes to leave it at that. Nothing in the
way of compensation is to be offered the vic-
tim ; and nothing is being done to the girl.
Brown was questioned upon both of these
points, and his replies were:—
(1) It is not usual to compensate in such cases.
(2) Nothing has been decided. There seems a
good deal of difficulty in taking action upon the
uncorroborated statement of a girl who. .. .had ad-
mitted perjury in the hearing of the case.
If the Victorian Parliament allows the mat-
ter to rest there, then it deserves to have an
Attorney-General like Brown. The question
of compensation is a minor one; but the
matter of protecting other innocent Brittons
from gaol is just about as large a one as any
Parliament could possibly handle. In the
first place, the incident shrieks for a public
defender —an official who shall see that the
State gives just as much help to an accused
Britton to prove his innocence as to a per-
juring woman to prove Ins guilt. Without
that official the State is liable to join in a
most wicked partnership with a wanton per-
son to rob a citizen, not only of his liberty,
but of his good name. Out of the appoint-
ment of a public defender would probably
spring the prosecution of some of these dan-
gerous perjurers. To-day the State is in
alliance with the perjurer; she is its witness.
But if there were a public defender, the
perjurer who tries to get a citizen convicted
would find a State official directly interested
in securing punishment for even a prosecut-
ing witness’s perjury.
A word from the Kid himself:—
Thirteen and a-half years ago, as a “State kid,” I
was, at the age of 12|, sent to a cow-worshipper on
the South Coast of N.S.W., and I was at once ini-
tiated into the mysteries of dragging fluid from the
unwilling Mrs. Bull. My daily round was as fol-
lows. My kindly employer’s yells would wake me at
3.30 a.m.—“Get up, you lazy young ; it’s
nearly 4 o’clock.” Tumbling out of bed, and bare-
footed, with only a shirt, trousers and hat, winter
or summer, I went to the night paddock, and hunted
up as many as 180 cows, while the boss and the men
had a cup of tea. Then I helped to milk (and after
a very short time was expected to turn out cow for
cow with the boss). Having done my lot, I hunted
in the horses, harnessed up one, and took the milk
to the station, four miles away. Usually there were
some errands to be done in town, so it was mostly
10.30 before I got back. My first job then was to
wash the cans and feed the horse and sometimes the
fowls; after that I was free to go and have my first
taste of food —and many a time I have been “roused!
on” for eating too much. Then I would help plough,
harvest, plant spuds, fence, or do any of the neces-
sary work of a farm until 2.30 (dinner was unneces-
sary to me, I was told, having had so late a break-
fast). Then it was bring in the cows again, milk
10 or 12, and turn the separator; wash the cans and
separator; feed the pigs, calves and fowls; chop
wood ; then pump up water to cool next day’s milk
—a shuddersome job that, one hour’s solid pumping.
By this time—often it was 9 o’clock—-I was that
tired it was a hard job to eat my evening meal. I’ve
been that weary next morning that the warmth of
the cow would send me to sleep—when, “swish!” a
greenhide leg-rope would come down on my poorlv-
clad back. The whole family was privileged to belt
me. I’ve had as many as half a dozen hi Mugs with
leg-rope, belt or stockwhip in one day, and my tired
brain was driven into a whirl trying to And the
reason. I carry a mark on my thigh to-day made by
an iron ring attached to a leg-rope. This is not
“flabby sentiment”—it is hard, cold experience. I
had five years like that—and though I didn’t know
then, I know now that it was all because I had lost
my parents before I was old enough to know them.
If what I suffered wasn’t child-slavery then I’ll ask
someone to define it. I was that worthless at 171
that when my time expired my employer offered me
7s. fid. a week and keep to stay on, and at the same
time I was handed a bank-book with £l3, my ac-
cumulated earnings. Truly an encouragement to be
a rural worker! I’m not. I came to Sydney and
learnt a trade, and to-day I’m working at it in my
own shop.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Mid sweat and tears, for years and years,
A lielot-people stooped,
Agasp for breath, at grips with death,
In reeking hovels cooped.
God’s sun at noon, God’s glorious moon,
Spread light for them in vain ;
For day and night, or dark and bright,
Brought no surcease of pain.
The stars might rise in darkling skies —
No stars arose for them.
O’er seas and trees the singing breeze
Sang but their requiem.
A famished horde, whose overlord
Grim Toil had crushed to earth,
What place had they in revel gay,
What part in human mirth?
In field and town, bowed down, bowed down
And chained, and gyved, and grooved,
They toiled, and moiled, and blackly soiled
God’s good earth where they moved.
Their cheeks were white, their day was
night,
Their night a drear eclipse,
Wherein they wept, or, shivering, slept
With pallid, muttering lips.
A wolfish few, a gilded crew,
A ravening, savage brood,
Ate of their flesh each day afresh,
And drank of their pale blood.
When drear distress, dread hopelessness,
Wife, child, man’s right to bread,
Gave manhood’s strength to them at length
Their country gave them lead.
Behold! Behold the brave, the bold,
The glorious sight! Behold
The picture blest! A toiler’s breast
Red-rent —hands clenched and cold.
N.S.W.

one of the land-hungry horde, who votes
anti-Labor witn wearisome monotony, re-

signed in order to become a five years’ lease-

little things, the screed mentioned : —-
All that piece or parcel of land containing 80
acres . . . yielding and paying therefor £6O per
annum. Now these presents witness etc. etc. that
during the first two years the lessee shall clear and
grass not less than twenty-five acres of the scrub
land adjoining owned by the said lessor and that on
completion of the improvements aforesaid he (the
said lessee) shall pay to the said lessor the ad-
ditional rent of fifteen shillings per acre for the
whole of the land mentioned in this agreement.
Tenant right in improvements, I don’t
think! Tenant penalty for improvements,
I do think! And rack-rent into the bar-
gain, I do swear! .Nevertheless this intel-
ligent voter can be backed to bellow “I want
my freehold!” at the first election hereafter,
and to vigorously denounce the Labor “con-
fiscators” who want to give him the land for
the first five years free, and to reappraise it
in 25 years, carefully excluding his improve-
ments from the calculation.
If to kick, bite and scratch —to interject,
charge, accuse and rave —will put men into
N.S.W. Ministries, then Fitzparrot, Wood,
Levy, Cohen, Onslow and Wade ought to be
absolute certainties for portfolios—if the
“Liberals” ever come to power again.
The expense of collecting the Common-
wealth land tax was £17,123, including
salaries. Wade’s advisory board in con-
nection with his* wonderful scheme of land
settlement cost £28,913 to handle only 14
estates or portions of estates.
Before leaving Melbourne Jack Longstaff
laid the foundations of a fund to put a stone
over Phil May’s grave in Kensal Green,
where now only a wooden sign intimates that
the great pen-artist is below. Admirers of
May recall that they have already been
waited upon with the hat; but that money
went in setting up a brass tablet in Phil’s
native town. Bertram Mackennal is eager
to carve a stone, to take the place of the
wooden slab ; and Australia may be relied
upon to do its little bit towards the cost.
The Argus is dreadfully worried over
Senator McGregor’s decision to favor con-
tracting firms tiiat employ union labor. It
sees corruption ahead—“plain, sordid, money
graft.” But while the Argus pretends to
see that a Labor Government may be cor-
rupted by bribes from union firms, the notion
never enters its silly old head that an anti-
Labor Government may be just as conceiv-
ably bribed by an anti-union firm. And if
the latter idea is preposterous and unfair and
all the rest of it, then the idea of a Labor
Government’s corruption is also preposterous
and unfair, and mere silly prejudice on the
Argus’s part.
The Australasian, stirred up by the
sugar-workers’ strike, asks, with tears bub-
bling inside its Fat neck, what is to be the
end of it when unionists deny to non-unioi -
ists “the common rights of citizenship—the
inalienable right in a free land to work and
to live by the labor of their hands.” Union-
ists don’t deny any such thing. They only
refuse to allow themselves to work with non-
unionists —just as the Argus refuses to allow'
any other paper to join in its cable service,
unless that other paper agrees to the rules of
the ARGUS-bossed cable union. The Argus
doesn’t call that “tyranny.” It says it is
merely “business.” And the laborer has just
as much right to apply business rules to the
sale of his labor as the Argus has to the sale
of its penny dreadful or its weekly mournful.
A voice from above: —
Seen for the first time last week, N.S.W. Assembly,
with its heavily upholstered seats, looked to me like
an immense railway carriage which was whirling
through miles of sordid back yards towards Bosh
and Blither. The members strewn round had the
weary air of men on a tiresome railway journey;
and little Fitzparrot was the pest the train-traveller
meets on his unlucky days. Sitting in high glee on
the front Opposition bench, with one foot tucked
under him, in the attitude of a mischievous small
boy, he kept yapping his tiresome interjections, or
acting as honorary echo to the least decrepit lions
of the herd. In the ugly, snarling rush at the Chair
he was side by side with the furious Cohen; but in
his most excited moments there seemed to be
a trace of uncertainty in his manner. He reminded
me of the new super who hopes one day to lead the
rabble, and keeps glancing towards the concealed
stage-manager in the wings to make sure he is
doing all right.
A humane measure, providing that chil-
dren born out of wedlock should be legiti-
matised on the marriage of their parents, last
week shocked the Vic. House of Fatuous Land-
lords till it could only gasp. Members hurled
Scripture at it—the same Scripture, by the
way, which says that it is easier for a camel
to go through the needle’s eye than for a Fat-
man to enter Heaven—and asked in horror
what the Churches would say about it. This
paper doesn’t know what they would sav;
but if they declared that an innocent child
should go on bearing the stigma of bastardy
when the marriage of the parents provided
a good reason for legally removing it, then
the Churches’ opinion is entitled to even less
respect than that of the House of Fat Land-
lords.
