“The Witness for the Defence” is an in-
teresting story, artistically rendered into
dramatic form. There are no strainings
after stage effects; no sensational “cur-
tains.” There is a dipsomaniac, who re-
frains from tearing to tatters the passion in-
duced by an impending attack of delirium
tremens, and a young woman, in whose
case—
Sorrow, like a heavy banging bell,
Once set on ringing, with his own weight goes
and who yet does not revel in grief to an
unreasonable extent. Everywhere the author
betrays knowledge of life and of the society
he depicts, and an independence of the cruder
stage conventions. Necessarily, in a play
which begins with a murder by the heroine,
and must end happily if it is to be a popular
success, there are features which do not
convince. It is impossible, for example, to
believe in Thresk. Ho is a barrister, so
obsessed by ambition mat, when his career
was beginning, he jilted Stella Ballantyne, in
order to devote himself wholly to work.
Years afterwards he dines with her and her
worthless husband in India. She shoots the
husband that night, and Thresk’s false evi-
dence saves her at the trial. He goes out
of her life again; two years pass ; she falls
in love with Richard Hazlewood. The young
man’s people have their doubts concerning
her innocence, and they ask Thresk to stay
with them in the country. And at this late
hour, the man who, two years previously,
did not hesitate to turn a murderess loose
on society, and to perjure himself in the
process, suddenly develops scruples. He
“ THE WITNESS FOR THE DEFENCE,"
AT THE SYDNEY CRT.
When Brief meets Brief, etc .
The family solicitor (Town-
send Whitling) cross-questions
Barrister Thresk, who lies
convincingly to him, and
Father Hazlewood (Plumpton
Wilson).
insists that Stella shall tell Richard of her
guilt. Stella, not unnaturally, objects.
Thresk says_ that he must, then, do it him-
self. She hints at the prejudicial effects of
a conviction for perjury on his career at the
Bar. He is amazingly reckless of consequences.
Simultaneously this man who, in his hot
youth, was cold and hard enough to abandon
Stella, is moved to ask her to throw over her
lover, and face the world with him. To para-
phrase Ibsen, successful barristers don’t do
such things.
* * * *
One may indicate Miss Ethel Irving’s
genius by negatives. In her moments of
pathos she is heart-breaking; yet violence,
rant, frenzy—the meretricious tricks of the
immemorial “star”—are foreign to her tem-
perament. She could not be exaggerated
or artificial or insincere if she tried. And
her personality and voice are the last words
in charm. Her methods being what they
are, naturalism and restraint are essential
in the rest of the cast. The soldier lover
(Warburton Gamble) is not a stagey youth
who has palpably burst into the Army List
under false pretences—in short, a flagrant
“leading juvenile”—but just such a cheery,
well-meaning, slightly fatuous young man as
the junior branch of the British Service turns
out in herds every year. Advocates like
Henry Thresk—not quite so suave and hand-
some as Stephen Ewart, a shade less dapper,
and lacking his Olympian calm in situations
that would hideously embarrass normal men
of the world, yet still, broadly speaking,
like him—pervade the Inns of Court during
term-time. Townsend Whitling presents a
likely solicitor, and Gilbert Porteous a per-
fectly-credible butler. J. P. Wilson is good
as the eccentric father, and Halliwell
Hobbes’s Ballantyne is excellent. It is, in
brief, a production in which all are praise-
worthy, and one is beyond praise.
On Saturday night the Melba Grand Opera
season opens at Her Majesty’s, Sydney,
with “La Traviata.” Melba will, of course,
sustain the role of the anguished Violetta,
and John McCormack, the Irish tenor, will
be her Alfredo. On Monday and Friday
evenings, and at a Wednesday matinee, the
management will stage “Faust” (Marguerite,
Miss Axarine; Siebel, Australia's own
Eileen Castles; and Faust, Mr. Quesnel) ;
on Wednesday evening “Traviata” again;
and on Tuesday and Thursday evenings
“Samsone and Dalila.” Delilah will be
played by Mrs. Sisneros at the first per-
formance, and by Miss Voluntas Ranzenberg
at the second.

Mrs. X is very indignant with “Miss
Gibbs” because the shop-girl is only staying
at Her Majesty’s for one week, and she can’t
anyhow catch up the 15 performances by
which Mrs Y beat her last time the “record”-
maker was in Sydney. The fact is, “Miss
Gibbs” is only packing to fill in the gap
between Irving and Melba. Blanche Browne
still has the title part, with Bertie Wright
as her cousin from Yorkshire.

“The Merry Widow” removes her weeds
from Sydney Theatre Royal on Friday night
next, and yields up the premises to “The
Waltz Dream.” And both are now so well
known that no more need be said about
either.
# * *
Things move so fast in “Nick Carter,
Detective,” the new Adelphi yellodrama,
that George Marlow can’t keep up with
them in less than 16 scenes. Carter is a
detective —famous, of course; all detectives
are —who has a habit of getting into tight
places, and then, when he has only three
seconds more to live, of getting out again.
He starts out to capture a gang of thieves
who have kidnapped his son, and meets with
all the excitement in the frantic rush to
reunite the family. He gets into the thieves’
den in disguise, only to be identified by a
scratch on his arm, received in an encounter
with one of the gang. He is sentenced to
die the most glorious death that the gang-
can find, but is rescued at the last moment
by a henpecked husband who knows that
Nicholas is a married man himself. The fall
through the skylight, the escape on horse-
back, and, finally, the train robbery, all
supply the thrills which the souls of the
great gods and goddesses of the Adelphi
yearn after.
Nick Carter (Cecil Mannering), the chief
of the gang (C. R. Stanford), and Timmins
(J. R. Howard) are the only male people
worth mentioning; and Ida Gresham, as
Stephania(of the gang), Ada Guildford (Mrs.
Carter —“wronged,” of course) and Lena
Langridge (Mrs. Timmins) do what is asked
of them on the other side. The staging, and
particularly the railway scene, is all right,
nc- * *
“The Old Woman Who Lives in a Shoe”
is playing matinees only at the Palace
Theatre (Sydney) these times; and when
she arrives with her family and goodness
knows how many tons of luggage on Wednes-
days and Saturdays, the language of “The
Man from Outback,” who has to remove his
traps and saddles and shearing-slieds and
things, would satisfy the most fastidious
that the Australian bushman could hold his
own against even Dill Macky’s yellow-pup
on the 12th of July.
** * *
Last Saturday the gods of Sydney Tivoli
respectfully watched Miss Louie Tracy, the
“Dapper, Dandy Boy,” dressed as a soldier,
smoke a large cigar with evident relish; and,
subsequently, they gave her their undivided
attention while she puffed at a short cutty
and walked about in the roomy garments of
the Royal Navy. Impersonator Lamberti,
'Clarke and Mostol (comedians and dancers),
and Alf Holt (mimic) supply three good
reasons why the Tivoli patron should ap-
proach the box office with four pieces of sil-
ver in his hand. Other reasons are supplied by
Sydney Bach (entertainer), Marguerite
Ismay (dancer), Fred Bluett (comedian),
and ventriloquist Carlton Max.
** * *
To-night (Thursday) and to-morrow night
are the last chances the incurable postponer
will have of hearing Joseph Blascheck and
Mildred Wrighton, now closing their season
at Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall. This city says
farewell to the bright entertainers with a
catch in its voice and a doorknob in its
throat.
** * *
Australia’s versatile old friend Inclement
Wragge, who has been in Maoriland for
some considerable time past lecturing and
exploring, will commence a series of lectures
at the Y.M.C.A. Hall next Monday evening.
The title of the first lecture is "A Voyage
through the Universe,” and the Inclement
ought to be a thoroughly qualified pilot for
the journey.

The National Amphitheatre continues to
hold its end up in the presence of teeming
audiences, partly owing to the strivings of
Jules Garrison and his alleged Roman
Maids, partly as a result of the labors of
Jordan and Harvey, alleged Sons of Israel,
and partly by virtue of the strenuous efforts
of a number of alleged comedians. Eva
Mudge is still there; likewise the Racoons
and Capt. Winter’s dogs.

The Lyric, in the early part of this week,
made a worthy feature of an imported
film, “His Mother,” a sentimental drama,
new to Australia; and “also rans” worth
noting were “The Feud,” a French melo-
drama. and “Pathe’s European Gazette.”
For the mid-week change, “A Man of
Honor,” “Making Bath Bricks,” and
“Madrid and the Spanish Army” are speci-
ally mentioned. “Scenes around the Coalt
of Maoriland” was a most attractive series
at the Colonial 1 Pictures. The Colonial ”
showed “Pathe’s Australian Gazette” and
some very interesting “Scenes around Bar-
celona” ; and for the new programme “Agri-
culture by Motor” and the “Story of James
V. of Scotland” are the attractions. At the

“The Chocolate Soldier,” to hand at Mel-
bourne Royal, comes up to expectation at all
points. The music, melodious and full-
bodied, runs on grand-opera lines; the spec-
tacle is rich, the story compact, the acting
almost as good as the singing—and in that
respect expectation is exceeded. Of course,
there is nothing of broad, unrestrained
low comedy in “The Chocolate Soldier” —
no privileged funny man who “gags” as he
pleases and breaks the continuity of the
piece by introducing an irrelevant turn with
the soubrette. It is a fairly close parody
on Shaw’s “Arms and the Man,” plus chorus
and scenic trimmings. In a synopsis of the
plot a London writer said: “Only in the
first act is the musical version at all like
the play. From then onwards the libret-
tists present an imbroglio that is mainly of
their own conception.” Asa matter of fact,
the plot of Shaw’s comedy is outlined
throughout the opera. True, the sordid
manservant is omitted, the ambitious maid-
servant is converted into a flippant relative
of the family, and the polite Bulgarian
officer who searches for the fugitive hero,
appears more than once as a grotesque Cap-
tain Massakroff; but the other people, under
changed names, are contrasted as in the
original, although their characteristics are
much varied.
The note of musical success is struck by
Miss O’Connor in an early solo with a
lovely waltz refrain, which recurs in the
2nd act, and again at the close of the
3rd. Miss O’Connor has never before
looked so pretty, sung so triumphantly or
shown such aptitude for archness of expres-
sion. The lady, who was rather hard, cold
and angular in “Tom Jones,” gets near to
coquettish vivacity where it is needed in
this case, and, strangely enough, she is
weakest when she affects the lofty air of
a mock idealist. Her duets with one and
the other man are very effective. Spiridoff,
the posing and imposing tenor (Noel Flem-
ing), fills the bill in appearance and affec-
tations of manner, and brings a sweet,
chesty voice into action, whilst.the cheerful
baritone Chocolate Soldier (Leslie Gaze) re-
presents frivolity in a hurry. His style is
amateurish, but vigorous and jovial. Re-
garded as a healthy, good-humored young
singer doing his best to make things lively,
Gaze is a success. His part “goes”—not in
the right way; but no matter. He at least
gets a move on it. If he isn’t quaintly hum-
orous, he is brisk, anyhow. Miss Rita
Presano makes her mark as a contralto
singer with a refined sense of comedy in the
THE CHOCOLATE SOLDIER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A cheerful chap who plays the goat,
In plain or military coat,
Is not the sort who much inspires
A maiden’s heart to high desires
Or dreams idealistic;
Yet he persuades her more than those
Who cultivate the picture pose
Of heroes that could never ue
In mortal form, on land or sea,
Where life is realistic.
And should a cneerful chap appear
When she has gone for quite a year
Without a Ci.ance to charm, or vex,
One member of the other sex,
Then, being just a woman,
She’s bound to find his vulgar laugh
More thrilling than a photograph.
For Love, in dreams, may often take
High fancy-flights; but Love, awake,
Is ever truly human.
VICTORIA.

** * *
H. B. Irving, who was welcomed to Mel-
bourne by a houseful of enthusiasts at the
renovated Exhibition-street Theatre on Sat-
urday, produces a Hamlet bent upon set-
tling disputes re the sanity of the depressed
Dane. Indeed, the actor emphasises so
strongly all the points implying that the or-
phan boy of Elsinore was merely aping mega-
lomania, that we are forced to arrive at one
of two conclusions: either Hamlet was an-
ticipating pertinacious controversies that
would wrack Shakspearian professors, ex-
perts and associations undreamed of in his
day ; or he was actually half convinced that
he had a brain kink, and was putting up a
big bluff. For the rest, this is a noisier,
more angular, angrier and more inarticulate
Hamlet than lias previously been associ-
ated with the Danish throne in these parts.
When Irving is most distinct, he seems to
own a unique brogue; when least distinct,
there is no articulation at all, but a hoarse
comnlaining or accusative rumble, like the
outcry of an impassioned animal. The Irv-
ing leg, the Irving elbow, and the Irving
hair—cherished family an
eerie distinction to the Irving Hamlet, a
performance which at one moment strikes a
note of profound emotion, and at the next
Angelini , the Maestro of Melba’s Opera Co.
reminds you of a hapless praying mantis,
just fished out of the inkbottle, preening its
funereal wings. It is one of the best and
worst Hamlets Melbourne has ever seen; a
source of pricking and persistent aggrava-
tion, it leaves the critic with a laurel
wreath in one hand and a brick in the other,
and so beset with various emotions that he
will never know which he should throw.
This means that H. B. Irving’s Hamlet is a
brain stimulant and an intellectual buck-up
of high value.
Dorothea Baird, as Ophelia, is a strange,
pre-Raphaelitish lady, a creature of freakish
attitudes and fitful feeling. She is at her
best in the mad scenes, where her marked
mannerisms help her to one or two uncanny
effects, and where she often touches the
right pitch of pathos. For the rest, this per-
formance of the premier Elizabethan tragedy
is as fiat as a wall, with a few decent gar-
goyles. The ghost, by Henry Vibart, is a
handsome, melodious and well-ordered spook
who commands respect. Arthur Whitby
strikes a fair level of senility for Polonius,
the old fool with a lucid interval; and Tom
Reynolds’s First Gravedigger is an unaggress-
ively humorous creation sprinkled with
hints of Asche’s Christopher Sly the tinker.

“Via Wireless” will present the vasty
deep in a turbulent state at the Princess’s
until to-night (Thursday), and then the
“Whip” co. will pass from Melbourne to
Adelaide and the West on its way to Eng-
land. Another co., with Miss Dorothy
Dix as a new leading lady, takes up the
Williamsonian running on Saturday, and
“The Speckled Band” will be the drama that
invites attention.
** * *
At the King’s, a short revival of “The
French Spy” is making a lurid appeal to the
unimportant. Walter Baker appears as
Captain Somers, who circumvents spies, and
explains the obvious in many conversations.
The fascinating Countess de Loriac is Miss
Frances Boss, who secures the right aristo-
cratic pallor with two or three coats or
poudre de riz. Charles Brown is an aerated
Irishman, and Arthur Styan figures as Gen-
eral Trepoff, the despondent Chief of the
Russian Police. Miss Vera Ilamee, as the
Countess Olga, is a Voice given to over-
emphasis ; while the best that can be said
for Richard Bellairs is that his Romanoff
Count possesses a good supply of animation.
** * *
At the Opera House nothing has yet ar-
rived to lessen the importance of the bright
but patchy turn of Clarke and Hamilton.
But several other people are at the end of
their tether, and will be set free to-night
(Thursday). So a considerable change of
bill is confidently expected.
** * *
The feature of “ The State Line," at
West’s, Melbourne, last Saturday, was its
big-hearted cowboy, who was a terror to the
wicked and a berruther to the weak. “The
Valley of the Nera, Spain,” wasn’t conspicu-
ously beautiful; but “A Dutch Gold Mine"
deserved a diploma for cute humor and novel
plot. Very good also was “A Romany Tra-
gedy,” in which two gipsies are slain with a
ceremony verging on pomposity.
■M * * *
“The Power of Beauty,” shown last week
at the P’ran Lyric (Vic.), had many items
of interest for the coquettish and the amor-
ous. The picture was full of secret kisses
and stolen hugs, while the chief lady-
character spent most of her time taking on
lovers as a kind of diet. Among other recent
Lyric films, “William Tell” was the most at-
tractive. The old story of the immaculate
archer was well portrayed by the Italian co.
which has figured in “Ai'da” and other pic-
tures of modern onerns.
* iti X *
In “The Speckled B iven its first
Australian trial at Adelaide Royal, the Wil-
liamson management has a winner. It is
tlmlling enough for melodrama, and clever
enough to please the critical; and thus it
rakes in everybody. Written up from a
single short Sherlock Holmes story, it has
had to be elaborated. Enid’s sister has died
mysteriously in the night, and an inquest,
held on the stage, produces nothing but sym-
pathy for the Anglo-Indian doctor, their
stepfather. Dr. Watson is ingeniously shown
as the friend of the family, and he talks
casually of Holmes. It is not till two years
later, when Enid has been compelled to take
the room her sister died in, and the same
death-heralding, creepy music has begun to
play in the night, that she flies to Holmes,
who drops in disguised, diagnoses the case
as a snake-and-ventilator trick, and beats
the “speckled band” back to where it came
from. There, being naturally annoyed, it
promptly bites Stepfather. The play is bet-
ter than the former S. Holmes drama, be-
cause it omits the love-interest which was
so unsuitable to the man. (It even dispenses
with love-making altogether. Enid gets en-
gaged to a man who does not appear, and
Watson to a girl of the same shadowy per-
suasion.) The sole cause for regret is that
the village shopkeeper, a pugnacious Metho-
dist, and Enid’s sole friend all through, has
not been allowed in at the death. He de-
served that reward for being the only in-
telligently suspicious person among a fat-
headed crowd. Cambourne plays him splen-
didly. Dorothy Dix, graceful and very
young, has little to do but look frightened,
but she does that charmingly, and rises to
the one chance of defiance that she gets.
Desmond, who does not appear till half-way
through, is a less “intense” S. Holmes than
Cuyler Hastings used to be. This is largely
the author’s doing, for Sherlock is made to
swagger humorously even in the tightest
places—which suggests that Conan Doyle
had a Yankee collaborator as dramatist.
Mervale, as the murder-planner, is the back-
bone of the play—with a make-up that should
get him hanged at sight. His is a fine study
of incipient homicidal mania, with twitching
fingers, rolling eyes, and dangerous mildness
all complete. Mackay is Dr. Watson, Athol-
wood a hypnotised butler, Leslie Victor and
Susie Vaughan have for once parts without
a smile, Holmes’s casual clients are beauti-
fully played, and the snake, real, large and
lively, causes many thrills, and completes a
great cast.
** * *
The King’s Theatre, Adelaide, has started
as a cheaper-priced rival to the Tivoli, with
Maude Mortimer and other new and old
friends in Variety. At the Tivoli itself,
Binns and Binns are delightful tricksters;
Harris and Vernon have just come in, and
Lazern is conjuring as of old. Desmond is
playing “The Silver King” at the Royal, to
Miss Irby Marshall’s Nellie Denver, Athol-
wood’s Jaikes, and Mackay’s “Spider.” The
picture shows are mostly specialising in the
Cardinal’s funeral, though the Empire has
a fine study of a lady and some wild beasts,
“Back to the Primitive.” And that remark-
able local singer, Miss Violet Paine, has
given an evening of operatic selections, in
costume. She ought to be on the opera
stage.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Sundry things that would have happened if the
two firms had continued their strife are not going
to happen now that the two are made one. The
birth of the Combine, it is said, gave the quietus to
the panto, which C. and M. were preparing for Mel-
bourne Royal’s next Christmas bill. That, however,
is not a matter for lamentation ; one panto.—at Her
Majesty’s—should suffice to feed the city which still
clings to the tradition of a Christmas annual; and
Sydney these times seems content to get its nymphs
and gnomes as an Easter offering. But the Combine
will probably turn down other expensive projects
that one or other of the firms had in view. It will
certainly in future offer foreign managers less
temptation to make an Australian tour on terms.
For instance, the terms that Oscar Asche got from
C. and M. had been previously refused by J.C.W.
Thus it was competition which gave us Asche’s
excellent productions. The same competition is re-
sponsible for some notable theatrical events just
now, and will account for a few others yet to come;
but, beyond that, the Cpmbine is thinking of new
business from a monopolist’s point of view. When
it comes to mere girl-show attractions, Australia
will be almost certain to think of the Yankee Meat
Trust.
“L.H.P.”: The Wowsers won’t like “Samson and
Delilah.” For a long time it was barred by the
English censor, and its production in 1909 at Covent
Garden was, so ’tis said, largely through Queen
Alexandra’s personal influence. Originally done at
Weimar in 1877, it found its way to the Paris
Grand Opera, and Paris had so little fault to find
with it that it has been staged over 300 times. Those
who hear it at Her Majesty’s will find the Scriptural
yarn modified by the librettist (Ferdinand Lemaire),
so that Delilah betrays Samson from the highest
patriotic motives, sacrificing her lover in the in-
terests of the Philistine State.
The Australian “dear girls’ ” late idol, Tittel
Brune, was at latest in Paris, having her song-ap-
paratus overhauled with a view to a possible grand-
opera dSbut. If all goes well, the little woman will
probably make her first bid for operatic fame in
Belasco’s “Girl of the Golden West,” the dramatic
version of which .she played hereabouts.
William Murdoch, who has been engaged as accom-
panist to Clara Butt on her forthcoming S’African
tour, originated in Bendigo, and used the Austral
Competitions as the first outlet for his talent. Next
he gained a scholarship at the Melbourne University
Conservatorium of Music, and then the Clarke
scholarship, which took him to the London Academy
of Music, where he gained fresh glories. The most
stringent t’otherside critics have been saying very
sweet things about him lately.
James Watts, a youth who promised exceptionally
well as dancer and tenor soloist when he was trying
to push his way along with J. C. Williamson’s
forces, left a while back to see if he couldn’t push
faster on the other side. Evidently he has caught
somebody’s eye, for word came by last mail that he
had been engaged to play Dame with Harry Lauder
in a forthcoming panto, production.
Like H. B. Irving, Ewart, leading man of the
Ethel Irving Co., is an English barrister.
Ballarat has revived its old desire for a Shak-
speare statue. In the boom days of 1888, or there-
abouts, some five or six hundred pounds were raised
for a local tribute to the bard’s memory ; for Balla-
rat prided itself on the culture it had received from'
Bill Holloway and Co. But the money was deposited
in the Mercantile Bank, and in the wreck of that in-
stitution a lot of the statue fund found a grave.
The rescued balance, plus accumulated interest,
amounts to £ 360; so the trustees propose to form
a Shakspeare Society, and send round the hat once
again. The baldheaded bard, in his low-cut collar
and saucer-track knickers, is a deserving subject for
a work of Australian art; but if it is to be Aus-
tralian beyond a doubt, experience shows that sub-
scribers must make that stipulation before they
drop their copper in the hat.
Ted Kalman, the music-hall comedian, blew
himself out of the world at a Melbourne hotel on
Saturday afternoon. He had recently moved on from
Sydney Tivoli to the Opera House; and nobody
seems to know what the trouble was. Down to seven
years ago Ted Kalman was Edwin Symons, seed-
merchant, of Perth.
Juan Bonaparte, who claims to be a genuine
descendant of the great Napoleon, and is admittedly
strikingly like him, has made a success in England
in a “sketch” specially written for him to go Nap
in. Now that he has proved that he can act, he is
to be given a chance in a revival of “A Royal
Divorce.”
Bert Bailey and Jules Grant (William Anderson’s
well-known general manager) have formed them-
selves into “Bailey Ltd.,” and, with a string of
robust melodramas and a suitable eo., will set out
upon an Australasian tour in November.
Randolph Bedford puts his old friend Jim Wilkin-
son into his new drama, “The Lady of the Pluck-
up.” The Pluck-up was a Purnamoota mine, and
“Big Jim” Wilkinson was in those days the driver
of the coach from Terowie to Silverton; that was in
the early ’Bo’s, just before Broken Hill was found.
Then he joined in the hurly-burly of the Barrier
silver-boom, and was one of the big men—big in
body and heart—of the great field. Afterwards he
was pearler and prospector; and two years ago,
after they had buried him under the button-grass
at the new Balfour rush in Tasmania, Bedford gave
his old adventurous friend a send-off in some fine
Bulletin verses. Now he appears in a play as
Buck Crawford, and if the character is as good as
the man, Bedford’s drama must be a success in
spots, anyhow.
“S.S.”: Clunes (Vic.) reports the curious case of
a girl of 10, who is thrown into convulsions by the
hissing of a goose. Possibly one or both parents
were actors. It certainly looks like hereditary
antipathy.
“Harrison O”: In his remarks (B. 24/8/'ll) re
slavish imitation of mummery, “0.K.” puts the ease
well from the star mime’s point of view; but what
about the general public? I, of course, admit that
when mimicry enters by the door art escapes by the
window ; but since Australian theatre-goers have the
most slender chance of observing the “creators” of
modern parts, is it not better that we should be
provided with an almost exact imitation of the
original rather than a new and, probably, indif-
ferent rendering by somebody who has not had the
chance (as most of the Fogtown “creators” have>
of hearing the author’s views as to how a part
should be played? If we cannot acquire an original,
by Anton Mauve or Corot, surely it is better that
we get a good copy rather than a brand new thing
by Nathaniel Dauber.
Many people will remember Agnes Thomas, who
was brought to Australia by old Dion Boucicault,
and subsequently acted for the J.C.W. firm. She js
now figuring in a dramatisation of Thurston’s
“Sally Bishop,” at the Prince of Wales, London,
and is looking so young that no one would charge
her with possessing a grown-up daughter. All the
same, she owns a chick who is a good deal over
seven, and is at present mumming with the Ethel
Irving Co. Eily Malyon is the lady’s name, and she
enacts those ingenue characters that just escape
being interesting.
ART AND VEGETABLES.
No man who has the interests of Art in
his composition can, after a visit to the
N. S. Wales Royal Art Society’s 1911 crop
of hardy annuals, fail to be impressed by
the fact that the recently announced short-
age of Chinese market-gardeners isn’t so in-
capable of solution as the Sydney Morning
Omnibus would have us believe. It is true,
of course, that we must have spuds; also,
some people consider onions to be the spice
of life; while cabbage is often affected by
even the intellectual. Now, all this paves
the way to do something for both Art and
vegetables, seeing that they are mutually
dependent upon each other. All such ques-
tions as Art, Kings, Betting Books, Amours,
Grand Opera, Churches, Billiard Saloons,
and Silk Stockings depend entirely upon the
public’s meals, and the discovery that there
wasn’t any horse-radish to be obtained for
love or money might entirely alter the inten-
tions of the noble patron who, at this very
moment, may be about to step forward and
give strength to the flower of Art struggling
for life in the community’s soul. Capital
might be driven out of the country in search
of turnips for its soup, and then who is
going to buy pictures? There is only one
thing to do, and it must be done at once.
In the cause of Art, those of its votaries
who can best be spared, must volunteer for
the turnip trench, or be forcibly led there.
Out of about a couple of hundred who have
done their best for the Royal Art show in
Sydney this year, The Bulletin reckons
a full half can be handed a pitchfork and a
hoe immediately. Next year another hun-
dred could get work, and so on. Thus the
passing of the Chinaman may be viewed
with satisfaction: cauliflower will be in its
usual place; and Art will be safe.
Taking this year’s show as a whole, one
cannot say that anything has been done to
raise the R. A. Society’s standard of the
past few years. The blue pencil of the
hanging committee has been a little busier
than usual, but still not busy enough; and
if the allegation is that the general level has
been bettered, a microscope will have to be
brought in to prove it. The outstanding ex-
hibits are a Longstaff portrait of Mrs. R.
Broomfield, the work of Hans Heysen, and
a batch of water-colors by B. E. Minns.
The Longstaff canvas shows the old know-
ledge of the craft, but it is not amongst the
artist’s best liners. Heysen collects all the
limelight with a big oil bushscape and a
panel of charming little water-colors. Most
attention will be given his “Hauling Tim-
ber” (oil), which has been purchased by the
trustees of the National Gallery. Unques-
tionably it is a fine study of the bush atmo-
sphere, and 200 guineas wasn’t too much
recompense in these times of piping pros-
perity. The only other canvas purchased
by the trustees was a black-and-white head,
characteristically gnarled and furrowed, by
Dattilo-Rubbo. It was the pick of a section
particularly weak. James A. Crisp s one
black-and-white effort was not up to " The
Apple Cart,” which he drove _ into the
National Gallery last year. Minns, while
not at liis best, helps the show along with a
string of gay water-colors; but neither Nor-
man Carter nor J. S. Watkins has anything
so good as at the last show. Watkins s
“Red Kimono” doesn’t get where he evi-
dently wants to go with a large canvas and
a sizeable assortment of warmed-up colors;
and Carter could have a hatful of ‘ Chintz
Sofas” for his last year’s girl in mauve. As
for Watkins’s portrait of Acting-Premier
Holman, one of its virtues is that it can be
labelled with somebody else’s name if Hol-
man declines to accept the responsibility.
E. W. Christmas gets the eye with a good
conception of Maoriland’s great, sombre
Alps; and Portia Geach, with a carefully-
handled Kaffir girl. Lister Lister serves up
the customary large slices of bush country
and copious quantities of Pacific, without
which no R.A. menu would be complete.
His main canvas, depicting pioneering work
on a rough selection, is good Lister. Dat-
tilo-Rubbo’s big oil, representing seven man-
ual grafters stopped at Circular Quay on
their way to toil, is too palpably an arrange-
ment of the studio. It would have been
better to have let tnose m.g.’s get to their
respective jobs. Evelyn Chapman’s portraits
are well painted, but it is difficult to dis-
cover the reason for taking up so much
good space with Jas. R. Jackson’s “Halcyon
Days,” unless the hanging committee has a
grudge against him. This year, again, Miss
Margaret R. MacPherson can have a place
among the honorably-mentioned for her still-
life representations. Arthur W. Chappie’s
“End of a Spring Day.” a couple of Gus-
tave Barnes’s water-colors. Jas. A. Crisp's
magpie brand of poultry, Chas. H. Barrie’s
poster-design pastel, and some of B. O.
Jones’s oils, are hereby discharged with a
certificate of character. The rest are re-
manded. But. undoubtedly, the necessity of
running a vegetable farm must not be over-
looked. The society has quite a number of
neople who could be doing something useful,
like rearing spuds, and spuds have got to
be reared somehow.
The only question is: Who is to be
turned into the turnip field? The Bulletin,
to avoid a scene, suggests that lots be
drawn.
A CHANCE FOR ART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Victoria is smitten with a vegetable famine. The
scarcity follows on a phenomenally wet season. —News
item.
Come forth, oh splendid Romeo,
Who fearfully have lurked
In corners dim where red beers flow,
And who, since days of long ago,
Have neither spun nor worked!
The danger most you fear has gone—
There is a turnip famine on !
Step into light of day again,
“The greatest Hamlet, sir,
That ever came within the ken
Of keen and cultivated men.”
You yet may make a stir.
The stage awaits you. Fear no foe—
Tomatoes have declined to grow !
Oh, Wilfrid Denvers unsurpassed,
And Ingomars divine,
From stages driven by the blast
Of carrots red, in anger cast;
Othello's superfine—
Why cower in dim, suburban bars
With tubers dear as motor cars?
Petruchio and Milky White
And Surfaces whose wit
Made gay the longest winter night—
Awake, arise, assert your right
To be supremely “It” !
A vegetable famine ! Lo,
No ammunition has the foe!
VICTORIA.

FIVE BOYS ESCAPE CROUP.
“During the winter months my five boys were
constantly attacked with colds and croup,” says
Mrs. H. Beanland, “Dutton Lee,” Howitt Street,
Ballarat, Vic. “I tried all sorts of medicines for
them, but Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy was the
only one that would ward off these attacks. It
cured each of my five children. I have used dozens
of bottles of this remedy, and within a quarter of
an hour after starting to give it, I have always been
able to break up an attack of croup.”*
Gentleman Bill created a stir in the Sports’ Club
Room the other Friday evening. He had never worn
a moustache, but this time he had a full-grown
military “mo” that converted him from mediocrity
to fame. It was a handsome affair, that moustache
of Bill’s. “Where’d you get it, Bill?” “Take it
off, Willie!” “How did you manage it?” were the
questions that were hurled at Bill as he walked into
the club-room. A reporter, however, got him into a
corner and asked him all about it, and Bill told him on
the quiet that he had used a few doses of Capillarine ;
and his beautiful moustache bore ample and highly
satisfactory testimony to its effectiveness. Bill said,
“I bought it at Craig and Aitken’s, r>s4 George
Street, Sydney, and it cost me half a guinea.”*
APOLOGY.
The Daily Telegraph Newspaper Co., Ltd.,
v. Australian Workers’ Union.
WE, The Australian Workers’ Union, hereby
publicly Apologise to The Daily Telegraph
Newspaper Co., Ltd., and its Directors and
Conductors, for having printed to the order ol a
customer a Leaflet attributing the political advocacy
of “The Daily Telegraph” in the recent Referendum
Campaign to grossly corrupt motives. v* e n ?"
place it on record that we have no sympathy with
the defamatory statements contained in the leaflet
complained of, and hereby admit that there never was
any foundation for making the same ; and in considera-
tion of The Daily Newspaper Co., Ltd., foregoing its
claim for damages and discontinuing this action, we
(The Australian Workers’ Union) hereby agree t
publish, at our own expense, this apology m sue
papers, in such conspicuous places therein, as in
Daily Newspaper Co., Ltd., shall direct, and will ais
pay such costs and expenses as between. Attorney an
Client as The Daily Telegraph Newspaper Co., iaa->
has been put to in the matter.
(Signed) THOS. WHITE,
Acting President.
Australian Workers’ union.
Dated this 18th day of August, 1911.

“Button-stick” on a military matter: —
“C.” pours a wild volley into “Acting Private”
in the argument as to whether Australia’s army should
pad the weary hoof or be hoisted up on a horse;
but he overshoots the mark. He says with a scorn-
ful sniff that an all-mounted army is impossible, be-
cause it couldn’t be fed. He argues that the army
would be reduced to carving a hind leg off its moke
for dinner, and that no army could tackle the trans-
port problem for all-mounted troops. He quotes
S’Africa; but he says nothing about the Boer army
there. That was a solid mounted army, and I don’t
think it ever had to go on shorter rations than the
Britisher. Of course the Boers were in their own
country, and had supplies stocked up. But wouldn’t
that apply equally here? And the Boers managed
to keep their transport arrangements going in some
miraculous fashion, in spite of the dislocations
caused by defeats and hurried scoots. Perhaps De
Wet might be persuaded to take on the job of
Quartermaster-General, and give us some wrinkles
on how to feed the brute —man and quadruped. He
did quite a lot of things that the text-books rule
out as utterly preposterous; and he didn’t seem to
sweat much over it. either.
Melbourne’s organ of the uppali classes is
exceedingly annoyed with a Prahran district
policeman and the local magistrates who
back him up in his scandalous habit of sum-
moning motor-car drivers. Recently this
dangerous official and enemy of Sassiety
“pulled” the State Gov.’s chauffeur for doing
a sprint along Tooralc-road, a thoroughfare
which ought to be regarded as a playground
for the motah classes. Gov. and Lady Ful-
ler happened to be riding in the car when it
broke the law; but there have been cases
when the chauffeurs of the A r ery Nicest were
summoned for driving their own friends at
a furious rate, thus bringing the name of the
influential owner of the car into notoriety.
In order to put a stop to this vulgarising of
a superior pastime, Norman Bayles, M.L.A.,
who moves in the Best Circles, has intro-
duced a Bill providing that any unauthor-
ised person who uses another person’s motah
shall be subject to a penalty not exceeding
£2O or a year’s imprisonment. Perhaps
when some common adventurer is languish-
ing in gaol for 12 months for unlawfully
using N. Bayles’s car, N. Bayles and the
rest of the Nicest may feel better —it will be
a sort of vengeance upon the class (or no-
class ) to which the annoying policeman
belongs.

It isn’t only the rag-shop girl who is given
the choice between the virtuous existence, cold
and colorless as a miserable wage pittance can
make it, and the sparkle of life procurable
only in her case by the other kind of exist-
ence. The girl with the greatest temptation
is probably the waitress in the tea and re-
freshment rooms, where the young bloods and
the old crocks talk “joy-rides,” theatres,
week-ends on the Mountains, and all the
other dangers that Virtue is heiress to each
day and every night. There are hundreds
of these girls in the
big Commonwealth
cities whose wages
range from 7s. 6d. a
week upward—n o t
too much upward—
and their hours linger
between 58 and 60
per working week.
Out of their few shil-
lings these girls are
expected by Snuffle-
dom to board them-
selves, dress “respect-
ably,” and be content
with their lot, or,
rather, little. If one
should break away
and snatch some of
the pleasure and gai-
ety of life by the only
means the sweater
leaves open to her,
Snutiledom knows no
uncharitableness too
severe for her “wan-
tonness,” no point of
view too unsympathe-
tic for her “wicked-
ness.” But Snuffle-
dom never rises in its
wrath to denounce
the cause of it all; it
did not even rise
when, as happened in
Sydney a few days
ago, a sweater, who
worked a 17-y ea r-
old girl 83J hours
a week, with no half-holiday, and paid her
7s. fid., was fined jive shillings!
* * * *
The law is a serious-minded animal which
jokes with difficulty, and mostly without in-
tention ; wherefore it recently listened stolidly
for several hours while a gent, one argued
at great length that a certain farmer had
not behaved as a farmer should. This far-
mer had been tossed bv a neighbor’s bull, and
was suing for damages, and tne defendant’s
chief plea was that the plaintiff’s “mode of
warning the bull off his premises caused it
irritation, and led to its being put in a cor-
ner from which it had no escape, wherefore
it was obliged to use its natural resources,
but only in the way of self-defence.” The
non-legal jury wasn’t so impressed by this
beautiful guff as it might have been, and
curtly remarked, “Damages, £115.”
FRIENDS.
" Well, anyhow, Major Smith-Jones had four dances with me, and only one with you,!"
“ But you seem to forget, dear, the dance was a charity affair!”
Years ago, Spencer-street, Melbourne, op-
posite the patched-up railway station, used
to be a sailors’ haunt, and the intending
train traveller used to cannon off groups of
yarning men from the wind-jammers in the
river. Then the Metropolitan Board of
Works wrenched itself loose from its rented
offices in Collins-street, and took possession
of the fine old building, while the sea-dogs
rolled down the hill with £lO,OOO in their
breeches pockets, and anchored themselves
with a foundation-stone on a new site, close
to the wharf, presented by the Harbor
Trust. Melbourne, thereabouts, is not very
firm on its feet; hence the new Home
has split from roof to basement, and is sink-
ing on its foundations. “Well, crack me
plates if ever I thought I’d sink ashore!”
says the son of Shiver-Me-Timbers, A. 8.,
looking up at the damage. All hands expect
shortly to be ordered to the financial pumps.
“Mallarapoonie,” re Westralia’s biggest
and deadest engineer :
Westralia hasn’t done much to honor the memory
of her dead engineer, O’Connor, by piling up the bald
and somewhat incongruous combination of bronze
and granite in the back yard of the shabby Harbor
Trust office at Fremantle. The eingy of the Big
Man isn’t a thing of beauty, anyhow, and its posi-
tion is worse. The figure faces out through a gap
between the sooty railway sheds, across a sombre ex-
panse of wharf floored with condemned railway
sleepers, to the garbage-strewn harbor, with its
cluster of coal hulks in the middle distance. Be-
hind it is the frowsiness of one of the oldest sea-
ports in Australia. On its right are the railway
yards; to the left the scrapheaps of an abandoned
foundry. This afternoon the funnel of a stinking
collier is belching smoke in its face. The grave,
thoughtful eyes (which you can notice if you mind
and don’t step backward off the wharf) seem to gaze
out across the harbor (which is really only his second-
greatest work) to the north-west ocean. Behind the
monument and the Trust office is Fremantle’s ridicu-
lous fort. This structure can’t be said to “grin,”
neither does it “bristle” in the manner of forts I
have read about. Australia’s enemies probably
won’t notice it when they come, and it wouldn’t
have been noticed in this paragraph except that
C. Y. O’Connor’s statue is in a line between it and
the open sea —that part of the ocean under the lee
of Rottnest where a hostile battleship might reason-
ably be expected to drop anchor if it wanted to
argue with Fremantle’s fort. Probablv it wouldn’t
want to do so, but, if one ever does, it will be a dead-
sure thing that the hostile gunners will use the
white granite pedestal of O’Connor’s statue for a
sighter to train their guns, and that won’t be nice
for the pedestal. Rather than put O’Connor up amid
such dismal surroundings, with the foul smoke of
colliers in his face, and a prospect of being some day
shot at, it would have looked much better to have
erected him on the scene of his greatest and most
successful labors —near the gigantic scheme that
finally worried him to death. Out of sheer decency
Westralia might have put him wlmre he would have
most wished —-on the clean, quiet hills above Mundar-
ing weir; there to gaze toward remote Kalgoorlie,
the wondrous desert-city, sprawling on its thirsty,
sun-smitten plain, that praises the great man’s name
with every drop of water it consumes.
The story of the “strained relations” :
The beginning goes back some distance —from the
time when the G.-G. wanted a steamer to be char-
tered that he might go to Papua, and the Common-
wealth Government refused to charter, but offered
him the more economical trip by regular steamer.
Then the departed Gov.-General wanted an expedi-
tion de luxe, and that was refused again in favor of
economical but quite adequate transport. After
that the G.-G. made an appointment with a Minister
at Sydney Government House for a certain hour, and
overlooked it or something, and left the Minister to
cool his heels on the mat, so to speak. The Minister
left a telephone address for two hours later, and
when the G.-G. rang him up at the time appointed,
he replied that he could make no other appointment
for that day. The G.-G. conducted a Coronation
levee in Melbourne, and not one Federal Minister
attended. He protested, and then he got facts —un-
pleasant facts. He was reminded that the Govern-
ment had done its duty in fitly and loyally recognis-
ing the Coronation; that, finding that the G.-G. did
not intend to give a State dinner in honor of the
Coronation, it had spent the Commonwealth’s money
-—maybe £ 2000—in giving one at which the G.-G.
sat at the head of the table. After that cold douche,
diplomatic relations broke off, and when the G.-G.
left Australia no Ministers attended to be frozen in
their Cabinet capacities. The dailies report that
one of them did visit the ship that bore the retiring
official away from the Best Country; but, if so, it
was only an expression of the young man’s kindness
of heart.
Chief Justice Stout’s report to the Maori-
laud Government on charges laid against
Captain Smith, Resident Commissioner of
the Cook Islands, which was placed before
the Parliament a few days ago, practically
says “Rubbish !” to them all. “In a small
community like Raratonga” (126 whites and
2620 natives), says Stout, “ it is not_ sur-
prising to find a difference of opinion
amongst the inhabitants. The climate also
invites misunderstandings.... If one were to
read a novel, say, of Jane Austen, Anthony
Trollope or J. M. Barrie, he would observe
that the same class of misunderstandings ap-
peared in villages in England and Scotland,
and, no doubt, arise there to-day.” Finally,
the Judge dismisses the Commissioner with
a round of applause for his administration,
which, he says, has brought about “exceeding
prosperity.” A number of people who orig-
inated the disturbance which ended in Stout,
C.J.,beingappointed Royal Commissioner are,
however, not satisfied, on the ground that
the inquiry was not a public inquiry, and
that Stout shows signs of prejudice; but
Stout no doubt knew what he was talking
about when he said that he could the better
acquire the fullest information by an in-
quiry that was not onen to the public. The
charge of prejudice was to be expected in
the ordinary course of business. It is hard
to please everybody these times—and every-
body doesn’t deserve to be pleased, anyhow.

—Bank of Victoria shareholders’ meeting.
Serf of the ledger and pen !
Slave of the desk and ink!
Justice has come to you, then,
Ere you’ve had time to think.
Balance at dead of night—
But be glad, ye slaves of the banks!
Money lias seen the Right—
Pays you with “votes of thanks.”
Money, that makes some men !
Money that eats men, too !
Rest in your sweating den —
Money, that plays the Jew ;
Yet at the core is just
(They who deny it are cranks) ;
You, who are down in the dust,
Are paid with a “vote of thanks” !
Here is a splendid scheme.
When the fatted butcher calls
Pay him with bits of an opium dream,
Flag-flapping, and loyal squalls.
Pay the grocer with loud hoorays,
Though he dwindle in brawn and shanks,
And the parson, who loudly for stipend brays,
Feed him with “votes of thanks.”
To the bank that keeps your account
(If to keep your account one’s game)
Pay-in bits of “The Sermon on the Mount” —
But draw cheques just the same.
And, once you’ve an overdraft,
You’ve joined the Monied Ranks;
And though they object till they’re blind and daft,
Pay with “a vote of thanks.”
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

The minister whose charge is small
Can waste no time, it’s plain ;
He breathes a friendly word to all,
And hopes you’ll come again.
He must get very frequently
The little fee he earns.
So shall his simple motto be:

“Historicus” : J. F. Hogan, of London, is
wrong in his allegation (B. 24/8/T1) that,
“since the death of James Bonwick, there has
been nobody in London officially charged
with the duty of looking after precious pieces
of paper”—such as the Captain Cook manu-
scripts, which were auctioned at Sotheby’s
last month. As a fact, a well-known London
firm of experts is officially employed for this
purpose by the Public Library_ trustees. The
agent’s duty is to inspect anything of the sort
that is offered anywhere in the United King-
dom. and, if the matter is urgent, to cable
for instructions. This was duly done in
regard to the Cook MSS., but, as they con-
sisted of only a few scattered sheets, it was
not thought worth while, at this end, to bid
for them. David Scott Mitchell (he of the
Library) left the cash for such purchases—
a matter of £70,000.
* * * *
Batchelor, of the External Affairs De-
partment, is home again, so nresumably the
Boon Gooev matter will be settled at once:
and it is to be hoped the Minister won’t
talk of settling it “without sentiment,” as
the Acting-Minister did. As a matter of
fact, the White Australia law is the most
sentimental work of legislation amongst tlie
statutes. It expresses the heart’s desire of
the white population. Findley’s talk of ad-
ministering it without sentiment was, there-
fore, as insincere as Scotchbyterian Balfour's
talk of God having joined Hop Boon Gooey
to his wife —a wife whom he had brought to
Australia under contract to send her back to
China in six months. Meanwhile the facts
of the case suggest to every honestly-senti-
mental White Australian a right and proper
settlement. A well-to-do Chow —a produce
merchant —has a business in Geelong, and a
wife who must go back to Hongkong.' Every
sentimental fibre in the Aliens Restriction
Act would be shocked if she were allowed to
remain here, and add Austral-Chinese chil-
dren to her husband’s other produce. Hence
she must go, and if God has joined them toge-
ther. as Balfour, M.L.C.. declares, her hus-
band will of course go with her.

Mr. Yung Liang Hwang, Chinese Consul-
General, made an important pronouncement
in Melbourne the other night—a pronounce-
ment eagerly seized upon by the morning
dailies. “I believe,” said Y. L. Hwang,
“that the doctrine of Christ will be of great
benefit to humanity.” Obviously China is
awakening. It may be, however, that there
is a smack of Oriental irony in the observa-
tion. Note, anyway, that Yung Hwang uses
the future tense, meaning, perhaps, that the
doctrine will be of great benefit to humanity
somewhere in the time to come, when the Fat
interests are outed and Christianity has a
chance of operating.
“Nix” : Now that the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment has made up its mind to be a foster-
father to Binghi, let me
draw attention to a scheme
which the squatters in the
nor’-west of W.A. have been
and still are working. They
are legally permitted. to
“sign on” a nigger for 12
months, and, during that
time, if he runs away, he
can be pursued and forcibly
brought back to Simon
Legree. In return, black
brother receives, or, rather,
he is supposed to receive, a
good ration allowance (in-
cluding tobacco), two suits
of clothes, and medicine
when he is sick ; but in these
nor’-west parts the average
squatter would not dream of
bankrupting himself for such
extravagances. The “sign-
ing on,” which is optional
< sometimes) with the nig.,
may be performed before a
magistrate, police constable,
or J. Pay; and the J. Pay
is generally a neighbor who
would be greatly distressed
to see a poor abo. unable to
make up his mind as to
what was best for his
own welfare. Not long ago
one aristocrat, after waiting
until a mounted policeman
had departed, “siened on” 12
bucks for a year, in the sym-
pathetic presence of a bro-
ther broadacres. And, if I
know anything about the
aristocrat, it wasn’t because
he was concerned about in-
conveniencing the trooper
that he passed the job on to
somebody else.
The N.S.W. Government’s tentative pro-
posal to set aside a sum for a public crema-
torium in Sydney begat a mild howl of oppo-
sition. One version was that, on Adelaide’s
showing, the thing was useless, only eight
cremations having taken place in S.A. in as
many years, and the incinerator had conse-
quently been closed. As a matter of fact —-
assuming that the curator of the Adelaide
cemetery states the fact —there have been no
less than 46 burnings since 1903, and the
practice is surely, if slowly, gaining favor.
Only the other day the body of the locally
weli-known G. S. Aldridge was so disposed
of. Centuries-old insanitary prejudice will
take a lot of undermining; but even on the
Adelaide figures, Sydney, with three times
the other city’s population, is justified in
making a start, and thus educating the public
up to cheaper and less sliuddersome methods
of body-disposal.

preme Court ap-
peal from a Jay
Pay decision re-
cently showed the
cussedness of the
law, as well as
the bumblelieaded-
ness of the magis-
trate. One Helan-
der had been con-

consisting of a

or bush J’s.P., of
fraudulent appro-
priation of certain
goods. The goods
had been jettisoned
by a vessel in dis-
tress in the Bellin-
ger River, and
were discovered
some time after by
Ilelander, who
dived and brought
them up. Some
were claimed and
given up to their
owners; but as
those not claimed
were kept by the
diver as salvage,
the villain was
prosecuted for lar-
ceny. Of course a
nolle prosequi was
entered bv the At-
torney - General.
Then the desper-
ately-eager police
charged the man
with fraudulent
appropriation, and
the Bene h con-
victed. Fo r t un-
ate Iy, Ilelander
was in a position
to take the matter
to the Full Court,
which promptly
(piashed the conviction, on various grounds
of law and merit. Then came the applica-
tion on behalf of Helander, who asked that
his costs should be paid by the blunderers
who had put him to all the expense and
worry; but Bring, J., said that, as he had
heard that the conviction was by a majority
vote of the magistrates’ Bench, if the Court
gave an order for costs “it would be punish-
ing a member of the Bench who held a cor-
rect view of the law.” So costs were re-
fused, and punishment is thus meted out to

for compensation should not lie against the
Crown. Meantime, presumably the ridicu-
lous J’s.P. who were responsible for the
blunder will resign. If they don’t do
it voluntarily, Holman should give them
his cordial assistance.
The Oldest Inhabitant among the ruins:
Two of the oldest houses in Sydney have just
been turned over to the knacker, in connection with
the big scheme for improving Lower George-street.
In the ’4o’s Henry Ferris, merchant, and Jules Jou-
bert, wine merchant, hung out their signs in one of
these places ; in the other was -printed Sydney’s
pioneer Mercantile Journal, and alongside was
the pub kept by Samuel Thornton, whose son, the
evergreen George, was Mayor in 1857. In the mid-
’6o’s both houses passed over to Chinese merchants,
and one at least of the original two business shingles
is up still —“On Chong,” though no doubt the real
On Chong, if he ever existed, disappeared many years
ago.

Victoria’s Foods Standards Committee has
collided with the Weary Cabinet, and has
recoiled with a stiff back and a determina-
tion to have its own way in the end. Re-
cently the committee learned that buttei
without preservative of any sort was being
placed on the London market from Den-
mark and America, and it didn't see why
Australia should use what others find un-
necessary. Before recommending anything
sudden, however, it tested 000 samples ofc
cowfat sold for local consumption, and
found half of them to be prime samples with-
out preservative. Knowing, further, that
preservatives of nearly all descriptions are
more or less harmful, and are mostly fraud-
ulent, in that they are used to conceal
some defect or rascality, the committee
recommended the Cabinet to pass the neces-
sary regulation. But the Cabinet wouldn t
do anything of the sort. It put its opinion
against the knowledge of experts, specially
appointed to do special work; and it kept
the door open for the unclean, the incompe-
tent, and the dishonest buttermaker. But
the committee isn’t lying down, and will
open up the matter at the next inter-State
conference on pure foods. And, by that time,
the Weary Cabinet may have become the
Worn-out Cabinet.
* * * *
A queer case has just been heard in Ade-
laide. A country constable had a complaint
from two young men that “Yendinna Jack”
had robbed their camp, and was in general a
bad nigger. The Law was busy or some-
thing, so it deputed the two complainants to
make an arrest, and they went to do it.
When they came across their mao there was
a struggle, one or two shots were fired, and
Jack was dropped with a wound in the leg
and taken off in a cart. It didn’t seem much
of a wound; nevertheless, Jack died on the
way. For this both the men and the con-
stable were tried for murder. They pleaded that
the wound was inflicted in self-defence, and
that death was really due to blackfellow funk.
The Chief Justice said the constable’s be-
havior was altogether illegal, but the shoot-
ing could not be murder, and he left it to the
jury to say what else it was. And since the
jjury was evidently in a fog, it chanced it, and
merely said Not Guilty.
Victoria’s quaint Attorney-General Dun
Brown was never suspected of being a humor-
ist until a week ago, and then he earned
the title by doing Maxwell. It was in the
matter of Laura Fox, who, after a particu-
larly exhaustive inquiry by the Coroner, had
been committed for trial on a charge of mur-
der-constructive murder. Being unable to
hire a barrister for her defence, she nomin-
ated lawyer Maxwell, who would be paid the
usual £7'7s. fee by the Crown. Brown, how-
ever, refused to accept her nomination, as
Maxwell had, in a previous case, acted in a
high-handed way of his own. Even assuming
Maxwell was in the wrong, his selection as
counsel by Laura Fox was a matter with
which Brown had nothing whatever to do.
Brown apparently recognised this in a day
or so, as he presently “accepted” Laura Fox’s
nomination of Maxwell on condition that the
lawyer promised to appear for her at the
proper time and place. Maxwell declined to
be bound by any conditions whatever, where-
upon Brown cogitated a while, and then
“accepted” the nomination unconditionally.
When the case came on, with Maxwell ap-
pearing for Laura Fox, Brown played his
trump card —a curt intimation by the Crown
Prosecutor that “no presentment had been
filed.” So Laura Fox goes free, Maxwell
loses his poor little fee, and Attorney-General
Dry Brown sits back and no doubt enjoys the
joke. And Laura Fox probably enjoys it too.
Whether the public should view it in the
same frivolous way is another matter.

Some interesting: facts
about the height of ocean
waves and the depth of
ocean troughs have been
gathered in lately by a
couple of Australian lin-
ers. The Orsova had a
nasty bucketing in the
Bight, when the waves
reached up and clawed
lumps off the captain's
bridge, which is (50ft.
above sea-level. They
also playfully snatched
overboard a windlass
cover, the cook’s galley,
and a miscellaneous lot
of iron piping and fit-
tings. As to the depth
of the hole in the water,
there is the report of the
Marine Court on the
damage done to the Catk-
in passing through Port
Phillip Heads. She had
a clear lift, under her
keel in crossing the
shallowest part of the Rip bank, yet she fell
into a hole which was deep enough to allow
her to bump the bottom. The Court found
no blame attaching to anyone except Mr.
Dave Jones, and it’s no good reprimanding
him or trying to take away his ticket.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Stingaree”: Recently witnessed a will-
ing “go” between a black-banded water snake and
ia green tree snake on the bank of a narrow creek.
In a smother of dust, twigs and leaves, the reptiles
were coiling and uncoiling with lightning rapidity,
advancing and retreating, and snapping with the
greatest fury. The tree snake was evidently getting
the better of the scrap, for it was engaged in cutting
off the retreat of its antagonist towards the creek.
At last the submarine snake adopted fresh tactics,
and, seizing the other, dragged him to the bank,
rolled with him into the water, and made desperate
efforts to lug him under the surface. He would
have managed it too, if I hadn’t introduced a
boulder weighing lewt. into the argument, which
thereupon ended in a dead heat.

TOMMY: “Say, dad, who was Shy lock? ”
DAD: “ What! Don't know who Shy lock was! What do I send you to Sunday-school

“Gecko” : Saw a fast greyhound flush a
fox from the cap of a kopje the other day, and start
down hill after him. I was just going to hoist
the numbers up as they reached a large gum,
when Keynard threw a kind of half twister, grabbed
the firm earth at the base of the tree with his fore-
claws, and hurled his after part Catherine-wheel-like
into position on the O.P. side of the butt. The dog
went straight on for about a mile, and, when he re-
turned to weigh in, he looked as glum as a Mayor
who had been jambed behind the door when Corona-
tion honors were ladled out.
“Eddyson” : This is how Mrs. Jerry got
married. Mrs. Jerry lived a mile and a half from
Tollbar, on the road to the Nine Mines. Cows kept
Mrs. Jerry. I don’t know how many cows there
were, but never was the blocky little woman en-
tirely free from the bovine animal. When she wasn’t
busy milking cows, or feeding cows, driving cows to
the paddock, or driving cows home, attending to the
complaints of ailing cows, or delivering milk to her
customers at Tollbar and Nine Mines —carrying it
with an old-fashioned wooden contrivance and two
pails—she was scalding pans, or cleaning up the
dairy or the cowyard. Mrs. Jerry was a widow, and
had three children, and my private opinion is that
she was the woman who never went to bed. How
she found time for courtship cannot be imagined,
but it was made known at Tollbar and Nine Mines
that she was to be married to Pisht, stoker at the
Grand Slam, on a given date. Meanwhile the
widow continued her zealous attendance on. cow, and
her excursions as sole milk-supply of two districts.
The wedding day came, the hour struck. Pisht, a
foreign person with the intellectual outlook of a
hen, and the genial expression of a large quantity
of dough, was available, waiting in the church with
several friends and an assortment of residents. Mrs.
Jerry was not on hand. Fifteen weary minutes
passed, and then came a rattling of cans in the
church porch, and presently the bonny bride ap-
peared, bearing her customary burden of cow extract,
as if interrupted in the middle of a round. She
set down her pails in the aisle, was well and truly
married to Pisht, and then incontinently resumed her
task of scattering milk around the township. When
I left, a year later, Pisht was a stoker, but not
practising, and cows were still keeping Mrs. Pisht.

“M&ysboy”: After two hours’ wrangle it
came to this. (1) The boss bet the mail-coach
driver his pub—lock, stock and pewter—that the
main bone in a horse’s leg cannot be set, because a
prad’s bones contain oil instead of marrow, and that
an apparent knit is brought about by a formation
of gristle binding the broken parts. (2) Coachy is
prepared to move in as soon as he can produce the
proof that the boss is talking through his neck.
Has anybody got the conclusive evidence handy ?

“J.V.SVIc.” : . I think the best-known abor-
iginal identity must surely have been the late
Paddy Perkins. He was known from Oharleville in
the west to Brisbane, and from Gympie to miles past
the N. S. Wales border. Paddy once sang “Annie
Laurie” to Melba, while she was passing through
Toowoomba by mail train. She let him off with a
sovereign, which he immediately sold for a guinea.
It was at Jimbour, lately resumed by the Queens-
land Government for closer settlement, that Paddy
with a black (buck) mate was sent out by the
manager to do some ringbarking. The manager
gave them a fortnight’s tucker, and said he would
be around in the evening. However, he did not
go; and at the end of a fortnight Paddy and Co.
came trooping in and asked for more tucker. “Why,”
asked the boss, “haven’t you finished?” “Finished,”
says Paddy,; “by cripes, we never started. You
never come round show us where to plurry well start
from!” What remains of Paddy lies near Dalby,
under a headstone raised by public subscription.
“T.V.T.” : It was a blazing hot day, and
the 130-ton haystack at Itillglassey was being
topped. One young husky, from whom the sweat
was dropping profusely, was making frantic efforts
to land his final half-dozen sheaves. The last ap-
peared to have him beat. Time after time he hurled
it up, only to see it come tumbling back to his feet
again. “Him good hand at throwin’ sheaf, Jacky!”
remarked the boss sarcastically, as he gazed at the
perspiring pitcher. “Huh,” grunted the nig, “mine
bin tinkit dat pfeller better at trowin’ plurry
boomerang.”
REFLECTIONS.
HOPELESS BILL (soliloquising): “ Ah! It's wonderful, wonderful how little we knew !”
GINGER RAFFERTY: “ Yes, yes! You and me might be sillin’ on this 'ere spot, Ginger,
healthy and well one day-and next be in gaol!"
“Iver Lau” : I want to sool the brave and
gallant Thomas Waddell on to “Mr. Justice Ocky”
(B. 20/7/’ll), for saying poisonous snakes don’t
climb. Last summer, in Dunoon, near Lismore
(N.S.W.), I saw 4ft. of black streak climbing a large
blue gum. He didn’t climb round the tree, but went
up one side —if a round tree can have a side —with
the usual wriggle. Years ago, in the same locality,
I chased a black snake into a loquat tree, and
chucked him under the chin with a prop. He
didn’t seem to mind it, either; anyhow, he didn’t
lodge any complaint at the inquest.
• * * *
“Ken Hill”: The episode related by
“Berkeley” (B. 29/6/’ll), re the snake that used to
get on the soft side of a cow for a drink, is not
unique by any means. Writer calls to mind one
Jones—-William his other name was —an influential
cow cocky of Gippsland (S.A.), who was obliged,
owing to the scarcity of labor, to utilise snakes of
the same species as “Berkeley’s.” Of course they
had to be first mesmerised. Jones, fortunately, pos-
sessed the power to do this. I myself tested several
of the cows after the milking operation, and found
them as dry as Houston Reid’s dog or the bed of the
Cotter.
* « * *
“Gecko” : The kookaburra is most cautious
in his attack on all species of reptiles. One Jack
that I trained in the bush to take grubs, lizards, and
small snakes, from my hand, always gripped the
wriggler by the Scruff. Tried him several times by
holding a bandy-bandy or “whip” by the head, but
he wouldn’t budge from his perch until I reversed
matters, and let the head dangle. Then Johnny
swooped and took the titbit. In winter, snakes
were not on the menu, and he seemed to blame me.
One day I bluffed him with a sausage. He belted it
tour times against the fence, and then swallowed it
doubtfully. I couldn’t fool him again. Probably
because I forgot to put the bones in.
“Geofred”: “Feenix” (B. 3/S/’ll) says
canaries don’t mind humans handling their eggs.
Wrong. The hen bird generally has no objection,
but unless “Feenix” has his lady-canary’s husband
put into a separate cage when the missus is setting,
he’d better not try handling the eggs. “Feenix”
says he’s been breeding ’naries for 15 years. I go
10 on that. Like “Feenix,” I used to make it a prac-
tice of purloining the eggs, one by one, and putting
them back when three eggs were laid, ready for in-
cubation. But every time I did this, directly his
better half’s back was turned, Mr. ’Nary would bang
the eggs on to the floor. I tried four different pairs
of birds at this game, and, as a result, got 12
broken eggs. As for “Grand Slam’s” emus (also
B. S/8/’ll), they must be different emus to those
in most other parts of ’Stralia. Up at Forbes last week,
some friends took me on an alleged fox-hunt. We
saw 5521$ rabbits, one kangaroo, no foxes, two
emus and one emu’s cottage. In the cottage, after
we had shoved Ma Emu away, were nine eggs. I
took two of them and left seven. Three days after,
when going back into Forbes, we chased Mrs. Emu
off her nest again, and there were still seven eggs
there, and she, apparently, hadn’t noticed that we
had been doing a subtraction sum on the premises.
Robert Salmon, of Boolcoomatta station
(S.A.), has been telling the Adelaide dailies that
the best way to keep dingoes from your sheep is to
breed kangaroos to keep them amused. He had
noticed that the wild dog always passes by sick
sheep for strong and active ones, so he assumed
that the brute is a real sportsman and likes to be
given a run for his meat; and this the ’roo cer-
tainly gives him. So the sheep are left alone. It
sounds steep, but that is Salmon’s theory ; and there
are the kangaroos, carefully preserved on Boolcoo-
matta, as evidence in the case.
* * * *
“Gip” forwards a winter day from the
highlands of Monaro (N.S.W.) :
Six inches of snow, still dry and powdery. The
sun shines warmly in a grey-blue sky. We plougn
a track through the whiteness, over the close-meeting
rocks of the creek, and up into the range. A score
or so of puzzled weaners, struggling upward, oblig-
ingly make a track for us. Here and there we
drop a fox bait, but we draw no trail. Cold? Not
a bit! We are shedding weight perceptibly as we
reach the top, and one of us is eating snow. A
wallaby leaps up out of one gully, crosses the sum-
mit, and scurries down into another. Here, foxes’
tracks are thick. By a white-crowned tussock a
rabbit sits motionless, ears flattened, body crouched.
Bang goes a gun, and startles a fox out of a near-by
bush. Another shot, and the dogs are after him,
diving and rolling, until suddenly they strike a
drift and disappear. We turn to assist them, only
to take laugh-raising headers ourselves. Reynard
leaps lightly for the scrub, and is seen no more.
The birds, strangely tamed, barely notice us. Robin
redbreasts are everywhere—wee, glowing things hop-
ping on the snow’s crust. Magpies scratch on
thawed patches; crows, cawing dismally, fly low.
A bronze-wing sits disheartened on a bough, and a
wounded wood-duck rises dejectedly and flies onward.
Parrots, full-fed, chatter bravely as they flash from
briar to briar, choosing, like epicures, the reddi s‘
berries. Here and there bunny has shovelled the
snow from his door, and left dainty tracks heading
tucker wards, but most of the family, lies snuggled
up in the home burrow. We return in fine spirits,
but with a very light bag—four rabbits to two
guns! The sun is spreading pink and gold and
faintest mauve upon the glittering hills, and the
snow grows crisp again, under the icy fingers of the
evening wind.

THE ROUSEABOUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am the Rouseabout!
The Generally Useful!
A mere social microbe
Of no status ..liatever.
The first and the last!
Impartial, dexterous, eternal.
Through 20 hours and some seconds
I swing the edged axe
At the roots of the ironbark,
Reducing them to kindling.
I groom, milk, and keep clean the cow-lawn.
Boots, knives, pots, wash-ups, yards, verandahs,
And the übiquitous outhouse
Are mine—so to, speak.
Anon,
Dumbfounded at reproof ;
Meek, contrite and solemn ;
A looselipped humility
iPersonified.
Again,
•Cyclonic, skilful, gentlemanly, aeroplanicseven-
things-at-once—
’“Yessir!” “Yessum !”
When whistled to,
Unhesitating as to which dog is called.
Intuitive as thought-reader ;
Expert as physiognomist;
Abnormally willing,
And
Equally obliging.
Smiling idiotically in the teeth of provocation ;
Acting Marconigraphically in the midst of whirling
D—, etc.
On Saturday,
Apologetic, statuefaced—
A back-curved atom in dungarees,
Waiting for 15 bob with guilty face —
That’s me,
The Rouseabout 1
VICTORIA.
MORPHIA.
THE MILKERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jim Merit came to Raffle’s door.
He drove a spotted cow before,
And close behind, cross-eyed and plain,
Behold his sister, Mary Jane.
Said he to Raffle: “You’re the gent
What printed this advertisement?”
He showed an ad. the rest among:
“A Milker Wanted. Must be Young.”
“I am,” the farmer answered him.
A sigh broke from the breast of Jim :
“I couldn’t make it out nohow —
What do you want, a wife or cow?
“If it’s a cow, well, here is Floss—
Best milker in the country, boss.
If it’s a wife, me sister there’s
The finest milker anywheres.”
VICTORIA.

“Barcoo”: “Calafanc” (B. 29/6/M 1) is
advised that what he takes to be green, blue or
pinkish stains in the sheep’s overcoat only occur
when vegetation is particularly abundant. When
next he goes robbing Mary’s little lamb of its
blanket, let him take a microscope along and more
closely inspect the apparent stain, and he will dis-
cover a colony of minute insects too small to be seen
by the unaided eye.
“Cyl”: “Klinker” (B. 29/6/M1) tells a
yarn of a methodical marine engineer. We have one
on our ship who has been trained with a gauge. If
he cuts a piece of paper the gauge must go on it.
So much has this gauge business become a habit
with him, that the other day at tea, when asking the
messroom boy for bread, he passed the gauge for the
youth to cut it by.

DRIFTING DAN: “ Well, you see, sir , after I'd treated meself ter a champagne supper at
