“Gecko”: There are yellow snakes sure
enough—yellow as Bro. Dill Macky’s kindred evil.
Killed three of them last summer at Manilla
(N.S.W.), where they are fairly numerous. But
they were all females, the male of the species being"
leaden-colored, with rufous stripes from throat to
vent. Haven’t heard anybody announce the attend-
ance of the black crawler, sprinkled irregularly with
blobs like .wattle blossom. Would the butler or the
rouseabout or somebody kindly yell out the name, so
that we can all hear?
“Alaric”: “C.0.D.” (B. 3/8/T1) wants
to know why common geranium scares Hades out of
snakes. It doesn’t; it no more gives them the “icy
heart” than a Yellow Pup meeting in Sydney does.
The only snake I have ever seen on our verandah
came out of a box of geraniums. Again, I know of
a tiger snake that took up his abode in a bed of
geraniums for a week, though there was plenty of
other cover available, and in all probability he
would be there yet if he hadn’t met the business-end
of a spade.
NOCTURNE.
(By a Post-Impressionist.)
“Causeway”: Poison-Cart Bill had been
“sinking” the proceeds of months of driving the
rabbit ’bus, and he was loaded to the nines by the
time he cannoned through the last swing-door, zig-
zagged across the road, and subsided gloriously on a
garbage heap. Here the flies, centipedes, alligators
and other insects disported themselves on his face,
and played hopscotch on his neck, until a bull-ant
rolled up his sleeves and tore into Bill’s ear. Where-
upon that usually tolerant person rose and snorted:
“That settles it! Now, you’ll all have to get off!”
“M.A.R.” (from a Gippsland camp) : Are
the bolts of Jove visible? Awhile ago, this neigh-
borhood, for five or six miles round, was frightened
'out of its bunk by the most thunderous crash that
will be heard this side of the Last Trump. In due
time it crawled from beneath the bed, and went out
to investigate. There was a strange odor abroad un-
like anything that had ever struck my hose before.
The sky was clear, and bore no trace of clouds or
other “secret places of thunder.” But, 50 yards from
my house, a hundred feet of big box gum had been
torn from its roots, shredded to fragments, and
strewn over the landscape. A big slice of the
stricken giant was deposited at the front door, and
other substantial trifles had been hurled over the
house —which was on higher ground than the tree—
overshooting it by a good 30 yards. The tree was
not burnt or charred in the least. Later, a large
meteoric stone was found at the foot of the hill
right below the scene of the tragedy. No one saw
that stone biff the tree, certainly, but the circum-
stantial evidence is strong enough to convict it.
* * * *
“A.Y.A.”: “Billy Bluegum” (B. 10/8/T1)
probably lets his patriotism run away with him.
Three years spent amidst the mosquitoes, crocs., and
semi-civilised pagans of S. America, convinced
me that Australia does not possess the king bird-
mimic. The true king is the American mocking-
bird—l forget his native name, and, anyway,
’twould loosen the linotype’s teeth to try to spell
it. Not only does he imitate feathered fliers, but he
is a perfect mimic of savage beasts, and On top of
that he is a reptile slaughterer.
* * * *
“Boswell” : Re “Mallarapoonie’s” query
as to the effect of eating the flesh of poisoned
animals. A body poisoned by strychnine is per-
meated throughout, and becomes simply a larger
bait than the original. Arsenic endures while ever
a bone of the victim remains. At a poison
tank on Bringagee station, Murrumbidgee River
(N.S.W.), during the rabbit war of 1890, a pile of
bunny’s bleaching bones accumulated. Four years
afterwards, when the water-poisoning business had
long been in liquidation, five valuable draught
horses of the station made their way to the said
boneyard. They licked the bones, tasted salt, and
licked some more. There were five dead horses on
Bringagee on the following day. Phosphorus
“poisoning,” which is more a burning out of the
stomach, does not have the same virulent after-
effects ; but, all the same, deaths from consuming
bunny killed in this way are not unknown. I
think in all cases the latest recipe of Wade, V.C.,
is the safest, viz., to “fling the bait back in their
faces.”
“Chink”: “Tiaki’s” note on the white-
eye (or tauhou) in Maoriland (B. 27/7/’ll), prompts
me to recall the early history of the little sprites,
so far as Jew Zealand is concerned. The first time
they were sighted there was in the winter of 1856,
when, one morning, the lighthouse-keeper on Dog
Island, in Foveaux Strait, found scores of them
lying dead in the lighthouse gallery. Evidently
they had arrived in the night, or early in the morn-
ing, and had dashed themselves against the lantern.
They must have come from Australia, and had
crossed 1000 miles of ocean before sighting land.
What impelled the small fowl to tackle such an en-
terprise passes comprehension. Members of the flock
that survived the stiff journey settled in the southern
part of Maoriland, and, like the family of old Ham,
the son of Noah, bred exceeding great. Although
they had crossed the 1000 miles of water —no doubt
involuntarily—the visitors meditated hard and long
before they would tackle the 20 or 30 miles of water,
in Cook Strait, separating the North and South
Islands. It was in the ’6o’s before their arrival up
North was simultaneously recorded by Walter Buller
in Wellington, and a Maori mailman up Waikanai
way. The Maoris welcomed the tauhou, i.e., stranger,
with enthusiasm, and they show their appreciation by
establishing him as a staple dish.
* # * *
“Tiaki” : Plunger is a sure cure for pre-
judices, as I have found out at various times cn
various wanderings in Maoriland. I’ve been glad
to welcome morepork toasted on a spit, and octopus
boiled with the centre of the nikau palm (Areca
sapida) as vegetable. Was marooned once on a
mangrove island for two days, foodless. First feed
was roasted spuds dipped in wild honey. Have also
indulged in “kotero”—potatoes steeped in water until
decayed, and then made into cakes and baked.
“Kaanga pirau” (rotten corn)—maize steeped in
water until it alarms the neighborhood—will, when
boiled, prove very satisfying—if there is, nothing
else to be had. Have had to do my best, too, with
crayfish steeped in fresh water (koura mara), ripe
shark, raw sea-eggs in their shell, raw fish, and the
white grub (Prinopulus reticularis) found in de-
cayed wood. This last, when served raw, is like
rich cream, and I know many whites who are very
fond of the dish, but won’t admit it. Roasted, the
grubs are a cross between pop-corn and shrimps.
Still, many of the above dishes are a circumstance
to the stews you can get in Auckland restaurants.
* * * *
“Obix”: Another emu has been gathered
unto his fathers, from his late residence at Cook
Park, Orange (N.S.W.). Like his partner, who pre-
deceased him some six months ago, his old curiosity
shop was full of marvels. To wit: Four pennies, 6
halfpennies, nine 23 in. nails, 5 marbles, 1 pump con-
nection, 1 umbrella ferule, 1 large key, 1 clock
wheel 2|in. in diameter, 2 collar studs, 1 safety-pin,
2 staples, 3 washers, 3 coat buttons, 24 pieces of
china, and a large pin which was found embedded m
the liver. That settled it.
» * * *
“Buzzard”: No man will ever find his
soul so close against Mother Nature’s kind old
breast as when he feels the exquisite tremor caused
by a tiny wild bird sitting on his finger or hand.
It has happened to me twice on the top of Mount
Pitt (Norfolk Island), and the bird was the wife of
the blue wren. Her shy eyes haunt me still.
“Yalgun”: That Bulletin par concern-
ing the youiig lady who found a mouse in a pie re-
minds me that, during the 1903 drought, while eat-
ing a piece of tinned sausage, my teeth came into
collision with a dog’s tooth. The accuracy of my
diagnosis was vouched for by a butcher and a vet.,
and my deduction that the remainder of the animal
was in the entrfie met with their entire approval. I
have never eaten imported mullock since.
* * * *
“Pick” : A haystack may be a somewhat
tardy bonfire, but if “The Haek”(B. 27/7/’il)wants to
know what conflagration can win the donkey race, it
is a burning sheepfold. At Waterloo station, Monaro
(N.S.W.), an old yard was accidentally fired by a
shepherd trying to burn off barley-grass, and the ac-
cumulated manure smouldered for 18 months before
burning itself out.

MY MORNING NOSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
(With apologies to Grant. Ilervey.)
In the morning I awaken
Feeling very sick and shaken;
And my hat hangs on the gas-jet—smeared with
grease it is and old;
In the dew-time
Comes a blue time
When my headpiece sing's like blazes as my mem-
ories unfold!
Yea, my head is fat and tender
As a bloated moneylender
After I have swung triumphant where the blonde
peroxide glows;
’Tis a sad time,
And a bad time,
W hen the glass reveals the horror of my freshly-
painted Nosel
From my window hot and flustered,
With my Nose all newly-lustred,
I lean out and hear the wild-fowl sing their morning
hymn to me !
From my place there,
With my face there,
I observe the turnips yelping in a nervous minor
key!
But the spud that singeth sweetest
Is a Peach ! ah ! Spud that greetest
Me with Sankey and with Moody while the dawn-lift
upward goes,
Thou art clever!
They can never
Understand who hate hysterics and a brightly-painted
Nose!
Nose Triumphant! Nose of Glory !
Red thou art! (Some might say gory!)
But the Nose that flashes where the Great
Souls are—
Whisky’s dower—
Marks the hour
When the buds blow up like thunder and the boiled
owl flies afar!
Spuds and Turnips! one regretful
Song will make me hot and fretful!
Sing me one of Harry Lauder’s! Play it on the
garden hose !
Now, you nettles !
Bang your petals !
Sing me something sweet and dew lilce—something
that will cool my Nose!
Yet and yet— (This hesitation
Won’t escape your observation!
Something’s very tender’s coming! . . . Right away !
the line is clear!)
She, my star-maid
(Murphy’s barmaid),
Said to me, “Oh, don’t be silly! Do go home now,
there’s a dear!”
Who has loved has got full measure —•
Some there are who spill the treasure —
Others leave quite half a pewter—-that the wand’ring
beerhound knows !
Just a woman,
Vain and human—
And the extra beer she gave me helped to paint my
morning Nose!
Wasted beers of careless rapture
Come no more for me to capture—
Wasted bens! My screaming garden has no more to
say to me!
Though the Rose’s
Mouth uncloses,
Comes from it no timely sermon on the virtue of
Cold Tea!
And the wattle with its banner
Greets me with no gay, “How’s Anna?
Seen young Birdie Goldtop lately? Lily says she’s
down at Joe’s!”
Lo! the tulip
Curls its dewlip !
It won’t even say “How goes it?” when it sights my
morning Nose!
In the morning, lo! I waken
After I’ve been soundly shaken!
Hot my hat hangs on the gas-jet—smeared with
grease it is and old;
But I care not
And I swear not
If the garden rrlls “Hole’s Anna?” and I still retain
some gold !
Yea, it was a time of splendor!
When that barmaid’s up I’ll send her
Word that, like a boa-constrictor, I tracked homeward
to repose;
And the dawn-lift —■
That cold yawn-lift,
Which conveys her to the beer-pump will reveal my
waiting Nose !
N.S.W.
GRANT O. LORD.


OUR RUDE FOREFATHERS.
By listening to Men of Fat.
On Empire Day, 1 fancied that
Old England was the Empire's Hub,
And we the Lioness's Cub;
But now it seems these things are fables;
At least, according to the Cables,
The Lioness is on the spree —
The Hub is on the jamboree !
When us a sugar strike upsets,
We do not need fixed bayonets
We get some arbitrators able
To fix it up around a table.
We do not grab each other's throttles.
We do not hurl brickbats or bottles.
And can it be, despite Bruce Smith,
That British culture is a myth ?
We do not slash or use the boot
T o settle up an old dispute;
And when perchance we disagree
We don’t call out the cavalry.
Are these the tactics that we need
To be of British Bulldog Breed ?
Are these the things that we must dp
T o show that we are British, too ?
When Revolution unafraid
Is building up his barricade,
And Britain's not so safe as we,
Whither is Capital to flee ?
1 would not be so much surprised
If to this country civilised
Old England sends her surplus cash,
In fear the Hub will come to smash.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Adelaide Register roused
the indignation of ex-Under
Sec. Boothby (uncle of nov-
elist Guy) by killing him in
a paragraph last week. The
amende was made next day,
but ruffled feelings were not
altogether smoothed. Long-
ago the Advertiser inserted
a premature obituary of
Sammy Raphael, who in
those days was continually
creating trouble in the Ade-
laide Council. Raphael in-
dignantly expostulated with
editor Jeff Stow, and in
next issue appeared a con-
tradiction beginning: “We
regret that the paragraph
inserted yesterday concern-
ing the death of Mr. Coun-
cillor Raphael was not
true.” The laughter lasted for the rest of
the councillor's life.
About some ancient inhabitants: —
A recent par. concerning the late J. D. Browne,
of Liverpool (N.S.W.), and his connection with the
Marquis of Sligo, has unearthed a shipload of people
who ought to be on drinking terms with the Peerage.
The Liverpool man, who died the other day, aged
82, was the grandson of a man who, coming from
the East India Co.’s service, got one of the earliest
land-grants in N.S.W. That first Browne came to
Sydney from Calcutta with a retinue of nigger ser-
vants ; but he had to send them back-—which shows
that there were dreadful persons (Governors and
such-like) who objected to Ram Bux as an immi-
grant even before the days of the Labor party. (It
happened about 1816.) But though Browne I. had
to do without R. Bux, he put up with the place
himself, and left many Brownes behind him. A
still-living grandson in N. S. Wales is W. J.
Browne, aged 84 ; and F. W. Browne (Wright,
Heaton and Co.) and Spencer Browne (Brisbane
Courier) belong to the fourth generation. The
land granted to Browne I. was in the Illawarra. At
Appin he built a half-way house to stop at when
driving from Sydney, and this place, Oaklands, is
now the property of F. W. Browne. The old place
has a half-mile frontage of oak-trees, planted over
80 years ago.
A. C. Macdonald, founder of the Geo-
graphical Society of Victoria, belongs to a
family of 10 whose ages aggregate 770
years. It is 73 years since a death occurred
in the family.
According to London Opinion, G.-G. Den-
man’s father-in-law, Lord Cowdray, the con-
tractor, was so delighted when the young
man got the job that he straightway doubled
the official screw of £lO,OOO a year.
Webb Adams, who died at Norfolk Is-
land the other dav, was the last child born
on Pitcairn Island in 1850 —the year when
the Bounty mutineers and their descendants
were removed to Norfolk Island.
“The Spout”: Among the “Liberal” sup-
porters on Wade’s platform at Gunnedah, a
few nights before the by-election, were
Messrs. Swindle, Crook and Done. Miss
Representation was,also present, in the dis-
guise of the ex-Premier.
July 24 of next year will be the 50th anni-
versary of the day on which McDouall
Stuart, the first man to cross the continent
in a direct line rrom Adelaide to the Indian
Ocean, planted his flag on the shores of Van
Diemen’s Gulf, and marked a tree. The tree
was, however, completely destroyed by a bush
fire recently, even the roots being burnt; and
the people of Darwin are raising a fund to
put a permanent memorial on the site.
Stuart made his triumphant re-entry into
Adelaide on the day that Howitt’s rescue
party reached the city en route for Mel-
bourne with the remains of Burke and
Wills. The latter’s marked tree at Inna-
mincka is, by the way, decaying rapidly.
R- J. Needham, Chief Inspector of Stock
in S.A., has crossed over, after getting a
very wide Australian experience. He was
in Fiji in the cannibal days, growing cotton
before the British occupation. He was a
pioneer pastoralisti in Western Queensland,
and the leader of a Northern Territory ex-
pedition which established and stocked New-
castle Waters and other stations. But he
did not fancy the Territory’s prospects, and
became a Government official 30 years ago.
Like so many other prominent South Aus-
tralians, Needham was the son of a parson.
Left the Inky Way for good at Dunedin
(M.L.), the other day, Henry E. Muir, of
Otago Daily Times. Muir was mixed up
with printers’ ink from his infancy. Back
in 1873 he was one of four who founded
the Clutlia Leader. Later he leased and
eventually became editor of the Timaru
Evening Mail, and at various subsequent
times edited the Ashburton Mail, Ashburton
Guardian, and Dunedin Globe. His long
press life produced a large hoard of valuable
experience. The pressman’s worry is that
no bank will honor a cheque drawn on that
account.
A case of three-card trickery on board the
Grantala, communicated by wireless to the
Sydney police, broke down on investigation,
the alleged robber satisfying the Law that
lie was only showing his companion the way
it was done. It recalls the horror with
which, long ago, some A.S.N. passengers wit-
nessed a portly Queensland Premier, George
Thorn, collecting half-crowns from a fellow-
traveller by means of the same simple de-
vice. The traveller persisted, but George
won every time, half a crown a try. It was
only after the dupe had left the boat at
Maryborough and the horrified passengers
were scattered that the man who had been
taken down put his hand in his pocket and
found all the half crowns. The trickster
had played still another game of sleight-of-
hand.

Dr. James Wharton Cox, just dead in
Sydney at 52, was the eldest son of Dr. J.
C. Cox, one of the men who made the
a.M.P. Society, and great-grandson of Pay-
master Cox. of the “Rum” Corps, who
superintended the making of the road over
the Blue Mountains.

SMALLGOODSMAN ( to new arrival): “Sausages ,

most of them's dead now."
The Bulletin’s Abo. page lost an inter-
esting contributor the other day, when some
mysterious thing went wrong with the brain-
works of W. S. Dr&ffin, headmaster of the
South Gundagai (N.S.W.) public school, and a
rifle-shot ended a useful life. Draffin (“Billy
Bluegum”) was a stalwart Australian, a
devoted husband and father, an excellent
teacher, and a good friend. Donald Fraser
(“Jimmie-Pannikin”), who was his inspector
for years, says he was “one of the whitest
Out-backers I ever met—a fine fellow of
boundless energy, bracing optimism and tem-
perate habits” ; and his passing-out, at the
muzzle-end of his own rifle, is a grim
puzzle.
George Aldridge, just dead in Adelaide at
04, had seen much of Australia since he
went to the Northern Territory in 1868 with
Goyder’s survey party. He was there when
the mining boom broke out, and made money
at Sandy Creek and Woolwonga. Tom Horn
was with him then; also brother “.Jim” Al-
dridge, well known since as the Richmond
Park breeder of racehorses. Afterwards
George tried auctioneering and brewing and
other things, but “found” himself at last as
a sharebroker; he was president of the Stock
Exchange of Adelaide from 1889 to 1903,
which included the Wild days of both Broken
Hill and Kalgoorlie booms. Aldridge, his for-
mer partner and brother-in-law Theodore
Bruce, and “Jim” Dick made one of Ade-
laide’s best-known group of stout friends.
Death has called for all three of them this
year.
Field-Marshal Onslow provided one of the
humorous items in the N.S.W. Opposition’s
scena of last week, entitled by the dailies
“A House in Chaos; or Chosen by Trick.”
After Speaker Willis had seated himself on
the box seat, the tall, thin “Liberal,” look-
ing very peaked after his soprano solos of
the morning, was listening wretchedly to
Cohen gasping on a question of privilege,
when Laborite Kelly made some remark.
The hero of a hundred tea-fights sprang up
as if he had been bitten by a concealed Boer.
“Mr. Speaker,” he shrilled, “he called me a
blackguard!” A shriek went up that dazed
the unhappy man. One of his own side,
grinning broadly, whispered something in his
ear. and as the Colonel fell back hurriedly,
Kelly was heard explaining that he had said
“graveyard,” not “blackguard.”
Dubbo (N.S.W.) has produced something
of a record—G. H. Taylor, the president of
its Mechanics’ Institute, was the other day
re-elected for bis 38th year of unbroken ser-
vice.

“Ginger” : I enter Dawes, of Koo-wee-rup
(Vic.), for the Champion Sires’ Stakes of
Australia. Dawes’s family-tree already has
37 olive branches. The male parent is a
village settler by occupation, and a follower
of Voliva by choice. North Pakenliam, in
the same part of the country, has three
families that go beyond a score. Koo-wee-
rup, in the blacks’ language, signifies a
basket of blackfish. It may, from present
indications, become a synonym for a cradle
of white fish. And here I recall that it was
on the borders of Koo-wee-rup that Clara
and Annie Snell were born—the dainty pair
who in a few years weighed 35st. and 29st.
respectively.
JUDGE JOSH WILLIAMS
(M.L.), recently insulted
with a Knighthood.
Kitchener, whose sword is generally under-
stood to be one of the main , supports of the
Empiah, made the frightful mistake of ap-
pearing at George’s Buckingham Palace
social in the wrong trousers. He had hardly
finished counting the crowned heads, when
the horrified First Trouser Button in Waiting
tip-toed up to him like an undertaker, and
whispered in his ear that Court etiquette
prescribed buckskin breeches for such occa-
sions, and that he (Kitchener) “must not let
it occur again” ! Kitchener wasn’t mortified;
he was merely so wild that he got away from
the fool-infested palace just as soon as he
could.
WHEN THE SPEAKER TOOK THE CHAIR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It took about an hour’s careful handling to subdue
the storm, but Mr. Willis did it most thoroughly.
He let the House see clearly that he was aware of
the powers he possessed, and was prepared to exer-
cise them.— Sydney paper.
They were howling like hyenas, they were screaming
as they pleased;
They were out to slaughter Willis, and their tongues
with hate were greased;
So they struck and bit like adders, and like jackals
snarled and tore;
For their colleague’s reputation was a bone upon the
floor.
Oh, they snapped and spat their venom, but he never
turned a hair—■
Merely sat and watched and listened with a calm,
reflective air ;
Seemed to find it most instructive, did that silent,
lonely man,
Who, to save us Fifty Thousand, was prepared to
follow Cann !
They were Liberal with insults, for the House was
out of joint,
And the Clerk could not correct them —he could
merely sit and point
At each wild and rowdy member when it came his
turn to swear
That he scorned the name of Willis, and would not
respect the Chair ;
’Twas a Liberal education to the public and the
press,
And it caused the silent “traitor” not a moment of
distress,
For he calmly watched them blither and their
proper duty shirk.
But —there was an alteration when the Speaker got
to work!
He spoke kindly, like a teacher to a class of kniclc-
cred boys ;
And they tried at first to drown him with a lot of
foolish noise ;
But he never lost his temper. Just to hear him was
a treat:
“Yes, the Honorable Member must ( what’s this!)
resume his seat.”
And the Honorable Member like an angry oyster sat,
Looking hot enough to sizzle in his own ill-mannered
fat;
And, when graciously permitted to again address the
Chair,
lie was choking, but respectful, to the grave official
there.
It was beautiful to see them when an hour or so
had gone,
And a dozen rude insurgents had been firmly sat
upon ;
For the atmosphere was placid, and no raucous
howls of hate
Marred the orderly progression of the asinine debate;
They resumed their nicest manners, and that deferen-
tial air,
Which in Parliamentary circles is adopted towards
the Chair.
So, good-luck to Speaker Willis, who would save our
golden quids,
For he whipped and drilled his party like a mob of
silly kids!
N.S.W.

A few years ago various Australian banks—and es-
pecially those of Sydney—found that they had done
so well under the blight of Federation, and the curse
of Freetrade and the horrors of “Labor domina-
tion, that they could pay more than 10 p.c. Two
or three of the more prudent concerns thought that,
rather than go beyond that figure, they should do
very much the same thing for their shareholders by
calling in capital (worth, say, 5 p.c.), and paying
10 p.c. at once upon it. The Union and the ’Asia”
however, had other ideas, probably because their
directors are in London, and won’t understand that
a big dividend is liable to attract hostile attention
in these parts. So, instead of getting in more capi-
tal, and thus strengthening the position of the bank
while incidentally dropping a plum into the mouths
of shareholders, the Union ran its dividend up to 14
p.c. The other plan unquestionably has most to
recommend it; and, as a matter of fact, the Union
has got into a position where it ought to have more
capital. Its capital and visible reserves combined
are £2,892,189; and it owes its depositors and
other creditors £25,404,403. Thus, for every £1
of its °wn funds, it has £8 16s. of the public’s;
and that is a proportion which, if it isn’t actually
excessive, is not far from it. It would be excessive
without any question or qualification whatever but
for two things. (1) The risk which a bank takes in
connection with its creditors’ money depends largely
upon the use to which that money is put. Ob-
viously, if the bank keeps a large proportion in
liquid assets, there isn’t so much risk of the share-
holders funds being called upon to make good the
loss of borrowed money. And this bank’s holding of
liquid assets is very large; it amounts to no less
than 46 p.c. of the total liabilities. (2) Besides
the reserves which a bank shows, it may have some
which it doesn’t show. Thus it may have its assets,
or some of them, written down below real value,
it may have some which it doesn’t value at any-
thing at all. The Union is very likely in that posi-
tion. It has been writing down its premises for
many years, until now they stand at very little
more than half a million; and their market value
is probably twice as much. The Union’s position is,
therefore, better than it looks as to the matter under
discussion; but it is to be hoped that its example
wont encourage any other Australian banking con-
cern to take £9 of deposits, etc., for every £1 of
its own funds. Anyhow, in connection with this
and every other bank, the point that needs to be
driven home is that, other things being equal, the
concern with the largest amount of debt in propor-
tion to capital and reserves is the one that offers
the least protection to the depositor. A big propor-
tion of deposits, etc., means large earnings, or ought
to mean that; but shareholders ought to set against
those large earnings the fact that a big proportion
of deposits also means large risks. When a share-
holder’s £1 has to guarantee the return of a de-
positor’s £9, there doesn’t have to be a very bad
slump in the security in which the £9 is invested
to completely wipe out some of the shareholder’s
little sovereign.
One can’t blame Australian banks, though, for
having piled up their deposits, so fast. The country
has been earning the money, and it has naturally
gone to the banks. This shows 10 years’ changes:—

By February, 1901, the Union had accumulated
£15,249,040 of deposits, after 64 years’ trading;
in the past 10 years it has accumulated just upon
half of that 64 years’ total. Still, when one looks
at the matter with an eye to possible future trouble,
it isn’t the deposits that count so much as the ad-
vances ; and it will be seen with satisfaction that
the Union has not increased its advances at anything
like the rate at which the deposits have grown.

Advances have moved very rapidly. They may be
quite right; on the other hand, it is highly pro-
bable that most securities are now at an inflated
value. The earnings are naturally very solid. On
capital and reserves combined they were at the rate
of £9 9s. 2d. p.c. p.a. for the last half-year. But,
as before mentioned, that high return is partly due
to the big proportion of deposits and other debt,
which means risk for the shareholders.

The Chillagoe Co., which owns mines and
railways in North Queensland: —

This has been one of the huge failures of North
Queensland —a monument of miscalculation (de-
liberate or otherwise), blundering, booming, and re-
constructing. Fortunately that doesn’t end it. Of
late years the history has been mainly one of enter-
prise and strenuous endeavor to pull the wreck off
the rocks. Most of the people who were connected
with the concern in its early days ought to have
been stone-broke long ago. Somehow or other a
good many of them seem fairly affluent still. Pro-
bab!y they knew their way about the share market.
This, m brief, is the record of the co.
Chillagoe Proprietary.—Formed in 1893 or there-
abouts. Capital, £960,000, in £2OO shares.
Chillagoe Railway and Mines.—lB9B. Money
wanted. Shares split up; old co. got 480,000 for
nix ( £2 shares) and another 120,000 for £l.
Ditto, first reconstruction.—lB99. More money
wanted. Shares reduced to £1 in order to encourage
a debenture issue (£400,000).
New Chillagoe Railway and Mines.—l9o2. More
money wanted. Share for share issued, but the new
shares made liable for 3s. Debenture issue by this
time, £498,300 at 6 p.c. J
Chillagoe Co., Ltd.—l9os. More money wanted.
Numerous shares had been forfeited, but 688,983
were left. These offered to old shareholders at Is.,
with Is. 6d. liability, on payment of which they
would be fully paid to 10s. Another 1017 shares
offered for subscription; and 10,000 given to the
Tolhurst-lveats people for bullocking the thing
through. Total shares, 700,000 of 10s. Things also
rearranged with debenture-holders.
Ditto.—l9o6. More money wanted. Debenture-
holders agreed to put up some more, provided the
shareholders put up another £150,000, in 300,000
shares. Under this arrangement money has been
dribbling in, so that no further reconstruction has
been necessary. The present paid capital is
£452,857. There are 1,000,000 shares, of which
15,000 are fully paid to 10s. ; most of the others
are liable for another Is. After that rearrangement
was made the co. decided to push its railway on to
the Etheridge, in order mainlv to get ore to feed its
works. In this big undertaking it had the help of
the Government, which undertakes to buy the line
at some time or other (probably it will be pretty
soon), and meantime guarantees 21 p.c. interest on
the certified outlay. The railway (the Etheridge
extension) stands in the books at £469,927 (cost),
of which the holders of special debentures have
provided £225,000. The ordinary debentures total
£573,300, and there are other liabilities amounting
to £86,544. 6
The co.’s great trouble was that it spent a heap
of money on a railway to serve mines which turned
out very poorly. That hit the co. in both eyes.
First the co. got nothing out of the mines, and then
it was burdened with interest on a railway which
had little work to do; for the other mines in the
district failed as badly as those belonging to the
Chillagoe Co. They were not and are not duffers
by any means ; but to spend a vast sum on a railway
to serve them was like buying an elephant to shift
a packet of pins. And the shocking miscalculation
over the mines led to a shocking miscalculation over
the reduction works. Hence the struggle in recent
years to find new mines, or to reach old ones in re-
mote districts, so that the railway and works may
have a better chance to earn a living.
The co. has now these strings to its bow: (1) Its
own Chillagoe mines and reduction works; (2) the
Chillagoe line, connecting with the Government
(Cairns) railway at Mareeba; (3) the Etheridge
line; (4) various newly-acquired mines on the
Etheridge; (5) wharf and other odds and ends.
There are some possibilities also in the Mount Mulli-
gan coal seams, 30 miles away from the line; but
these may be reckoned in with the reduction works.
The Chillagoe railway is the most cheerful item
among the assets at present. This shows its more
recent history:—

The mines and reduction works, apart from inci-
dentals (telephones, live stock, stores, sidings, etc.),
represent £418,818 of the co.’s capital and deben-
tures ; and, though last year’s results were unusually
good, even then the return was only about 4f p.c.,
whereas the debenture debt is 5 p.c. And, out of the
£21,584 they earned, depreciation has to be written
off. Probably 4f p.c. isn’t enough to write off ; in which
case the net return from this department was nil,
or less than that. But still the mines and works
have to be kept going, or the railway wouldn’t be
fed.
The future depends very largely on the develop-
ment of the mines in the Etheridge district; and
there is still much uncertainty about them. At pre-
sent there is not much promise of anything very
large. Many from small to medium mines are, how-
ever, opening up; and the co. looks to have bread
and butter assured for its debenture-holders, though
steady meals for the shareholders are still uncertain
in the extreme. Even an occasional snack isn’t yet
visible on the horizon.

When the Government decided to continue the North
Shore tram service from the Spit to Manly, there
were some people who wondered how hard this ferry
co. was going to be hit. The tram was running for the
New Year traffic, so this co.’s balance-sheet, just
presented, shows the effect of the first half-year’s
competition. The co.’s flags were at half-mast the other
day, but it probably wasn’t for the results shown in
the accounts. The tram certainly is often crowded ;
but it is a poor little thing, comparatively speaking - ,
?i S tle Dme-table is so grotesquely misarranged
that the cars may land a passenger from Milson’s
Point to Manly in 40 minutes, or may dawdle him
along for two hours, the ferry co. continues to carry at
least 50 passengers for every one that goes the other
way. And the proportion will probably never get
much closer than that. The co. has been an amaz-
ing success. A few years ago Sydney started to
discover Manly, and Manly has now become a
habit. At any time Ma’s capital loves out-door
pleasure. During the past five or six years of
phenomena l prosperity the city has been able to
gratify its love to an unusual extent, and this co
has coined money therefrom. When the Manly boom
started this was the co.’s position:—
Capital, 20,000 shares at £2 19s. each
paid to 255. . ’ £25,000
Debentures (terminable) . . . . 20 000
Interminable debentures (issued to the
old Manly Co-op. Co.) .. .. 8,745
In 1906 the terminable debentures were paid off
and the others converted into shares at 100 p.c. pre-
The new capital was thus £25,000,’ plus
£4372 _ £29,372. (A few of the shares were,
however, not taken up.) Next year the stock was
watered to the extent of £2 for every £1 of paid
capital. So the capital became £88,005. Within
two years £17,601 more profit (mostly unacknow-
ledged) was capitalised, so the capital became
£105,606 —
Capital actually paid .. .. .. £29 335
Ditto, paid out of profits .. .. 762 71
Total £105,606
Thus the 13J p.c. div. is really about 45 p.c.! And,
as the table shows, the dividend absorbs only a very
small part of the profit.
Of course the co. doesn’t confess to anything like
the profits shown in the table. It is so very modest
that its Profit and Loss account says: “Net profit
after providing for depreciation, etc., £7018,” and’
that “etc.” covers a multitude of grabs. This sets
out how the profit shown in the table is arrived at:—
Added to Public Risk Reserve .. .. £1627
Steamers’ Insurance Fund .. .. *263
Reserve Fund .. .. .. ..’ 15 000
Plant Renewal Fund .. .. .’569
Alleged Net Profit .. .. * 7,018
Total (Profit as per table).. .. £24,477
Happy is the co. whose shareholders actually put up
only about £30,000, and which can yet drop £15,000
into its formal Reserve Fund and pretend not to
notice it! How long this fatness is going to last
this paper doesn’t know. Some day, perhaps, a Gov-
ernment will tell this co. that it will have to either
bring its fares down, or stand the racket. of State
competition. Some day also the present good times,
when a run to Manly is neither here nor there, will
come to an end, more or less. And some day, too,
the co. may be hit by a disaster which may cost it
all its assets in claims for compensation. Against
the last contingency it has a poor little public
risk reserve of £6OOO. On the other hand, Manly is
Manly, and there is only one of it; and Sydney is
going to grow, and it isn’t at all likely that it will
change its love of out-door pleasuring.

The Electric Light and Power Supply Cor-
poration, Ltd. (Sydney), which began business
under a contract with the Balmain suburban coun-
cil, and started with a deficit of £9876, represent-
ing Parliamentary rights and other preliminary
exes.:—

Further goodwill items added, £1346 .. 12,226

Dec., 1910 .. 1104 £530 11,652
June, 1911 .. 1406 5 p.c. = £1075 531 11,852
Three or four years back the Sydney City Council,
which had established electric light and power works,
began to eat up the electric light cos. within the
city; and some of the people interested in one or
more of those concerns looked elsewhere for an open-
ing of the same kind. An arrangement was made
with the Balmain (suburban) municipality, a bill
was put through, this co. was formed, and works
were established. Later on it made a 57 years’
agreement with the Newtown Council; and during
the past half-year another with Petersham. To get
the extra capital required for the Newtown exten-
sion, debentures for £25,000 were issued; now, to
provide for the further extension, arrangements
have been made with the bank for an advance up to
£25,000 secured by a debenture “issued subject to
the same terms and conditions as the first issue of
debentures, except as to interest, which is to be at
the rate current from time to time upon
secured overdrafts.” That will make the capital
£50,000, and the debenture debt £50,000.
It is too early yet to say what profit the co. is going
to make, as it is so far really only fighting for busi-
ness, and the Australian (Sydney) Gas Co. isn’t a
paltry competitor ; but as the latter co. pays a 15 p.c.
div.. it plainly doesn't cut prices to the bone. There is
however, the Sydney municipal electric supply to
be considered, and some at least of the agreements
made by this co. provide for treatment as good in
some respects as that civic enterprise offers. Anything
in the shape of very fat dividends therefore seems
improbable, inasmuch as the city supply isn’t out
after dividends. As the line or two on top of the
table indicate, the co. has to provide dividend on a
quantity of “water”; in fact, the present amount
of the deficit is exactly the total amount of the capi-
tal and loans unrepresented by tangible assets. Part of
this “water” is, however, represented by ordinary
shares. The whole position as to capital is—De-
bentures £25,000 (and another . £25,000 arranged
for), to take the first bite of profits; £43,000 in
pref. shares, entitled to the second bite; and £7OOO
in ordinaries, which come in last. Pending the issue
of the second lot of debentures, the co., on June 30,
had debts (mainly overdraft) totalling £9450.

This co. owns and works a line which runs up in a
rather tired way into western Riverina from the
Victorian railway terminus at Moama. There isn’t
a great deal for it to do, because most of the coun-
try is held in big sheep-runs, and the State Frights
Government won’t build lines to feed this one, since
that would mean feeding the Victorian railways and
Melbourne. In fact, instead of feeding this line
and getting the stock and other produce to the
nearest big market and port, the N.S.W. railways
attract freight which otherwise might go to this line
by offering specially low rates to Sydney. It is pos-
sible, though, that that form of madness will be
pretty soon stamped out, more or less. For the
reasons mentioned, this co. hasn’t had a gaudy
time; but it has accepted the situation, and paid
only a very small div. when things were bad. Con-
sequently it has put itself into a pretty strong
financial position. In 10 years things have moved
this way:—

Liabilities have thus been reduced by just upon

of the landed property before mentioned), while
cash and book debts have been added to a trifle.
Meantime a good deal has certainly been spent on
plant. The amount written off out of profits as a
set-off against wear and tear is not disclosed, and a
great deal depends on that; but, judging by the
recent valuations, the writing down has been more
than adequate. In fact, the surplus shown over
previous book values represents profits which were
written off in excess of the legitimate demands of
the case. There can, then, be no question about the
excellent record of the co, during the past five years;
and, though the next five years need not necessarily
be anything like the past live, the co. will certainly
face poor times with a very much better chance of
maintaining moderate dividends.

wouldn’t consciously affect their published accounts,
it might quite conceivably induce *them to take a
rather more than usually gloomy view of things in

That looks as though the co. hasp’t held its own in

words, that the dividends have been to a small extent

one would have to be sure that mining and fixed

a trifle. For while it has added only £11,365 to its
non-liquid assets, its surplus of liquid assets has been
reduced by £21,487.

Mining-engineer Danvers Power dissents
from a recent par. (B. 24/8/’ll): Great Cobar’s
concentration and flotation plants will not cost any-
thing like £50,000. The former is being built on
foundations already existing, and most of the
material is already on the ground. Fresh construc-
tion work, with the exception of the concentration
plant, is not being started because there is no need for
any, the heavy construction of the past year being
practically completed. The reduction in the per-
sonnel of the staff is due to the same reason; the
work for which they were engaged is now finished.
The furnaces are doing excellent work, which goes
to show that, given a fair chance, they can do what
the management claims, for when fully supplied
with ore they have exceeded expectations. The
mines have a large quantity of payable ore de-
veloped, that of the different mines being blended
in the most economical manner for smelting, the ex-
tractable copper being reduced to allow of more gold
ore being treated, the latter metal not being subject
to market fluctuations. The institution of picking
belts and bedding bin has made it possible to get
the greatest use from the iron excess of the ore. The
Cobar Gold Mines property supplies 250 tons a day;
there are 344,000 tons, worth 1.3 p.c. copper and
Bdwt. gold per ton, developed, so there is no urgent
need io push forward with further development at
present. Under the circumstances, would it pay to
sink another main shaft in hard rock for the sake of
250 tons a day? The development work in all the
mines is well in advance of the present and future
requirements, and it is interesting to note that the
central ore body in the Great Cobar mine has the
same area and values at No. 11 level as at No. 10
level. I cannot compare the northern and southern
ore bodies, as they have not yet been sufficiently

Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
14 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things remarks (p. 1465) : “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at Breck-
enridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000 have been
sold. This machine has revolutionised ore-dressing,
and as such is a phenomenal success. The original
form, and forerunner of all modern shaking tables
with longitudinal riffles . . has been greatly improv-
ed.” And further on (p. 1478) remarks: “One feature
of the Wilfley table to which especial attention should
be called is its ability to separate sands from slimes
while making the ordinary concentration. This sep-
aration is more complete and thorough than can be
made by any classifier, and should be taken advant-
age of in designing mills.” In the extensive addi-
tions just made to the Golden Horseshoe Mine (W.A.)
a large number of Wilfley Concentrators have been
installed. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole manufac-

Mount Boppy gold mine (N.S.W.) is
about to start the erection of a Moore vacuum slime
plant, similar to the one at the Occidental. A. J.
Chappie (formerly metallurgist to the latter co.),
and agent for the Moore people, is to keep his eye
on the new plant. Negus, the co.’s superintendent,
has hopes, too, of installing a suction gas plant to
supply power to the whole mine and works. One
thing, there is no shortage of cash here. Boppy is
easily the most profitable gold mine in N. YVales.
Cobar Copper, Ltd. (formerly Young
Australian and Mount Pleasant), is getting close to
the end of t three months’ partial labor suspen-
sion ; three o. four tributors are working on the
claim. It’s about time these people were served
with a little of the move on or get out medicine
that was administered at Crowl Creek.
* * * *
No Broken Hill mine has lately shown
such changes as the British. For years it has been
reckoned to have large possibilities, not only on
account of its area, but also because of its position,
inasmuch as it is the connecting link between the B.
H. Prop, and Block 14 on the south, and the Junction,
Junction North and North group to the north. But
somehow it hasn’t usually shown much energy, and
has had the habit of frequently knocking off work to
sit down and look at things. The recent find, which
has given the co. a new mine, was really touched in
1908, when the co. got on to the edge of the ore.
It was not until early this year, however, that the
thing was really stabbed. Thomson shaft was put
down to 900 ft., and a crosscut at that depth showed
35ft. of very high grade—just about as good as any
big sulphide body on the Barrier. At various
points, right up ’ to 300 ft., this body has been
proved; at 500 ft. it seems to be 137 ft. wide, though
that figure is arrived at only by boring. At 800 ft.
a second body was found, a few feet east of the main
body; this second body is 22ft. wide, and high
grade. From the 300 ft. to the 900 ft., in fact, the
grade is excellent, as far as the little investigation
has shown. The length of the shoot cannot yet be
said for certain. At the 600 ft. level the drills prove
it to be 500 ft. (it is at this point 95ft. wide) ; but
that may be considerably beyond the average. Only
a very rough estimate can be made of the reserves;
but it seems fairly safe to say that there are over
I, tons. It will be interesting now to watch de-
velopments in the top levels, for it is at least possible
that, above the sulphide zone, there may be a valu-
able oxidised ore body. In the old days the British
turned out very fine carbonates; and, though the ore
may be irregular near the surface, something very
well worth having may be found there.. The co. has
a mill capable of doing (three shifts) about 4000
tons a. week; but it seems pretty certain that the
mine will call for something further than that.

* Old Residues. t Mostly tributors’ ore.
Westralia is about to incorporate the div-
idend duty and income tax Acts into one statute,
and pattern it somewhat on the lines of the Queens-
land law. Mining and other cos., which now part
up 5 p.c. on dividends, will continue to do so;
and provision will be made to reach a number of large
profit-earning business concerns which hitherto have
dodged the tax.
* * * *
Associated Gold Mines, Ltd., lias just
ended another profitless year; and, although the
Board says otherwise, there isn’t much chance of a
distribution during the current 12 months. The
working accounts show a surplus of £22,897, less
£3476 for development and £3923 for diamond-
drilling. Cash resources amount to £131,633, and
still consist mainly of Consols and Transvaal Gov-
ernment stock. What with some fairly good develop-
ments between the 12 and 14 levels, and the finding
of one or two good makes of ore above the No. 2
and below the No. 4 levels, the appearance of the
property has improved during the past few months ;
but unless something a good deal better than has
already been exposed is discovered in the bottom,
the mine can’t be a stayer.
* * * *
Oroya Links (W.A.) wrote off £5568 for
depreciation and £5185 for mine development last
year, and after paying the cost of mining operations
had £23,589 left. From this the previous year’s
debit balance of £8744, plus the £14,406 that
went to pay an unwise dividend, was deducted,
leaving £439 to be carried forward. Apart from
the remaining ore in the derelict Oroya-Brown Hill
leases, which the general managers say they can’t
estimate either as to quantity or value, the reserves
are computed at 82,509 tons, worth 28.475. per ton.
Both in the Eclipse and Golden Eagle leases, which
now form the backbone of the nroperty, the No. 4
level is stated to have disclosed a great length of
payable ore, and the Board is advised that when
the shaft, now being sunk, is put down to 750 ft.,
the reserves should be considerably increased, and
the remodelled mill kept running at its full capacity
on sulphide ore. Meanwhile the co. is barely
making ends meet, and the liquid assets, which
have now dwindled to £42,000, are in danger of
being further encroached on.
* * * *
Gwalia South (W.A.) has taken up
Oroya Links’ turned-down option on the Gimlet at
Ora Banda, and has eight months to decide whether
it will hold on to it or not. The purchase price is
fixed at £28,000, and the conditions of the option
are that the option-holder shall sink the shaft to
400 ft., and drive two levels the full length of the
shoot.
* * * *
Whim Well (W.A.) reports cheerfully,
and says that Dlenty of rich ore is exposed in the
bottom of the present mine workings. Last quar-
ter’s output was 496 tons fine copper, the biggest
yet obtained. Most of the ore is still sent to Euro-
pean smelters; but when the much-talked of new
plant starts early next year, everything is to be
treated on the spot, and a profit of £50,000 per
annum is reckoned on. The Bulletin has no way
of checking the prophecy.
The last will probably soon be heard of
the Westralia Waihi Co. Nothing worth having in the
shape of a new property can apparently be found in
the West, and even if one is found, the co. hasn’t
the money to either buy or develop it. It may
by some subtle means induce shareholders to fork
out a bit more, but in view of the way scrip-owners
were misled, first over the value of the Davyhurst
property and then over the awful failure which
somehow the co. became saddled with at Norseman,
it is very doubtful whether they will stump up any
more cash. At the end of the half-year just closed,
the principal assets were cash £253, stores £231,
sundry debtors £126; but against these credits
were sundry creditors, with accounts aggregating
£224.
* * * *
Sirdar (W.A.) had its half-yearly roll-up
the other day, when the chairman announced that
since the co.’s inception in July, 1908, £17,778
had. been split up among employees and trades-
people. The value of the bullion won was £20,833,
and, although no dividends had been paid, share-
holders were earnestly invited to comfort themselves
with the reflection that a co. which had done such
a good turn to others was deserving of support, be-
sides being a credit to the district where it carried
on business. The accounts showed a credit balance
of a few hundred pounds, while the manager’s re-
port was of the hopeful variety.
* * * *
Peak Hill (W.A.) has given it up, and
its la§t remains are to be auctioned on September 14.
For some time past the co. has been busy gouging
out and treating everything in sight, and when it
couldn’t find anything else to put through the treat-
ment plant, it simply let the mine drown, and
scooted. The bond and debenture holders then stepped
in, and are now trying to realise on the leavings,
which, needless to say, are not much.
Hampton Uruguay, Ltd., which dabbles
in mines, estates, live-stock and other risks, was
thinking, when the last mail left London, of forming
a separate co. to give the Lady Miller at Norseman
another trial. Towards the end of 1908 this show
was examined by A. E. Thomas, who reported that
20,000 tons of 355. ore were available for stoping,
without taking into account a lot of lower-grade
stuff above the No. 1 level, which he thought could
be worked at a profit. Thomas’s opinion confirmed
that of the then manager; but, although a proposal
to form a new co. to dig out this wealth was backed
up by both of them, the Board was not “having
any” at the time.
* * * *
P. W. Tewksbury won’t cease worrying
Peter Mcßride over the latter’s regulations declaring
that the Bright (Vic.) dredges shall re-soil the
country they chew; whereat Tewksbury gets no sym-
pathy worth mentioning from his fellow dredge-
masters. What Tewksbury would like to do would
be to aeroplane himself and his five dredges to
Araluen, or the Northern Territory—anywhere out of
the sight of Peter and his regulations. He might
growl less if he were doing better ; and he would do
better if he met Peter without the display of the
mailed fist. As it is, his big co. has only had a
surplus of profit of I.Bd. per cubic yard, and that
means the sick feeling that comes to most mining
men when they are spelled from the divvy list for a
while. Bright doesn’t, however, want to lose its
“Tewks.” He has been a generous employer, and
hates to turn hands adrift; yet he will flit if he can
manage it. Then, perhaps, someone more complaisant
will hail Peter with deference, and instal the soiler,
and get on nicely.
*' * * *
The old Egerton and Black Horse country
(Vic.) never loses its attraction, but none of the
prospecting shows have alighted on the gold such as
made Martin Loughlen, Bailey and others of the old
days. A try was made to get Bewick, Moreing and
Go. to engineer the Black Horse, and recently J.
Bull was asked direct to put up some cash. Now
comes news that the South Egerton has got good
stone, but so much is being made of its being on the
continuation of the “famous” Rose reef that every-
one shies off. This does not say that the district
ought to be left alone, but, as the old Black Horse
and Egerton haven’t enough vitality in them to
track an elephant, a new course must be shaped by
the prospectors of to-day.
A couple of Melbourne cos. begin to dig at
the Lake Boga (Vic.) gypsum. These deposits are
close to rail, and were discovered 20 years ago by
one Maloney. The stuff occurs in two forms—the
crystal, which is to be used for plaster of, Paris
manufacture, and the earthy, which forms a manure
basis.
The Ten Mile district, up Woods Point
way (Vic.), shows more derelict mines than empty
beer bottles along the hill sides. One Melbourne-
owned claim, the Champion, survives to tell a mild
tale of divs. It has a big face of stone, with water
to give cheap power for the greater part of the year,
so that if it has the gilt there is every chance of
turning out profits. The average for the past half-
year is 7dwt., which, under such conditions, should,
unless the gold is exceedingly patchy, enable rattling
good money to be won. If there is, as is testified to,
an Bft. reef in one level and a 15ft. reef in another,
the need of the claim is a big battery of modern
type. To pay £l5OO in dividends, and then only
have £l6O to go with, is not mining at all. The
presence of a break, or a drop in the value of the
stone, and troubles start at once. The £l5OO ought
to have gone into the mine or into plant—preferably
the former, for with the lode proved big, even if it
were worth only 4dwt., the Board would have no
difficulty in getting any reasonable capital it might
desire for the claim. As it is, no one outside is
trustful about the future, because there is no cer-
tainty that dividends won’t stop *at any time.*
Cassilis, Gippsland, is like the verdant
debutante—it always thinks the newest man the best.
Andrews, Coote, Nicholas, Dickson, Drummond —all
hang among the portraits of the bosses of the past;
but their monuments have to be searched for in the
scrap-heap. The present pebble is Lilburne, formerly
of the South German ; and he isn’t abusing his pre-
decessors, thank goodness! It looks as if he was
licking things into shipshape. As he had to start on
low-grade dirt, he had less room to turn in than
some of the others. When he talks of 7dwt. cover-
ing costs, he is putting up a halo for himself, be-
cause if he can work on that basis, he has the chance
to convert the Cassilis into a central establishment
for a mining field which has always wanted cheap
costs and good brains to make it a big producer.
Lilburne tells a tale of the ore at the bottom level
widening and improving in values. Let him be
warned against emulating the great prophets, other-
wise he will go the way of men before him. Cassias
shoots of ore cut in and cut out like a dog at a fair.
The complex mineral contents of the lode so tar
have been persistent; but experience in Gippsland
warns the cautious man to take nothing for granted,
and to talk only of what’s right under the point of
the pick. Lilburne’s programme is sink, sink, sink ;
and if he can get the Board to keep pace with him,
and if his slimes scheme pans out successfully,
sundry share scoops will no doubt soothe chairman
Saddler. #
The other day The Bulletin cast an eye
to Glen Wills (Vic.), to see what one of Alec Pea-
cock’s cos. was doing there. Peacock has had under
his gaudy wing the Maude and Homeward. Bound,
known generally as Maudy. Once upon a time the
claim was worked with the shareholders taking the
risk like men. Then the tributor was brought in,
and things went on in that way till Peter Mcßride
went on the trail of the man who was content to
let the tributor save his pocket. Someone then
asked for the transfer of Maudy to his camp, and got
the beauty. Now there is to be a clearing-up sale,
with 16,214 shares to cut up the proceeds. Those
in the first float did not do badly, for the total cash
put up was only £3295, while the gold won was
£90,375. Of that £39,560 went in wages, and the
shareholders got £10,400 in divs. ; the payments to
the tributor on account of royalty were £25,000,
showing that he has been the backbone of the mine
for years past. #
The mines of the Mammoth Copper Co.
(Q.) offer further encouragement. The Keppoch has
had its winze from the Soft, level deepened a further
22ft., and “the ore has increased to 2ft., and assays
8 p.c. copper and sdwt. gold.” In the Allan the
prospects are also healthy, while various shafts on
the outcrop of the Noonday are promising. No. 1
shows a sft. lode, and No. 2 4ft., with copper
verging on 9 p.c. But good stuff is needed, seeing
that it has to be taken by traction engine 25 miles
to Mungana, en route for the furnaces at Chillagoe.
* * * *
Many Peaks, which supplies Mount Mor-
gan with iron flux, held its annual meeting the
other day, and told itself that its reserves are
bigger than ever. Since Mount Morgan started to
do business with it, in 1907, the young co. has re-
ceived £12,500 as royalty on 100,000 tons, at 2s. 6d.
per ton. Of this total, 36,322 tons are not yet
delivered, so it may be four or five months before
royalty will begin to flow in again. All the uncalled
capital has been asked in; and when the lot has
been paid up the co.’s shares (2041 of £1) will be
cut up into 81,640, nominally of sixpence each. In
the directors’ report nothing is said of a shipment or
two of stuff sent to Port Kembla smelters, though
that is a point of some interest. The bank credit
balance was a modest £460 at the end of the co.’s
year; but when there is a bit to cut up the share-
holders get it —the last little lot was 30s. per share.
* * * *
The Welcome, near Charters Towers,
has been attracting the eye of the speculator. For-
merly it was worked by its owner in a small way,
but five well-known Towers mining men took a hand
recently. A lot of 36 tons —shovelled from the mul-
lock heap, it is alleged—returned 28s. 6d. a ton;
and a small picked four-ton lot broken from below
gave soz. of- gold, with £4 10s. for the sands. The
Towers is interested, and wants to know what will
happen with depth. #
Oaks Rush (Q-). which is striking a good
many difficulties in connection with its plant, is in-
creasing its capital to £12,000 by the issue of
60,000 new 2s. shares. Present scrip-holders have a
prior right to take this lot.
Charters Towers has an idea that if it
could get cheap fuel from Blair Athol it would boom
again, so it has started to pull the political strings
in order to get a railway connection with Clermont,
on the Central line. And the Towers is largely run
by men who know a good deal about pulling politi-
cal strings.
* * * *
Speculation is slack at Charters Towers
because deep-level prospects are not up to the mark.
So far the Brilliant Block has failed to pick up the
Day Dawn reef. When the drive was extended from
the Freehold boundary into the Brilliant lease, there
was a blank, and, generally speaking, prospects were
in and out; but some good stone was struck in a
winze below the level (1613 ft.), work ceasing when
the winze was down 100 ft. To add to the uncer-
tainty, the make of stone was dipping at a much
steeper angle than the Day Dawn generally does,
which of course spoiled the yarn about “all the
characteristics of the Day Dawn” being in evidence.
* * * *
Swedenborg, Charters Towers, is an out-
sider in same locality as the Black Jack and Stock-
holm. A trial crushing of stone from this old show,
which was abandoned 20 years since, is to be sent
to the mill. Some of the stone on view in the
Towers is heavily mineralised. The original shaft
was sunk 700 ft. on the underlay of the reef.
* * * *
After being left alone for half a century a
coal mine at Mogil Mogil Creek, near Brisbane, has
been re-prospected with encouraging results. In the
long ago only the outcrop was rooted, and the coal
was of poor quality. This time, however, a shaft
has been sunk, and coal of good quality struck.
* * * *
Up north it is said that Mount Elliott has
secured an option over the Limestone mine, which
has been much in evidence lately as a producer of
rich ore. The latest report is that the shaft at 100 ft.
disclosed a strong body of stone; 130 tons raised in
the course of sinking were good enough to ship.
This show is 15 miles from Selwyn (Mount Elliott
township). Other options are also reported, includ-
ing the Labor Victory.
* * * *
In spite of the financial leeway that is
sure to be disclosed by Great Fitzroy’s half-
yearly report, the market jumped at the first sign of
a profit. But shareholders can’t afford to lose sight
of the fact that debenture interest takes a big share
of any good things that come along.
North Lyell is not shaping too well, so the
old footnote appears at the bottom of Sticht’s monthly
statement: “Blister copper output temporarily
affected by lower output from some of the stopes.
This isn’t inspiring, nor is it altogether candid, and
it is just as well that Bowes Kelly and Joe Syme
should be told the fact plainly and squarely. They
get £lOOO a year each, and it is up to them to
define just what “temporarily” means. If they
don’t know they had better bustle Sticlit up to give
the information. What is more, they owe it to the
shareholders to say what stopes are poor. In June
the output was 647 tons of copper. Now it is 511
tons. A shrinkage of 20 p.c. cannot be dismissed
lightly, and if Joe Syme were running the Age nowa-
days he would hit out. If it is the bottom level,
as is suspected, that is poor, there is more than one
reason why the cards should be laid on the^table.
Though it spent £4178 last half-year,
mostly on the one job, Lyell Blocks is making very
slow progress with its shaft, which is down about
750 ft. in a mixture of schist and hard conglomerate.
There is a heap to do yet before a prospecting crosscut
can be started in search of the North Lyell ore body.
The mineral seen in some of the sinking is, however,
considered a “good indication” —a half-brother to
the good intention which is used in one place for
paving-stones. Besides having to battle with very
hard rock, the co. occasionally meets a slippery head,
which further retards progress.
* * * *
The Mines Assets Purchase Co. has been
formed in London by Alexander Hill and Stewart,
mining engineers and metallurgists to the Lyell Com-
stock, Oonah, Queensland Copper Freeholds, Queens-
land Exploration, and other concerns. The idea is
to buv the plant and machinery of “bung’ mines,
and sell them to young, new, lusty concerns, full ot
ioie de vivre, and with plenty of cash at bankers.
The co. has a paid capital of £20,000. It s a good
scheme, and there’s money in it for people who
understand the second-hand machinery business.
Earl of Ranfurly has been talking glow-
ingly as chairman of the New Guinea Development
Co. Ex-Speaker Cowley, of Queensland, is local
director. The co. has 100,000 acres of plantation
land, and paid a shocking price for it, considering
what ex-Premier and book-agent Jenkins, of S’Aus-
tralia, got it at. The co. is digging plantations for
rubber and copra, and establishing trading stations
along the coast, and talks of harnessing the Rona
Falls on the Leloki River for producing power for
the “copper mines.” The “copper mines” of the
Astrolabe plateau are mostly oxidised ores occurring
in flat beds, and are a long way off requiring any
power worth mentioning.

Nobody realises belter than
a hospital nurse the benefit of a
freshly made cup of tea. She
has to be always on the alert,
and from the time she goes on
duty till she comes off it’s one
constant strain—and if she is
in charge of a big ward she is
kept on her feet so much that
sometimes she feels just ready
to drop.
I fmd a cup of "Robur” tea
before I go on duty, and another
after I come off keeps me right
—I prefer the "Robur” to any

because I’ve found it most
refreshing but there, you’ll know
