VERY OLD COGNAC.
OTARD BRANDY
1875, 1800, 1848, 1820 VINTAGES.
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, August 28, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
Melba, in her lovely nest at Rose Bay, with
an untrained feathered chorus addressing the
sunset in song from a giant Moreton Bay fig
opposite her hall door, is in joyous mood.
The Woman who has Achieved, she flirts
with Time, and snaps her fingers at snarls.
“But not always,” she says. “It hurts some-
times, when I’m working hard, to hear the
iies that are told about me.”
Impulsively, Melba pushes away a teacup,
and puts her hand on the table. “Here we
are, all together, like a lot of tiny black ants
struggling over the flat surface Then one,
more persevering, more gifted, perhaps, be-
gins to rise just a little above the others.
What happens? There is a rush to try to
drag that one down. Listen to me —nothing
is so much hated as Success! On the night
of my debut in Brussels, my husband, who
was not a goed-liumored man, heard a lady in
the next box say to the man with her,
‘Melba’s debut:? Nonsense! I heard her
hissed in Spain 10 years ago!’ My husband
went immediately to the box and spoke to
the man. ‘ls that lady your wife? Yes?
Well, the lady on the stage is my wife. This
is her debut in Europe, and, if you don’t
make your wife apologise for the lie I over-
heard, I’ll punch your head now.’ The
apology was made, and that slander went no
further. But you can’t often catch a lie in
the throat like that.”
Edging away from the prickly path of
Publicity, Melba talked gaily of Coombe Cot-
tage. “The surrounding country is beautiful,
and I love the place. My son is going to
farm 500 acres ; 75 acres with the cottage—
really a cottage, though it has a number of
rooms —I am going to reserve for myself.
Already the farm is in working order, and I
am making £5O a month out of the dairy
herd. I believe in the best—there are sev-
eral breeds, some Jerseys, but all the very
best milking strains. Coombe Cottage is a
great delight to me. I will spend some
months in each year there, and I have pre-
served its cottage character by furnishing it
simply—yet I think in an artistic manner.
Australian wood is used throughout, and
chintz draperies—water-colors on the walls,
and a few of my art treasures, but only those
that will harmonise with a country cottage.
I will keep on my London house, and won’t
move the collection of pictures, etc., away
from it.”
A flash of sunset from the watery west
made a broken ladder of gilt steps across the
harbor. Melba, in her Fernleigh nest,
watched the clearing of the storm in an
ecstasy over the enchanting scenic trimmings
of her present eyrie. “Let me tell you an-
other story to illustrate the extraordinary lies
that have pursued me right through my musi-
cal career. Nothing is too strange to be said
of me. In London I gave a friend, a Mrs.
Williams, a magpie, which, as a joke, she
called ‘Melba.’ One day she said to a visi-
tor : ‘Melba’ is getting very troublesome to
everyone. She has taken to eating mice, and
they make her very sick.’ The lady said,
‘How odd !’—nothing more. Will you believe
it, the story got about that I had taken to
eating mice, and that they made me so sick
that I couldn’t sing? Mrs. Williams, when she
heard this ridiculous fabrication —actually
believed, mind vou! —came to me in great
distress with the explanation. Lots of
people probably still think that I dine off
mice!”
The Melba co. eats its meals in a more ex-
pensive fashion than many of the wandering
minstrels who have preceded them here in
the Italian language. They arrived in a
flock at the Australia, rather shaken up by a
southerly gale, and took possession of suites
—not mere “bed and board.” This air of
luxury flings a golden haze round a cosmo-
politan aviary which says its grace in nine
languages.
Tenor John McCormack, with a large
waist, broad shoulders and a cheery, well-fed
appearance, has brought out a secretary and
nurse (the latter for the babies), and travels
like a globe-trotting governor or other poten-
tate in a good way of business.
“Private View” day at the Royal Art So-
ciety’s Show was nearly blotted out by last
week’s persistent rainfall. President Lister
Lister, Secretary Daplyn, Mr. Hadfield and
other R.A.S. members floated round amongst
friends, possible purchasers and stray um-
brellas. It was, altogether, a damp stream
of visitors that trickled in.
The price of vegetables has gone up. At
the R.A.S. display, Miss Margaret McPher-
son (Adelaide) asks 15 guineas for a cauli-
flower. Victorians, wdio recently sold cab-
bages at Is. 6d. each, ought to note this boom
in cauliflowers. Eggs laid in a glass bowd_,
by the same artist, are also marked 15
guineas. Norman Carter values a chintz sofa
at 150 guineas, but there are two Nice Girls
sitting on it, and a black cat that looks as
if its neck were getting tickled. Hans Iley-
sen has flooded his pictures with such a
blaze of s-olden sunshine that his water-color
group makes the adjacent herbage look
like a pale green ghost.
At the Criterion Theatre, Ethel Irving’s
teardrops are twinkling on her eyelashes
every evening except Sunday, amidst loud
applause. As Stella Ballantyne in “The
Witness for the Defence,” her clothes are
subservient to her sufferings, though both are
noticed with enthusiasm. There’s an azure
shimmer of satin, with a glitter of crystal
tunic and a film of tobacco brown. Both are
apparently built for a slim mermaid with no
knees and a fish-tail finish. Ethel Irving’s
walk is the graceful glide of the carefully-
trained, so she doesn’t wriggle in her eel-
skin draperies. She has an easier knee-
movement in an emotional interlude of vieux-
rose, with hand-embroidered motifs, and she
makes the Great Confession in a romantic
episode in brown, with an extra half-yard of
circumference.
Mason puts a subtle feminine touch into
Stella’s confession of her tragic misdeed.
“And —afterwards —l went to bed —I sup-
posed was numbed —or dulled with the misery
of it —if you’ll believe me” (sinks into a
chair), “I really did go to sleep.” From the
gallery, on the first night, floated “Poor
Wretch!” a spontaneous chunk of sympathy
from woman to woman. This emotional
type, the Stella Ballantynes, with nerves
like twisted fiddle-strings, probably would fall
asleep, after a sudden, abnormal crime, from
sheer physical reaction.
Florence Nelson (Mrs. Pettifer) clothes
Grundyism in a carefully-revised edition of
the Worst Girl’s usually-shrieking attire.
Copper-tinted locks, an aggressive blue coat
and skirt, and a saucy hat with a riotous
feather give a frivolous appearance to Mrs.
Pettifer’s remarks. In spite of this cheerful
garb, she is an elderly lady cat, who knows
her duty to Society, and scratches Stella in
the same old place—behind her back.
Her Majesty’s manager’s box on Saturday
night—“ Miss Gibbs” was again in business
at the old stand—contained more diamonds
than Sydney has seen on one lady’s head for
a long while, and the bouse soon had its eyes
on them and on Melba, who was re-
sponsible for the show of wealth. At the
conclusion of the piece, Leslie Holland ad-
dressed the house, drew its attention (quite
unnecessarily) to the Presence, and
called for three cheers ; and the stage told
her that she .was a jolly good fellow. The
Presence bowed its thanks over the edge of
the box.
ELEONORA DE CISNEROS, OF THE
MELBA OPERA CO.
Photo, by Matzene, Chicago.
Greenoaks, where the Mort family lived
in the long ago, had a christening party on
Saturday, and now wears a new clerical bib
and tucker as Bishopscourt. Henceforth it is
to be the permanent roost of Sydney’s Angli-
can head. The Randwick house, where the
smiling Smiths had to deal out soft drinks
and buns to swarms of clergy at the occa-
sional garden-party, was too far from the
city for practical purposes. Canon D’Arcy
Irvine presented the new Anglican baby with
its official right to the label “Bishopscourt” ;
and St. Andrew’s Choir gave a musical trim-
ming to the water-party.
The “authority to whom a mistake in the
matter should be impossible ” did, neverthe-
less, err in saying that Archbishop Kelly,
who succeeds the late Cardinal Moran as
local head of the R.C. flock, is suffering
from inoperable cancer. Dr. Flynn, the
Archbishop’s adviser, certifies that he lias
never found a trace of the disease, inoper-
able or otherwise: and that ought to allay
any uneasiness which may have been felt in
the diocese. My inaccurate authority mingles
his regrets with a much larger quantity of
gladness; and I fill up the vessel with the
same mixture.
Eleonora de Cisneros, of the Melba Opera
Co., stands sft, Bm. in her black silk stock-
ings, and the other day she showed the gener-
ous curves of Juno under a black glace silk
hip jacket, and unhobbled skirt en suite,
short enough to give a pair of shapely ankles
plenty of fresh-air exercise. She is large
and handsome, and runs well in double har-
ness; has acquired from her Cuban husband
a Spanish flavor to her remarks; was born
in U.S. of Irish and Scottish parents; got
her Puritan grey eyes from the Land of
Kilts, and her spicy manner from the Green
Isle; has a voice described as “mezzo-
soprano-contralto,” and makes her debut here
in Delilah.
Samson, in opera, will have a poor chance
of saving his hair when Eleonora-Delilah
meets him in one of Paquin’s classic creations
(no corsets, and early-Eastern fashion plates
faithfully copied)—a vision of pink and sil-
ver, with flowers embossed . ail over her
radiant person. In the American fashion,
Eleonora goes to Paris for her frocks, and
the tags in her waist-bands are all stamped
“Paquin” ; she may even go to Paris when she
dies. Nevertheless, she was the first, or one
of the first, home-trained grand-opera artists
in the U.S., though she put the polish on her
experience by brilliant seasons in Italv and
at Covent Garden.
“Rosna's” line or two from London :
Marie Narelle is back, with a forest of laurels, col-
lected in her tour, with John McCormack, of Uncle
Sam’s country. According to American critics, our
Marie is the greatest living singer of Irish songs,
and she will set off to sample a further triumpnal
progress as soon as John McCormack is through
with the Melba opera season in Australia. Looking
a very radiant nightingale, with a green feather nod-
ding in her millinery, she wandered into the Aus-
tralian garden revel at the Dominions Club the
other day, with Emily Soldene and a troop of
friends. She is rejoicing in the possession of a dove-
cote at Bayswater, and has her two musical babes
with her.
Stormy petrels and other human water-
fowl paddled through the wet streets last
week, and some of them dived into Vickery’s
Chambers, to drip amongst the Arts and
Crafts Society’s exhibits. A one-day show
a month is the society’s new outbreak, and
several inches of rain fell on the first effort.
Eirene Mort’s designs generally get out of
(the ruck, and a flight of black swans across
a idloudless sky of red leather made a Blotter
of Beauty. Mildred Creed’s enamelling, rich
in opalescent fires of blue, green, and crim-
son, was tucked away in a snug glass case.
Mrs. Soderberg hand-beats brass till it rises
up into dragons and other wild beasts. Mary
Makinson carves -wood into bough and bud.
Various other Arts-and-Crafters were repre-
sented, and a life-saving apparatus—hot tea,
etc.—was produced at 4 p.m., to resuscitate
the Nearly Drowned.
Outside the Y.W.C.A. Hall last week a
motor or two puffed petrol fumes into the wet
atmosphere while the Women’s Conference
galloped through a few hospitals and other
charities under the wing of the National
Council of Women. The plain person merely
exuded mud, and squelched up the stairs to
the hall, in or out of goloshes, according to
her view of rubber. A 10-minute time limit
had been set on the speakers; but the lady
bellringer tactfully concealed her crime by
getting well into the background, and hiding
round one corner of the organ.
The woman wdio will surrender without a
kick to the tinkle of a bell is, however, either
dead or dumb. Each speaker fled from the
Y.W.C.A. Hall platform when the Lady with
the Clock gave the alarm, but each in turn
made a magnificent rally, and got in back-an-
swers from the floor during the long-drawn-out
discussions. An outline of the history of Syd-
ney could be given in less time than it took
the loquacious to get to the point. The
Admiralty Lady presided on the second day,
with Mrs. Hugh Dixson close to her left ear.
Mrs. McMillan, the Calico Knight’s lady,
wearing her lately-returned London garb,
confirmed the local impression that flowers
are going to polka up the sides of summer
hats.
Dr. Constance D’Arcy, who got away at a
gallop on the track of the Royal Hospital
for Women; Dr. Carlile Fox, on Sydney
Medical Mission (“They call us the three-
penny doctors”) ; and the Health Society
lady, o© dirty drains, germs, insanitary tele-
phones, and other crimes (“still fighting”)—-
these ran away from all competition on the
second day of the Conference.
The Bathurst Suttor family is spreading
over this part of the earth, and one tendril,
Bruce Suttor, has crossed the harbor and
■climbed up to the rose gardens of Chatswood.
By way of a send-off, there was a silver-
plated gathering of the clan around the
bridal veil of the Bruce Suttor daughter when
she married Dr. Frank Stokes the other day.
Parson Beck tied the knot in St. John's
Church, Darlinghurst.
Solicitor Wegg Horne, who married Mrs.
Kathleen Kentish, of this city, in the fore-
part of August, is bringing his little lady-
bird back to her Sydney nest this week.
They honeymooned in the Cabbage Patch.
From London :
The J. C. Williamsons have taken George Ed-
wardes’s house in Park Square, London. But they
don’t intend to absolutely vegetate in it. Business
arranged; they propose to set out on a series of long
motor whirls through Ireland, Wales, Scotland, and
- 'Other wild and little-inhabited parts.
Mrs. Paterson, the sister and guest of our
strenuous Rose Bay skylark, is a gentle, soft-
voiced woman, with a Whistler atmosphere
about her slender lines. The occupation of
sometimes sistering anything built, mentally
and physically, in the colossal style .of
Melba, is akin to sending out to sea a small
yacht to act as consort to a battleship.
The late artist Arthur Collingridge’s son,
William E. Collingridge, was married the
■other day amongst the hills and dales near
Ryde (Villa Maria Church was the place),
to Miss Mary Heydon, only daughter of L. F.
Heydon, M.L.C., and Mrs. Heydon, of Hun-
, ter’s Hill. The brothers George and Arthur
Collingridge, both artists, came out to this
patch from overseas, to do wood-engravings
for the Picturesque Atlas. The family has
a roving history; Artist George shook a fist
in the Italian scrap which had Garibaldi for
a storm centre.
Australian Kebe Kussmann, the girl fiddler
who recently snared the Guild Hall gold
medal in the Thames village, is still winning
rewards. The last is an open scholarship
worth 20 guineas a year for three years. The
Merchant Saddlers of London are responsi-
ble for this bursary.
Leopold of Battenberg, whose soulful eyes
and eggshell china complexion were noticed
in these parts last year, recently had the bad
luck to begin breaking himself up in the
Cold Land by a spill from his horse. After
a world’s tour in the hope of getting himself
mended, Leopold’s last breakage has added
another pang to the meditations of ambitious
Beatrice, who has the Spanish succession to
keep her awake o" nights.

Novelist Myrtle Reed, who suicided re-
cently, got on the trail of the unsatisfactory
husband in her last book, “Master of the
Vineyard.” This makes a new note of in-
terest, remembering the alleged reason for
her sudden exit. The dismal line of dedi-
cation, "To all who have loved in vain,” is
also full of female blues.
Dairy Instruction, as it has recently been
chased by girl students at the Technical
College, is not without unexpected sharp
corners. The reason has been the moving-in
of the Agricultural branch to old Ultimo
House, now a Government preserve. A wildly-
horned tale about a dairy damsel who
wanted information re milk testing, but ac-
cidentally imbibed a lecture on the points
of a horse, comes this way. It was, of
course, all owing to the unsettled condition
of the College furniture that the milk-tester
was doomed for a time to swallow horse-
flesh—in the abstract, of course.
Mortimer Menpes, of Australia, has given
the Commonwealth, in gratitude to his
native land, a desirable assortment of his
copies of Old Masters. They run to half a
hundred or so, and big William Lyne has
them stowed away amongst his incoming
baggage. . ~

Assembly recently are inevitable when men-
tal conflict reaches an acute stage. The cru-
dities of political emotions are best smoothed
out by the harmonising influence of D.C.L.
wisdom. To banish uproarious pugnacity
with the spirit of wise geniality is the in-
spiring policy of D.C.L., the Whisky of the
Wise.*

MELBOURNE .
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 28, 1911.
Mr Dear Tarrie, —
The Melbourne Medical Association pre-
scribed refreshments and speeches for itself,
at the Grand Hotel, on Saturday night. Gov-
ernor Fuller was the guest of the evening,
and only managed to get away in time to
pick up her Ex. at the conclusion of the
H. B. Irving play.
The alterations to Her Majesty’s kept the
audience interested before the curtain rose
and during the waits. Buff and gold are the
groundwork of the new decorative scheme:
a vivid line of scarlet binds circle and boxes,
and clustered electrics glow like magnolia
blossoms. The eight boxes have crushed-
mouse curtains and dead-lion walls; the
effect of these subdued colors is to throw
brilliant toilettes into strong relief.
Lady Fuller walked over a scarlet carpet
to her box, and an aide unwrapped her
from her enveloping sables, and disclosed her
clad in a greenish blue frock —a striking
Eeacock effect. A tiara which flashed like a
rst-class lighthouse was the top dressing,
llis Ex. was dining with the doctors else-
where; so the two gentlemen-helps were in
plain evening dress, without the usual col-
ored lapels.
Mrs. Tallis, in white, occupied the next
box, with a very pleased husband. Fan
Dango had a woman’s party in another pen ;
and ex-Mayor Weedon and his lady beamed
from a higher niche. A prominent military
man sat back in another cage, with a wife
clau in black and gold. The best shelf of
all, though, was the one which contained a
bouquet of five pretty girls.
Artist Dancey has made a hit with two
large panels, one on either side of the
proscenium. The figures are, as usual, Trag-
edy and Comedy; but Dancey’s pair arrest
the eye and keep it in custody. The foyer
has a new outbreak of cretonne, which is
almost loud enough to interrupt the chatter
of the loungers. In the coffee corner, how-
ever, the chair costumes are soft ivory and
rose chintz. The Brenan girls have, by the
way, taken possession of this intermittent
foodery. The waiting is done by smart
maids in black, with cherry-colored ribbons
on their tiny muslin aprons. Similar visions
carry the sweets around the circle, and, if
you desire it, book your table an act in ad-
vance for the next interval.
A swaggah club has been simmering with
rage ever since the farewell dinner it ten-
dered to a departed notability. Many of the
members declined to attend until they had
been rounded up by a horrified president,
who preached to them with “club courtesy”
as a text. The intending absentees ceded
the point, and agreed to sit at salt with the
departing one. They duly attended, and all
went fairly well till the guest of the even-
ing rose at speechifying time and spoke
wrathfully to the roomful of men on the
attitude they had adopted towards him.
When he had quite finished, the stunned
gathering took the full count lying back in
its collective chair, while the awed waiters
stepped lightly like undertakers. The sore-
ness has not worn off yet.
Every chocolate-fed dog aristocrat had its
owner on a string on Friday, when the
Ladies’ Kennel Club opened its show at
Wirths’ Park. Secretary Nancy Grice was
dragged to the place of judgment by her
wire : haired pet. Mrs. Carlyle Smythe
hurried after a big Irish terrier; while her
sister, Miss Addie Smythe, was towed by
“Highgate Nell,” a taker of many prizes.
Miss Hope-Campbell, an Irish girl with a
Scotch name, had her work cut out to hold
her two excited collies —two Scotch dogs
with Irish names, “Derry Boy” and “Blar-
ney Girl.” She and Miss Bagot worked
hard at hanging up the prize-winners’ cards,
for an anxious world must know as soon as
possible just where the' winners are.
Lady Denman came along in the morning.
“She knows dogs all right,” commented some
dog-lover; “she lets them sniff her hand be-
fore patting them.” Big dogs are her fav-
orites. There were only two or three St.
Bernards in the show; our warm climate
plays up with the tempers of the heavily-
coated things, so there are not many of them
in the country. In Germany they shave
dogs for the s,ummer, and large animals run
about short-coated, like lions, except for a
ruff round the neck.
The Smythes’ Irish terrier, “Highgate
Nell,” a “well-featured” matron, had to sit
humbly under a third-prize ticket, and see
her puppy “Marcus” carry off the first. Dr.
Iredell’s imported bulldog, “Hilltown Doc-
tor,” planted his bandy legs firmly on his
bench and managed to score a special, but
could not beat the Australian-bred cham-
pions. Mrs. Ernest Keep’s Pomeranian,
“Tinker Bell,” kept up the doggy records of
the family, but looked a bit anxious when
someone mentioned that Mrs. Keep was
bringing out some new rivals from the place
where the breed is treated reverently. The
Australian terriers were a disappointingly
mixed lot. Every kind of small, rough-
haired barking machine seems to be eligible
for the Australian bench. The real Aus-
tralian dog is the wonderfully clever kelpie,
descendant of the short-haired collie of Scot-
land and the lawless dingo. After the judg-
ing, there was a tented lunch, at which the
women talked results, and the dogs held a
yelping convention, which was almost as
noisy as the Ma State’s Parliament last
week.
Some of the Very Best sports took a lively
interest in the Denmans at their first Aus-
tralian hunt. The little G.-G., perched on
a fine bit of horseflesh, took the first high
jump in gallant style; but the lady waited
until the top rail was lowered, and then
made for the gap, followed by a discreet
gentleman-in-waiting. The perils of the
chase are much tempered to a gent-in-wait-
ing unless he insists on restoring the top
rail to its place before pursuing his course
of duty.
Smart womankind, grown wise in domes-
tic experiences, smiles at the optimism of
those newchum friends of the G.-G. who have
started a suburban residence with the idea
that one maid-of-all-work and a nurse (there
are two kiddies) will “do for them” very
nicely. _ The newchums’ views of a Mary
Ann’s tireless devotion to duty are so essen-
tially English, you know. They haven’t yet
been confronted with a Mary Ann who puts
you through a catechism before stating that
you will not suit.
Mrs. Harper caught two Governors’ wives
with one At Home, and Toorak smiled en-
viously. The drawing card was the Victoria
League of Victoria, of which impressive as-
sembly the hostess holds the rank of chair-
woman of the Hospitality Committee. Lady
Fuller was invited to “meet” Lady Denman,
and the League members were invited to meet
them both. It was a crowded function, for
Toorak came in force, and many fine ostrich
feathers were roughly treated. Mrs. Ewart
waved the women of the V.L.V. Choir
through several numbers; Jessie Masson
played the piano, and pretty Mrs. Carmen
I'mschof Harris sang a song composed by
Mrs. Ewart. Later on the company re-
paired to another apartment filled with small
tables, and fed to dance music supplied by a
band in the hall. The Denman secretary
and a Fuller aide were the only men present.
So late did the Excellencies stay that the
people who departed early had to walk to
the gate to enmotor, for the Denman car-
riage and the yellow Fuller motor were in
the porch, and none might burn oil before
them. On departing, a purple footman re-
spectfully enveloped the Denman lady in her
chinchilla coat, while the shover of the
Yellow Peril held the door for the sub-Excel-
lency.
Mere carriage folk have to be acrobats;
the low, convenient motor is mounted with
ease even by the gouty. After the V.L.V.
gathering at Mrs. Harper’s, Lady Denman
showed several inches of trim ankle when
getting into the landau for the homeward
drive; Mrs. Holroyd had to have the assist-
ance of the footman to climb into her phae-
ton ; and a Sassiety lady who keeps to the
sedate brougham showed the other guests a
full sample of the latest silk hose as she
pulled herself up to her seat.
The new boom in skating has brought a
fresh crop of slipping novices on to the ice.
Onlookers, with their toes resting on the
hot-water pipes, revel in the contortion acts
of the dogged learners. Some of them walk
heavily on their skates, and wait wretchedly
for the floor to hit them on the back of the
head; others, more dashing, do (or dare)
“the glide,” and pull up with a dislocating
jerk like trains when the Westinghouse is
jambed hard on. The grave and graceful
instructors lightly cut eights in a superior
way, supporting themselves meanwhile by
stroking the atmosphere with white-gloved
hands. Wide-eyed flapper pupils watch
these exquisite performers with a longing,
hopeless air.
A fancy-dress carnival is to slither this
(Thursday) evening at the Glassy Harem,
under vice-regal patronage.
George Armstrong de Melba is seen around
Melbourne again, with white spats at one»
end of him and a paddy-green lid at the other.
George’s metropolitan air is more blase than
ever, but down at Lilydale he perturbs the
countryside by his reckless galloping and
leaps over local hedges. That is to say, the
gee-gee gallops and leaps whilst George pre-
serves an easy demeanor on top. As a bold
horseman, he is undeniably It.
It’s understood that the Vera Clarke-Lan-
dale engagement, lately announced, will not
reach its matrimonial destination until the
gallant captain's clan is quite out of mourn-
ing for the death of Landale pere.
Alderman Brenan’s dimpled daughter
Maggie was quietly married, on Monday of
last week, to Mr. Bird, and her seven sisters
waved her a happy farewell at Spencer-
street, as the Sydney express bore her olf
with her husband for a Harborside honey-
mooning. Only the members of the family
were invited to the marrying; but the church
was packed with friends who came deter-
mined to see the popular girl in her white
satin and orange blossom. Mrs. Bird is the
sister who helped Jennie Brenan most with
her ballet drills.
The stage-struck ones, looking for personal
items re the Chocolate Soldier co., remark
that the tenor, Noel Fleming, has a wife in
attendance, and that the very Italian-looking
contralto brightens her embonpoint with some
beautiful diamonds in private life.
At a recent House of Commons dinner
given by the Doughtys, Arthur Balfour sat
on the right of the lady who was “Try-
phena,” while Father Bernard Vaughan sat
on her left. More than this the social
climber cannot hope to achieve. It is the
dizzy limit.
Princess Hassan(who was Ola Jane Hum-
phrey) turned up at the M.L. Wards’ fare-
well party in London. Her hair has taken
on a marvellous Titian tint, and her gown of
white lace was a creation to dream about.
Mrs. Felix Levien is back again in Vic-
toria after some years spent in England.
She was in a motor accident some time ago,
and, as a result, was still on crutches when
sailing-day arrived. However, on the home-
ward voyage she picked up wonderfully,
and, after landing, went on to Mildura to
see her son.
The Jack Wallaces, who . lately acquired
a new baby, are giving up their house in
JLoorak, and making for the land again. The
W. J. T. Clarkes are taking the place for a
term of years, so the smart chicks of the
family are evidently bent on the complete
social conquest of Melbourne.
Miss Eileen Gordon, daughter of the irre-
pressible General, is off to Sydney to study
singing under Madame Christian. Lately
she was having a breezy time at Warrnam-
bool with the Joe Archibalds.
A young spinster of the newly-arrived
G.-G. party sets an example of economy.
Apparently she brought out from England
a stock of mother’s visiting cards, and these
she utilises by striking out the “Mrs.” and
substituting “Miss.”
One of the Osterley’s wool-buying
contingent got up a flirtation with a
pretty homing Coronateur. But one
day, whilst the ship was tied up at
one of the intermediate ports, Mr. Wool was
discovered hugging a delectable girl visitor.
Miss Coronateur indulged in some hysterics,
but forgave the faithless one when the ship
got out to sea. At the next port, however,
she cast him off for ever. This time there
were two strange women competing for the
long lost hug.
Radical Parson Strong, who years ago
left the Scotch Church, and, with his large
following, formed the Australian Church, is
being moved on from the high-stepped taber-
nacle in smoky Ffinders-street. The place
originally cost £23,000, and there is still a
debilitating debt of about £ll,OOO on it.
Wherever he g;oes, Strong will take his
young men with him; for the young
male person to whom other pilots appeal in
vain goes to Strong in such numbers that
the best Sunday hat of the female worship-
per generally appears as a florid island of
millinery in a sea of male domes.
A Bulletin penwoman in London tells
me that she at a Suffragette meeting
given by the Actresses’ Franchise League at
the Piccadilly Restaurant, and found a
swagger and frocky function, hostessed by
Bendigo’s Alice Crawford. “The Australian
damsel, in a frock of old-rose silk and some
black and white millinery, was remarkably
good to see, in spite of the fact that sickness
had lately wrested her from her part in
‘Dombey and Son.’ One of the bright
speakers of the afternoon, by the way, was
the wife of novelist Zangwill. They are
cute folk who engineer the suffrage societies
on this side, and none of the speeches are
dull, whatever else they may be.”
For weeks past, the members of Mel-
bourne’s oldest club for women have been
shaking in their shoes (as much as high
heels would let them). Writs for libel are
fearsome things to carry round in one’s muff,
and everyone wants to know when the case
is to be called. Nice young women are con-
tinually button-holing busy men-o’-law for
information regarding the programme of the
day; but, as the divorce harvest is just being
reaped, most of the men who wear sofa
stuffing on their heads are too busy getting
their home-shattering documents together to
bother with agitated clubeens.
A male scoffer writes:—
The stem, inflexible respectability of the Women’s
Nat. Pol. League was revealed to me in one act the
other morning. A middle-aged lady, with a bauble
at her breast, sat opposite me in a tram-car, and her
air of self-sufficiency as she studied her notebook
denoted a long experience in addressing meetings
and moving resolutions. The bauble, attached to a
bow of ribbon, told its tale in capital letters—
W.N.P.L., or was it W.P.N.L. ? There was a neat-
ness and unpretentious expensiveness about the lady's
attire which seemed intended to invite admiration
mingled with awe. 1 coughed once or twice, hoping
she might offer me a jujube, but it didn’t come off.
Then the tram conductor came along to take her
fare. She tendered half a crown, and received
2s. 3d. in small change. Coin by coin she dropped
the change into her handbag—all save a threepenny-
bit, which she returned to the conductor with,
“Please give me another one for this. It is rather
battered.” Sworn enemy to all disreputable things,
from Socialistic principles right down to question-
able thrums I
The literary circle of the Swastika Club is
struggling hard to get several floors above
the hat-gossip level of most feminine clubs.
On Thursday afternoon, a full house of
women listened reverently to the reading of
a paper by Mrs. Porter on the homes of
Lowell and Emerson. Unie Russell recited
“Miles Standish,” and provided some choice
musical morsels; Miss Green sang a few of
Longfellow’s songs, which have been set to
music by her cousin, Marian Alsop; and
Miss Boyes mentioned melodiously that “The
Sea Hath Its Pearls,” though up at Thirsty
Island they complain that a man has to dive
a lot deeper these days to find them. Tea
was provided at a number of small tables.
President Mrs. Staughton took Mrs. J. L.
Currie and a Molesworth Greene representa-
tive to her corner. The literary secretary,
Miss Ilurst, is arranging a Browning after-
noon for the middle of September, so the cul-
ture-climbing is to go on. With a judicious
mixture of music and tea, the crowd may be
enticed right up to the roof garden.
The charity dance flourished exceedingly
this winter, and now, with Spring knocking
on the door with a branch of wattle, Mrs.
Staughton means to go one better than all
the crowd with her Try Boys’ hop. It is
to be held on the eve of the Caulfield Cup,
“when all my friends will be down,” saith
the Widow. No doubt Woolocracy will
dutifully two-step up with its half-guineas
and make it a big success. All the Handles
in Melbourne have promised to act on the
committee with the whole flock of mayor-
esses.
There is a dreadful boom in ancestors just
now. A girl portrait-painter, who had des-
pairfully given up Art for crochet and other
sedentary vices, was moved to get out her
brushes by meeting an unusually pretty girl.
The completed picture was much admired,
and the result was a visit from the sitter’s
mother, who, after pressing a handsome fee
into the artist’s hand, requested her to pro-
vide some far-back grandfathers and grand-
mothers. A cheerful girl-artist is now very
busy hunting up old, distinguished-looking
models and studying antique costumes in
dusty libraries.
Mrs. Professor Peterson has a daughter
who plays the flute. She is in her teens, and
has an original style of hairdressing—two
long Marguerite plaits rolled twice round her
small head. The effect is very smart.
The Underwoods are giving their dance in
the Haw-Hawthorn Town Hall at the end of
the month. The family own a few vaults of
the Sanderson shipping wealth, and great
things are expected.
Norman and Dorothy Vane Menzies are
threatening a children’s fancy dress and pos-
ter ball for charity towards the end of Sep-
tember The lounge of the big hotel is to be
reserved tor the affair, and it is understood
that no grown-up will be admitted unless
acting as attendant to some infant.
Perhaps the recent outbreak in England
was the aftermath of the Coronation free-
dinners. When half-starved Oliver Twist
revolted and smote the bully on the nose, the
Beadle remarked: “Too much meat!” and
put the insurgent on a low diet. Fortu-
nately for the Earl of Shares and the Duke of
Hividends, Coronations only come at lon®'
intervals; if there were free food once a
month, there is no telling what the dreadful
rabble might not be up to!
n ? e f Majesty’s, on Friday night, sounded
and looked very unlike a favorite amuse-
ment house. It breathed fresh paint and re-
echoed the furious toil of a regiment of men
armed mostly with hammers. Sassiety has
now eight boxes wherein to disport ‘ltself,
lhe inner walls are covered with dull gold
tapestry, and the perches are admirably
adapted for showing off the boxed peacocks
to the rest of the house. This tremendously
important point has hitherto not been
studied sufficiently in Australia ; to heaps of
women the play is only half the thing—the
frock is the other half. Both circle and
gallery benefit by the added boxes. The two
haif-cirdes are shaped like horseshoes, and
the latecomer at the end gets almost as good
a view as the early-booker in the centre.
Iwo gold, flower-wreathed cherubs frolic
over the boxes, and the proscenium rests in
a rising sun of electrics and painted rays.

From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Our Grand National meeting opened on Saturday
There was a big crowd of women, mostly coat-and-
skirted. The Government House box held only the
Admiral and Captain Fletcher, the feminine portion
mTT S l lU i n S y dne y- Eager raised
joy in the hearts of the few who backed him bv
’ n Stepney Welter, £22 for second
place. Mr. R. M. Hawker scored wins with Ilar-
pargus and Tiercel and Mr. A. P. Bowman got a
second with The Greek. Yudnapinna, who carried
off honors m the Grand National Hurdle Race, is
the property of W. T. Mortlock; so, between
Hawkers and Bowmans and Mortlocks, the squatters
had a good day.
The St. Peter’s College sports fell on the same dav
as the races, and took a -crowd of relatives and
school girls out to the college grounds. An outbreak
of measles cast a faint tinge of gloom over the
revels. The C.J.’s lady gave away the prizes: she
was supported by the usual band of officials and
college governors.
A house in North Adelaide has been leased for
the Bishop and his family while their rightful home
is being repaired. The temporary residence is no
palace; and, oddly enough, it’s situated immediately
next an R.O. church and priorv.
Mayor Cohen has brought 'back with him plans
and specifications of the King Edward memorial.
The statue, which is cited in good Yankee fashion
as “the largest in Australia,” is to be located near
Government House gates, an arrangement which will
force Venus to move eastwards and make room.
Sculptor Mackennal, who has charge of the work,
declares it a two years’ task.
From the North comes this tale: After a dance,
at which the supper was of the usual country order
—good and plenty—the key of the supper-room door
was found to be missing, and so the remains of the
feast could not be adequately secured. One brave,
having his suspicions, volunteered as watchman, and
after a lonely vigil had his patience rewarded by the
sight of a trio of erstwhile guests making their way
carefully into the room 1 History doesn’t relate
what ensued, but in that town three names are miss-
ing from the visiting books of the elect.
There are rumors that a church pageant is under
weigh, and that the Theatre Royal is to be hired for
the purpose. What the early-Victorian section of
the Anglicans will say, goodness knows, but some of
them are likely to be eloquent.
Hunting enthusiasts j’umped over some fresh coun-
try near Largs Bay at their last run. Dr. Morris
and Captain Osborne were responsible for the inno-
vation. Fences had to be capped, and new “metal”
rolled for the comfort of “roadsters,” so the two
spent a busy week of preparation. The move was
necessary, owing to the invasion of one-time subur-
ban runs by electric trams and red-roofed “villas.”
Lady and Miss Bosanqiiet returned from Sydney on
Wednesday with Mr. Rowe. Mrs. Rowe is due to
arrive from England some time next month.
Spring is on us—we know it by the new finery in
the shops, a blaze of spring flowers in the gardens,
and a few odd garden parties. The Waites’ home
at Glen Osmond boasts one of the best gardens we
have, an! the women folk who foregathered there
at an At Home last week, came away wildly j'ealous.
The house stands on a rise, with park-like grounds
sloping before it towards a ribbon of sea in the dis-
tance.
Street verandahs are the latest subject of argu-
ment ; some say they’re architectural eye-sores, others
say they’ve their uses in hot and wet weather. If
the removal of the verandahs will be followed by
a removal of the dead-beats who lounge against
their posts, they may well be spared. Otherwise it
seems a pity to condemn them in a country where
the thermometer has such lofty ambitions.

THERE seems to be a widespread idea that Beauty Culture is a luxury which
lies only within the reach of those who are blessed with a superfluity of this
world’s goods. It is true that complicated Face Treatments, which require
infinite skill and experience on the part of the operator, are expensive ; but that a
moderately good complexion cannot be improved and preserved by a simple home-
treatment, without the expenditure of a considerable sum of money, is a fallacy
which should long since have died a natural death.
Australia’s leading Face Specialist, Madame Helena Rubinstein, of 274
Collins Street, Melbourne, and 158 Pitt Street, Sydney, who has the distinction
of commanding very high fees for special professional services, for the best of
leasons, of course—that she produces results that satisfy her numerous clients—-on
being consulted on this point, unhesitatingly gave it as her opinion that the woman
who can only afford to spend jfx to a year on her complexion may do an
amazing amount of good for herself.
She should first of all provide herself with a
jar of Valaze, the marvellous Skin Food, which
beautifies the skin as no ordinary face cream has
power to do. This preparation removes freckles
and blotches, moderates lines and wrinkles until
the complexion is soft, smooth and flawless, and
m place of sallowness appear the beautiful pink
and white tints of a perfectly healthy skin.
She should next get a jar of Novena Cerate,
which is, without doubt, one of the finest skin
cleansers (without the use of soap or water) in
existence, and which not only cleans the outer sur-
face of the skin, but penetrates through its several
layers, exercising a beneficent and healing influ-
ence. It gives the skin a delicious velvety soft-
ness, and allays all irritation.
To these preparations should be added Valaze
Skin Tonic, which tones and braces up the skin
—astringent, antiseptic, stimulating and nourish-
ing—a tonic in the truest sense of the word.
This need only be used about twice or three times a
week.
Powder, of course, very few women can do without. Mme. Rubinstein is
one of the rare people who have devoted thought to this important point, and as a
result she has produced two distinct varieties, one for greasy skins, and one for
dry. Both of these are made in four tints, so that every shade of complexion may
be suited.
In conjunction with Valaze Complexion Soap should be used that unique
speciality, Yoskpasta, and the two invariably produce that smooth, supple, satiny
skin that is so noticeable in all Mme Rubinstein’s clients.
The prices of the foregoing preparations are surprisingly moderate; thus,
Valaze is 3/6 and 6/6 a jar, and Novena Cerate 2/- and 3/6, whilst the powders
maybe bought from 2/-and 3/-a box, Skin Tonic from 3/6 a bottle, the Voskpasta
is 3/-, and the Valaze Complexion Soap 2/-. How economical they are in use may
be gauged by the fact that a 6/6 jar of Valaze, which holds more than three times
as much as the smaller size, will, with ordinary usage, last for about five months.

CURED HIS
SCIATICA.
»
This Man got Relief After
Four Months' Suffering.
“I was ailing for about four months off
and on with sciatica,” said Mr. Arthur E.
White, 64 Rosehill Street, Redfern. “I got
a chill at my work, and it brought on a
severe pain in my back, just across the
loins. It would ease off during the day
after I had a little exercise, but it was bad
at night-time. I could hardly close my
eyes. I’d be twisting and turning all ways
to try and get some relief. It was like a
toothache, with the constant nagging, gnaw-
ing pain. The skin would be very red
and tender. I rubbed myself with lini-
ments, but found them no good. The pain
extended down the side of my left leg and
the back of my thigh. For about an hour
after I’d get out of bed I could only limp
about. It would start about five each night
and last for hours. I’d always be worse
when warm in bed. I cduldn’t bend my
back without the pain catching me like a
knife. The pain only seemed to settle
deeper as time went on. At last, on a
friend’s advice, I tried Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. I felt a shade of ease with the first
box, and so I got another, and then I really
began to feel distinct benefit. My back
began to get supple again. I could bend
down with less difficulty. I was working
on the railway line at Belmore then. By
the time I had finished the third box I was
better still. The pains had lessened so
much that 1 could sleep quite easily. The
darting spasms of pain down my leg went
away, as did the stiffness on rising. I took
two more boxes, and they finally drove away
every sign of the sciatica, and now I’m as
supple as ever.”
Remember, only the genuine Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills cure. Don’t be gulled into
taking something said to be just as good. Of
all dealers at 2s. 9d. per box, 6 boxes los.
3d., or from the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.
of Australasia Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
THE SEE THAT DOESN'T SAW.
N. S. WALES: “It isn't much of a game at
the best; but if they can t get a move out
of the thing I’m going to get up."
Lord Rosebery (whom the late George
Dibbs, a shrewd observer, described as “the
biggest snob in England”) recently made the
terrible discovery that an Englishman's
’ouse is no longer his castle, because “under
the Finance Act a valuer may enter any
house and inspect it at his will for the pur-
pose of land valuation.” For years and years
the gas inspector, and the water inspector,
and the truancy man, and sanitary and medi-
cal officers, and plenty of otners have been
in the habit of looking iix at the poor man’s
home, and the poor man hasn’t had any
sympathy; but as soon as rich Rosebery’s
aristocratic privacy is at all threatened,
there’s a howl, and Freedom falls dead.
Newman, M.P., having introduced to the
Maoriland Elouse of Reps, an Hereditary
Titles Prevention Bill, Auckland Herald,
without knowing the contents, shrieked that
it “reflected discredit upon its promoter and
all those who encouraged it,” and was “un-
worthy of self-respecting men.” Whereupon
the polite Newman wrote to the enraged
editor; —
I venture to suggest that this somewhat intemper-
ate language is not justified by the circumstances,
and I desire, with all courtesy, to point out to you
that, as a matter of fact, you have not yet had an
opportunity of even perusing the Bill in question.. .
I would be very sorry to put any restraint upon
your enthusiasm upon this subject, but at the same
time I must in self-defence suggest that it would be
a safe rule, even for the editor of a newspaper, to
defer criticism of any Bill until after perusal of the
same.
And such is the ingratitude of mankind and
editors that the hasty critic did not rise
and apologise, let alone invite the author to
the place where the man sells the bottled
dog.
Thougli the poor employer is being ruined
by high wages, there is apparently a trifle
of money still left in his pockets. South
Aus. has now made a move to provide a
fighting fund of £lOO,OOO, euphoniously desig-
nated an Employers’ Mutual Assurance Co.,
and employers are expected to take up
“shares” according to the amount of wages
they pay. Melbourne’s Employers’ Federa-
tion started a similar fund a while back,
when the Harvester strike was on, and cer-
tain adipose persons subscribed £lOOO and
£5OO each ; but it seems to have stopped to
renew its carburetter or something.
A hole has been found in the N.S.W.
Workmen’s Compensation Act large enough
for a lawyer to crawl through. Recently a
mill employee had his hand hurt. After he
had been away a fortnight the employers asked
him to come in and do what he could to help
them through a rush. He was one of the
willing sort, and did it; but in a few days he
found that even this light work was impos-
sible, and he had to knock off. Yet, accord-
ing to the Act, he was debarred from claim-
ing the compensation to which he was other-
wise entitled, simply because he had returned
to work to oblige his employer. When the
talked-of Amending Bill is being drafted, it
is to be hoped the Labor Government won’t
forget to put a patch over this unpleasant
orifice.
“A Worried Grandma” rose the other
morning and hurled her ball of cotton wool
at Australia’s poor little compulsory-service
scheme. The parade grounds are already
littered with the mud and stones of the
junior pushes, and the inferior English of
unpati’iotic aldermen; so “Grandma’s” pro-
jectile came as an agreeable novelty. The
distracted old lady asked tremblingly, in the
manner of one who is sending a loved one to
almost certain destruction, if the boys were
to be compelled to go to drill in all weathers!
Unreasonable “Grandma” quite possibly
the mother of one of the aldermen who have
been refusing area officers the use of their
halls because the young army might want
the floor some night when a “social” desired
to revolve to incredibly bad music. If she
isn’t, she probably knows two other worried
grandmas who have mothered a mayor or
two. In either case, instead of hurling wool
about, and breathing a useless “Deary me!”
in the old folks’ corner of the family news-
paper, she might use her vote and the influ-
ence of her grey hairs on the men who ax*e
really responsible for keeping the kids out in
the wet.

Municipal election day in and around Mel-
bourne was a great day for the rnotah. “Run
you to the booth, ma’am,” said a tout with a
cab to one duly-qualified female in the street.
“No, thanks,” she replied coldly ; “I shall vote
for one with a motor.” In a good many
instances the contest was really Motors
versus Motives —with the odds on the Motors.
SAILING MATES.
At the P.L.L. conference,. Mr. Holman, with great
emphasis, drove home his contention that he was in
no way bound to follow the Federal lead.... The
cheers which greeted him as he concluded showed
that he had the other side beaten.... As a matter
of fact the conference soon showed itself on the side
of the defendants. —Sydney D.T.
EACH (to the other) : “ Just wot I see, love!
Why should we quarrel over Australian
industries , wages, strikes a n’ sick trifles ,
when we both ’as a equal share in this
beautiful place? ”
Before considering the question of higher
Protection, Customs Minister Tudor asked
manufacturers for certain information, and
he regrets that he hasn’t had many replies.
Those, however, who have replied complain
of shortage of labor rather than of orders.
It may be granted that many—perhaps most
—manufacturers are busy; but the import
statistics show that there are not half as
many manufacturers as there ought to be ; and
if Tudor hasn’t got replies, it is in many cases
because there is nobody to do the replying.
The Department can’t expect demands' for
more Protection from manufacturers of (say)
calico, flannelette, or cotton piece goods,
when the Protection hasn’t been sufficient
to encourage anybody to start up in those
industries. Hence the most eloquent reply
that Tudor will get from anywhere will be
from the unborn manufacturers who clamor
through the Department’s own statistics.
Miss Eleanor Cameron, recently mentioned
as having separated herself from the Vic.
Wimmen’s National League, is still attached
to that organisation. This paper is assured
that the lady has had no difference with the
league, but has merely resigned her secre-
tary's job to play the more limelit part of
“speaker and organiser.”
PRO VIDENCE AND THE HOG.
“ Thank Heaven it wasn’t a cow!"
“White Ants” from the West; —
They say W.A. has no droughts. Ugh! From
Geralrtton in the north, to Beverley in the south, at
the moment of writing, the drought has its grip.
Half the new-ehum cockies are in despair—no water,
no feed, no anything. Even some of the Government
surveyors are carting water from 10 to 30 miles.
And all the time, in order to save the big landlords
who hold the coastal country where the rainfall is
pretty' sure, the Government pushes settlers out, out,
out. There’s going to be a smash before long.
If these strenuous
anti-Socialists are not
careful they will hurt
themselves in their
wild rush to embrace
the “tiger,” and call
it their long-lost bro-
ther. At latest, the
Vic. Government had
yielded to entreaties
and undertaken to put
up a canning factory,
for fruit and vege-
tables, at Shepparton.
Rochester has also got
a half-promise of a
cannery, and Kya-
bram is i - aising Cain
because it has been
overlooked. Kyabram,
where the ’l’ootra
first mounted his
dunghill and began
to crow, was sup-
posed to be dead, but
apparently it isn’t too
deceased to want to
get up and shake hands with the other part
of the Zoo.
From an old hand
The N.S.W. State Parliament still keeps the belt
as a “Wild West” show. The election of Speaker
Willis, with its Cromwellian culmination, was the
end of a series of scenes of buffoonery and disorder
which have not been paralleled since the time when
a certain insignificant member sat in the Speaker’s
chair with a waste-paper basket on his head, and
robes of floor carpet; or since that other time when
“Tom was doin’ splendid!” The late scenes are
also remindful of the time when Bill Lyne, big
Ginger Clarke, John Perry and others fought, not
singly or in pairs, but like boarding rushes in bucca-
neering times ; and other dates when Jimmy Fletcher
tried to choke John Haynes, and nearly succeeded;
or when John and the late Crick were enfolded in a
deadly embrace on the floor of the vestibule, and
were feeling for their poniards.
No matter how hopeless the fight may be,
the Labor party can always find an enthusi-
ast to contest a “Liberal” seat, and scatter
a few grains of truth while keeping the
enemy moving. The Weary-Willie Govern-
ment in Victoria wishes its crowd was built
like that. Lately a candidate was wanted to
run against Tunnecliffe at Eaglehawk; but,
when the applications for the job closed, the
only one lodged was on behalf of H. R. Wil-
liams, a poor, derelict, political has-been. The
local bosses looked at Williams, and then at
his chances, and decided to try again. They
had an idea that Hicks, a member of the
House of Fat Landlords, might get some-
where near the top ; but, after sampling the
reef, Hicks said it was a duffer, and sat
tight where he was. When the fishers
pulled in their net the second time, the old
derelict was therefore still the only thing
inside it ; and Eaglehawk “Liberals” have
sadly settled down to the conviction that it
will have to be Williams or nothing.
THE DECLINE AND FALL OF US.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In an examination of the causes that led to the
downfall of Rome, the Spectator observes in them
a striking resemblance to some of the most charac-
teristic features of social and political life at the
present day.—Melb. Argus.
The pampered proletariat is doing far too well,
And thus, of course, we’re leaping down a short,
steep place to Hell;
Australia slides perditionwards with fizz-corks in
her liair,
Because Billjim Australia wants his beastly, proper
share.
Just think of it. Observe how near to ltome-like
smash and blazes
Australia drifts! In dreadful fear
The patriot forward gazes!
In ancient Rome the common, plain, hard-working
Masses fed
On turkey, wine, and peacocks’ tongues whilst lying
on a bed;
Large piles of luscious grapes they had, and bowls of
Romish beer,
With fried lark-livers, sturgeon soup, and similar
good cheer.
Corned beef and turnips, day by day, the Classes
had for dinner.
The Masses beat ’em every way,
A simple, easy skinner!
Now, too, among our Masses do we see the same
decay,
And all the old debaucheries on seven-and-six a day ;
The same old, fatted, daily fowl, the same expensive
swill,
The same old Classes sweating for the good of Jim
and Bill!
Is it not so? .. . Yet, every day, despite the
Classes’ preaching,
Doomed Bill goes on his lively way
For Food and Ruin reaching!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.
SNARLIAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Honorable Member for Dry Gully had the floor ;
The talking Chamber echoed and re-echoed to his
roar;
His waistcoat swelled, his bald head swat, his fists
clawed towards “the Chair,”
And ev’ry time he paused for breath, his friends re-
marked “Hair, hair!”
He called men “Traitors!” shook with rage, and
thumped with frightful force;
Then gulped a little water down when he was get-
ting hoarse;
He. bellowed, advertised and bragged. The placid
Hansard men
Recorded ev’ry worthless yelp with swift and skilful
pen.
His own side yawned behind his back; his foemen
long had fled,
Save one who by the table sat, and held his aching
head ;
Dry Gully sizzled in his ear—Dry Gully blazed and
spat,
And in a fat, heroic way kept talking through his
hat;
The pencils of the Hansard men slipped onward,
page by page,
Preserving for posterity that fat man in a rage.
(It was their duty so to do. Some men have jobs
absurd !
What will To-morrow care about Dry Gully’s
brightest word?)
The silent voters, packed and jammed in plain, hard
galleries,
looked down respectfully upon the men who sprawled
at ease ;
And here and there one closely watched the “Han-
sards” as they wrote,
Taking with wondrous skill a true Recording Angel
note;
With Pitman’s magic did those scribes, unruffled and
immense,
Catch up and bottle, cork and store that mud-sprung
eloquence;,
No yelp or bellow ran to waste—they were too keen
for that;
He might have been Demosthenes, That talker through
a hat.
But in the long press gallery unmoved reporters
read
Their first editions languidly, or watched Dry Gully’s
head ;
Not theirs to chase him with a pen, harpooning ev’ry
yell;
They’d mentioned he was wild, artd there was nothing
more to tell;
For space is scarce, and life is short, and men, what-
e’er their views,
Have very little time to spare for Snarliamentarv
News ;
Though bald Dry' Gully' boiled for hours his patriotic
blood,
The State without was merely told: “Dry Gully’s
been in flood.”
When Voter Jones has done with games the State
may get a thought,
And so it happens with his X he scores a frequent 0 :
He never has the time to watch Dry Gully and his
friends;
He merely marks his ballot-slip, and there the matter
ends;
He reads Dry Gully (as boiled down by those who
watch on top),
But never goes in person to that stuffy talking-shop.
As one who hustles for his crust, ’twould give him
quite a shock
To see how Gully strangles Time, and burns his
ticking clock.
They sit on long, upholstered seats, in lounging, yap-
ping rows ;
And in the little balcony the Hansard pencil goes ;
J lie visitors like waxworks sit, the weary pressmen
yawn,
And through the windows nervously peeps in To-
morrow’s dawn ;
The toil-worn country after work is resting un-
ashamed ;
’Twill never see Dry Gully rise, inflated and in-
flamed ;
’Twill never hear his strident yell—and God be
praised for that!
He’s just a paper shadow hooting through a paper
hat!
