SPORTING
NOTIONS
Adelaide Racing Club pulled down the
curtain on its most successful meeting on Saturday,
anu the totalisator supplied a golden fringe to it.
Investments in the machine during the afternoon
climbed to ±,22,536, or over £7OOO more than last
year. In the two days, £44,065 was pushed through
the machine —an increase of nearly £14,000. The first
race on the menu provided an appetiser, when out-
jl. . j Lady Frederick; downed the field, and paid a
dividend of £79 10s. per £1 invested. Other
reasonable divs. were Tadanga’s £ll 175., and
bweet Aroon’s £lO Bs.; while San Toy’s gathering
of the Grand National Steeple yielded £7 15s. The
second” diys. were not very glittering, but the
return of even the invested boodle, and a trifle
over, would cause Ikey Noses to take a fit. Alto-
gether it was a meeting to arouse jealousy in the
bosom of N.S.W. and Victorian punters, who have
to take what Noses offers —and be thankful. The
fleetness of the gee-gees was also responsible for the
murder of two S. Aus. records: Waipuna’s two
miles in 3min. 54 3-ssec. was a second ahead of the
previous best, while San Toy’s Steeple time (6min.
lOsec) is 4sec. better.
The most consistent backer of horses in
Australasia, or probably anywhere else, is the Maori.
Also he is a skilful, plucky and lucKy backer. At
recent Grand National meeting at Christchurch
(“5. , on , e Tekani Pere bet Ikey Noses that he
could pick the winners of the three big races, in ad-
e , 10 JV to , tlle double. Noses laid him £BOO to
£1 that he couldn’t nominate the treble, and £2OO
to £1 that he couldn’t sort out the double. Israel
is exactly £lOOO out on the argument.
* * * *
The ink wasn’t dry on The Bulletin’s
prediction, last week, when Eyeglass was scratched
for the Metrop., owing to “flats” (who thought they
were ‘sharps”) having bulged Ikey Noses’s bag with
coin, and thereby offended the owner of the brute.
And over the whole thing the fiendish smile of Ikev
spreads horribly. The Eyeglass trouble is only one
of thousands which annually sap the public’s coin,
and it shrieks to the N.S.W. and Victorian Govern-
ments to come along witli a Totalisator Bill, and
thereby notify Noses to get work.
The balance-sheet of the A.J.C. (Sydney)
shows the fruit of the boom. The revenue from
admission-money exceeded the previous year’s total
by £11,000; and, as it was the club’s first effort to
collect the gate-money (up to this it had sold
the rights to Private Enterprise), the departure
panned out very well. The A.J.C. is piling £5550
on to the prize-money for the coming season, and is
offering some more accommodation to the public.
This is the English team which leaves for
Australia on September 29: P. F. Warner, F. R.
Foster, J. B. Hobbs, H. Strudwick, Douglas (Essex),
S. P. Einneir, E. J. Smith, J. W. Hearne, J. Ire-
monger, G. Gunn, W. R. Rhodes, F. E. Woolley,
C. P. Mead, J. Vine and S. F. Barnes. Of this lot
Mead tops the batting averages with 51.38; Kin-
neir follows (49.06); and then comes Foster with
43.63. Foster’s bowling average (19.67) is the
second best in England to-day, and Wilfrid Rhodes
(23.43) follows him. ®
The team comes without the star amateurs, but it
has a solid body of professionals, and on paper has
the makings of a better side than the one which,
under the same captain (Plum Warner), annexed
the ashes in 1903-4. Further, Australian cricket
is (on paper) not so strong as it was eight years
ago. Without Noble, with Hill doubtful, and with
Cotter making flesh, Australia hasn’t won the
rubber before the games start.
Warner has already been to Australia twice as
skipper of an English XI. In 1902 he was captain
of Lord Hawke’s “picnic” team, which toured
Maoriland and played a few matches in Australia.
The following year he brought out a team good
enough to bag the ashes. Of that combination only
Rhodes and Strudwick are again included. Hobbs,
of Surrey, is the only other member that has visited
Australia before.
* * * *
C. Yon del- Luft (Vic.) and H. Rumball
(N.S.W.) treated the crowds who patronised the
sixth Australasian amateur billiard championship
match to a ding-dong battle. The rep. from the
land; of the Weary Government played more con-
sistently than his opponent, and finally won by 135
points. In the game of 3000, Von der Luft’s average
worked out at 13.10 per cue, while Rumball’s efforts
panned out at The tallest break (131) was
credited to Rumball; but it was the young un’s con-
sistency that did the trick. On behalf of N.S.W.
Rumball won the title in 1906 and 1909, and Bel-
field in 1907 and 1908 ; but Victoria is getting a
rush on, owing to A. Tricks’s success last year and
Von der Luft taking the bauble at his first try.
That enthusiastic Australian, Mark Foy,
writing from London, winds up a list of “champion-
ships we now hold,” with this: “And now, in yacht-
ing, we hold the Anglo-Australian Shield.” The
armor is in Mark Foy’s own keeping. Unfortunately,
he didn’t have a race for it —he got it by default.
Foy ;had had the Southerly Buster built on Sydney
Harbor models for the express purpose of racing
for the shield ; and, to show what Australia could do,
he had the boat built from keel to truck in the
Commonwealth. The Medway Club, however, said
that the Shield was to be raced for by seven-metre
boats, and wanted him to build another craft to
suit English ideas. Foy protested that the club had
previously built the Maid of Kent specially to down
his Trex, and the Maid had done it; now, when he
bad put his experience into the job of winning the
Shield back, they wanted to tie him up and race
with what suited them. The rules were finally pro-
duced, measurements of the Southerly Buster made
—and then the Shield was handed over, and, says
Mr. Foy, “I received it on behalf of the Sydney
Flying Squadron and Australia.” He concludes:
“Now, they say there is no agreement to the effect
that they must go to Australia to race; but surely
that is understood. And, anyhow, my club will
never agree to anything else, I am sure. I am ex-
tremely sorry to have to take the Shield without a
contest for it, but I tried everything in my power to
get a race, and I think would have won anyhow. I
also think they thought so too, and, therefore, would
not go to the expense and then lose the Shield as
well. ... It would have cost me a considerable
amount of money to bring the boys to England to
sail the Buster, and I think I will form that into a
fund for the future defence of the Shield.” Good
sport!
The second Rugby Union tussle between
N.S.W. and Queensland, at Sydney, was a repetition
of the initial kick-up. The early half was well
contested, with the scores at 16 to 10 in favor of
N.S.W. ; but, after the interval, the Ma players piled
up goals and tries till the tally reached 34 to 15.
In the first match N.S.W. won by 34 points to 14 ;
and the aggregate of 68 points to 29 represents
pretty fairly the difference between the teams. It
was the 88th match between the States; N.S.W. has
won Queensland 22, and 4 have been drawn.
The Northern (N.S.W.) Rugby League
team (which recently trampled on Queensland)
rubbed a “Metropolitan XIII.” in the slush at Syd-
ney, last week, in a strikingly even game. The first
section ended 18 all, and, after a vigorous second
half, the game ended: Northern 31, Metropolitan 24.
The feature of the match was the exceptional kick-
ing; Messenger (Met.) booted six goals, and Miller
(Northern) had a record of five goals and two tries.
The mighty “Dally” had, therefore, the new experi-
ence of being threatened at his own pet branch of
football.
Balmain will be able to open a jeweller’s
shop after the Soccer season. The Association Cup
reposes in the coaly suburb, which is also almost
out of danger in the Rawson Cup contest, and it has
something to the good in the struggle for the Gar-
diner Can. Saturday’s matches provided some close
finishes. Powerful beat Glebe (1 goal to nil) for the
late Governor’s bowl, and Sydney drew with Balmain
(1 goal each) for the Gardiner Dipper. Newtown
forfeited to Granville.
* * * *
On grounds covered with slush, Sydney
Rugby League footballers pushed each other about
vigorously on Saturday. It was a day for forward
rushes, and the burly players bogged in and rubbed
each other exultantly in the soup. The splashing
pulled down the shutters on the second round, and
incidentally shifted the premiership figures. South
Sydney and Balmain supplied the upheaval of the
afternoon, when the watersiders—who had previously
won only two matches out of 13 —trampled Souths
in the mud by 9 points to 3. Of the other matches,
Glebe accounted for Western Suburbs by 24 to nil;
Eastern Suburbs squelched North Sydney by 3 to nil;
Newtown tore over Annandale by 17 to 5. The re-
sult of the mud-dumping is to push Glebe to the top
of the table with 22 points, South Sydney and
Eastern Suburbs being next with 20 each. Others
don’t matter. A play-off between Eastern Suburbs
and South Sydney will be necessary to determine
Glebe’s adversaries for the final.
# Mr #
N.S.W. Rugby League footballers have
struck a new “enjoyment,” which the officials will
have to meet with an axe before trouble gets on the
doorstep. On Saturday players dipped one referee
in the mud at whistle-time, and the other only es-
caped through being smart enough to dodge into
the dressing enclosure. On the holiday, last week,
similar happenings were on the menu. From any
viewpoint the “fun” is the small end of trouble.
* * * *
The first semi-final supplied the fun
among the Australian Rulers in Sydney on Saturday,
when Y.M.C.A. and Newtown clashed at Erskineville
quagmire. It was a rood game for three quarters,
but in the final quarter the accumulation of the
earth on the ball was too much for Newtown, and
the Young Men prevailed by 50 points to 20, and
thereby secured their place in the final. The other
semi-final is down for argument between Sydney and
East Sydney next Saturday.
* * * *
Queensland Rugby Union XV. swam
better than Sydney Metropolitan XV. at the Univer-
sity bath on Saturday, and Ma’s boys went under by
12 points to 11. Bananaland, which led 8 to 3 at
half-time, struggled out with a one-point victory;
and when referee Swannell blew a long whistle at
the end, the bespattered players crawled off the
ground with half the oval attached to them.
* * * *
Saturday’s Melbourne ’oofball games
settled the hopes of various teams to be of the select
four in the finals. Could ’Ro.v beat Essendon, it still
had an outside chance of ousting ’Wood, so it bogged
in regardless. But all in vain. ’Don’s swiftness,
precision and method gave ’Roy no hope, and the
trouble ended with the “same old” securing 46
points to ’Roy’s 25. Even while the maroons were
doing their last dash, ’Wood was nullifying their
efforts by walloping Carlton decisively. The latter’s
splendid marking was discounted by its awful kick-
ing. Towards the end ’Wood lost its captain through
a sprained ankle, but that made no difference, for it
already had the Carlton 15- and 17-stoners beaten—-
’Wood 56 points, Carlton 41. The Students, who
had drawn so heavily on their Metropolitan Associa-
tion team that the latter let its match with Caul-
field go by default, kept their end up against Mel-
bourne till three-quarter time; then it went to
pieces, and Melb. won by 91 points to 29. Rich-
mond polished off Saints by 123 to 36, and South got
the surprise of its life at Geelong. It led at halh
time by 43 points to 18, but after that the sea-air,
or over-anxiety, or something, got on its nerves, and
Geelong came at the finish and upended its sprouting
ambitions by notching 72 points to South’s 63.
* * * *
Saturday’s games finished the first round
in the Ass., and left Essendon, North, P’ran and
Brunswick to fight out the finals. Port was level
with the Brickies on games, but ’Wick, which met
Preston that day, contemptuously piled Up 178
points to Preston’s 18, and thereby passed Port into
outer darkness. As to Preston, which lias won four
games in three years, the Ass. warned it last year,
and the boot should be applied now. The other dis-
turbances resulted in the defeat of P’ran bjr Essen-
don, of ’Town by North, of ’Cote by ’Scray, and of
Brighton by Port; but none of them had any rele-
vance.
* * * *
Though the Victorian League’s first round
doesn’t end till Saturday of this week, the positions
of the leaders cannot be altered, and for the third
year running Collingwood, Carlton, South and Essen-
don will argue the point in the finals. In the Ass.,
likewise, the same four as last year will discuss the
question. ’Roy, in the League, and Port in the Ass.,
made a great effort to gain places in the charmed
quartette, but it takes two and sometimes three
seasons to do that. Football is a game, but it is
also a matter of endurance, skill, brains and, above
all, training. It takes two or three seasons’ strenu-
ous endeavor to lick a mere score of young men into
anything approaching effective form. ' Yet there are
fools who think an effective defence force can be
taught to bivouac, march, manoeuvre and shoot in a
day.
* * * *
Five or six thousand people were present
at the Mehegan benefit—for that is all the fight
between him and Maorilander Charlie Griffin, on
Monday night, at the Stadium (Svdney) amounted to
—and came away satisfied that the Australian light-
weight will punch his w. ' into a pavable future.
Griffin is not the fighting Charlie of old—no fire, no
thump, no defence, nothing but good intentions and
game resolve. The entertainment lasted five rounds,
when Griffin, already in a dazed condition, was
floored. Mehegan fought for the body practically all
the time, striking once or twice dangerously low. It
was body punishment that Griffin never had a hope
of standing for long, and, as a matter of fact, he
should have been saved some of it by the towel being
sent in before the fifth round. There isn’t much
bright and lofty intelligence in pinning faith to
the obviously hopeless; in fact, there isn’t so much
as plain donkey intelligence in it.
The heavyweight disturbance between Bil-
lang and Tommy Burns’s protege, Jack Lester, will,
if Providence sees fit and proper, take place at the
Stadium next Saturday night; and, as a good deal
hangs to the issue in the immediate future, both
men may be expected to be as excessively disagree-
able as the Marquis of Queensberry’s rules of debate
will permit.
* * * *
Bob McCarthy (Bst. fought “Spider”
Elliott (7st. 91b.) at Melbourne Athletic Club’s hall
the other evening for the State bantam champion-
ship and certain emoluments. Elliott was much the
brighter boy, and showed McCarthy just how it
should be done, through about 16 rounds of willing
milling. In the course of the 48 minutes McCarthy
was the thankless recipient of many fancy knocks
and artful punches; but Bob is the sort that stub-
bornly declines to recognise his inferiority as a
fighter. He continued wading in as if nothing
were happening to him, and was rewarded for his
pertinacity. In the 18th Elliott collapsed to wear
and tear, and in the 19th was all out, and the
white-work floated over his recumbent form.
McCarthy is now champion small bird of the Weary
province.
Last week 63 N.S.W. teams plugged
away on the Randwick rifle range for the right to
fondle the A.N.A. Cup. The country shooters rolled
up forcibly, and teams from Junee, Young, Peak
Hill, Manilla, and other far-away places made the
trip. The competition was very keen, and ended in
a win for Parramatta No. 3, with 473 points out of
a possible 500; Parramatta No. 1 (470), Randwick
No. 1 (467), and Gunning (462) were not far be-
hind. The fact that so many riflemen faced the
damp conditions (so bad at times that the target
wasn’t visible), is an indication that there are some
Australians who consider that football barracking
is not the apex of citizenship. And here’s to ’em!
* * * *
N.S.W. amateur cross-country champion-
ship at Randwick racecourse, on Saturday, was
worth a long journey to see. Six “A” grade teams,
of nine members each, were despatched by Lands
Minister Flowers on a five-mile journey over 40 ob-
stacles of various kinds; and Newtown was the only
band which didn’t finish with sufficient numbers to
be recognised. The battle for the individual cham-
pionship provided the best finish in these contests
for many years, and it was only in the last sprint
that T. J. Wood (E. Sydney) shook off 0. Weyman
(Botany), and won by 4yds., with F. Flowers (Red-
fern) 7yds. away, and Hamilton (Toowong, Q.)
close at hand. The journey was traversed in 29min.
41Asec.—the fastest time since 1902. The adjust-
ment of placings gave the championship to Botany
with 48 points, Redfern (101), East Sydney (109),
South Sydney (118), and Toowong, Q. (165), being
next in order. The success of Botany keeps the title
in the club’s pantry for the fourth successive year;
and as T. J. Wood also gathered in the individual
honors last year, a porter won’t be needed to shift
the baubles.
The “B” grade spin followed, and Botany scored a
second easy victory with 62 points, while South
Sydney (123), Cumberland (138), Redfern (154),
and E. Sydney (207) supplied the joints of a drawn-
out _ tail. The bright spot was the fine run by J.
McKenzie (Y.M.C.A.), who won individual honors
easily in 31min. 22 4-ssec. ; and on the performance
“little Mac” must be treated with respect in future
championships.
* * * *
Victorian 10-mile cross-country champion-
ship, at Caulfield, on September 16, will have a
strong inter-State flavor. T. J. Wood and the Botany
Harriers (with the N.S.W. honors hanging to their
singlets) will join in the trouble on behalf of Ma ;
while Bananalander A. J. Luton will make the trip
to defend the title which he gathered in last year.
Tassy promises to forward two teams; and as Vic-
toria’s champions will also be on deck, the event
promises to be interesting.
* * * *
Holway, the Yankee runner, has collared
another championship. At the Sydney Agricultural
Ground, on Prince of Wales Birthday, he streaked
away from Maorilander Trembath in a run for the
440yds. world’s championship. Although the M.L.
goer led for the first half of the race, the Yank flew
past him, and won by syds., in 50 4-ssec. —good
going for heavy ground. On the same day Mrs.
Dpennan (holder) and Miss Moore (challenger) met
for the 100yds. ladies’ championship of Australia.
The spinster’s velocity was not equal to the maid’s,
and the maid won by 24yds. in 13 3-ssee.
* * * *
The Victorian lacrosse players didn’t bar-
gain for aquatic sport when they came to play
N.S.W., but they got it at Manly Oval on the day
Sydney observes as Prince of Wales’ Birthday. When
the two teams emerged from the slush, Vic. had
scored Bto Ma’s 5. Play throughout was second-
rate on account of the wet, but N.S.W., according
to the southern manager, shows a great improvement
in theory since its game with S. Aus. ; and on Satur-
day it defeated the visiting Bananalanders for the
first time in six years.
* * * *
Only one baseball match was played in
Sydney on Saturday, the other clubs keeping them-
selves dry in the pavilions. Lane Cove and Burwood
were not of the melting brand, and in a match
which was full of mud and fun, Lane Cove won by
14 runs to 4. The competition is now extremely
tangled, and the playing of a host of postponed
matches will be necessary to sort out those with
a “possible” in the final.
* * * *
The Taranaki (M.L.) circular cycling
road race was spun through in rotten weather.
Drizzling rain confronted the 65 plucky athletes
who faced the 104-miles run; and, after the
cyclists had been scraped, Jas. Clark (32min.) was
recognised as first man home and fastest time-
winner, followed by Greenless (37min.) and Stoddart
(50min.). Clark’s time was 6hr. 17min. 59 2-ssec.
The glory is supolemented by £5O and the right to
keep a £5O challenge mug in his cupboard.
C. J. Senior put up a good bike ride re-
cently, when he lowered the Westralian 25-mile record
to 63min. 3sec., thereby knocking lmin. 47sec. off
G;. Beardon’s effort of two years ago. During the
past three years Senior has won two 50-mile races,
besides first and fastest time prizes in the Beverley -
Perth 110-mile spin.
* * * *
N.S.W. amateur cyclist E. W. Schneider
ended his run of bad luck at Bath (Eng.) last week
by reducing the half-mile grass-track record to
64 l-ssec., and also gathering in the five-mile
scratch event in 13min. 44sec. The Australian has
probably recovered some of the dash which was
shown before he hurried from these parts; and it is
consoling to know that many English cycling snig-
gerers will have to gulp down the early silly criticism
with which they tantalised the Australian.
South Carolina (U.S.A.) Supreme Court
has just hit the motor “joy-rider” with a thunder-
bolt. Evidence showed that the owner of a car had
ordered it into the garage. Instead, the chauffeur
went for a midnight spin with some pals, and, inci-
dentally, bumped a horse and buggy. Nobody was
hbrt, but the whole festive bunch was arrested, tried
for “conspiracy,” and sentenced to five years’ gaol.
The ruling, which has caused a sensation in the
States, was that “conspiracy” consisted of several
persons doing an unlawful act which affected the
public or individual (the car-owner), or doing a
lawful act (motoring) by unlawful means (in a for-
bidden ear). After this, the Carolina chauffeur’s
pals will most likely drop midnight motoring with
great suddenness. 6
Still, the chauffeur who knows his way about
and most of them do—will generally find some com
in Lgypt if he stands well with a garage. The gar-
age s usual gag, especially for newehums at the
motoring game, is: “After we have fixed her up we
may have to give her a bit of a run to see that she
goes smoothly.” Nineteen times out of 20 the
owner says “Certainly! ” and then anything mav
happen. In fact, if the garage isn’t above that sort
of thing, the car may be hired out for a few odd
days.
* * * *
The last letter from Birtles, the over-
landing cyclist, who is making a trip round Aus-
tralia with a cinematograph outfit, comes from Nor-
manton (up where the Gulf of Carpentaria would
spill itself if it weren’t locked in). He seems to be
getting plenty of excitement. At Cloncurry he was
out with a shooting party, when a local shot’s rifle
went off, and the bullet grazed Birtles’s back, and
scratched his elbow ; then he trod on a catfish, and
had trouble with a wild boar and got mixed up in
a buggy smash. From Normanton he makes Wynd-
ham, in the north of Westralia; and in November
he will try to break the 30 days’ record from Perth
to Sydney.
* # * £
The Sydney Bike and Motor Cycle Club
has decided to send a rep. to England to compete in
next year’s Tourist Trophy motor-cycle race. Tests
will be held in N.S.W. to choose Mother’s boy, and
the lucky one will leave for England in March.
The motor-cycle race for the world’s cham-
pionship resulted in a win for De Rosier, of America.
The first event was a willing one. Collier, from
England, kept in front till 100yds. from the end.
when the Yank dashed past him, and won by three
lengths. His time for the five miles was 4min.
2 4-ssec. —an average of 80.59 miles per hour. In
the second go, while he was travelling at 80 miles
per hour, De Rosier’s front tyre came off, and Collier
won, the miles taking him lOmin. 12sec., an
average of 79.9 miles per hour. Collier’s machine
misfired in the third race; De Rosier’s time for the
27 miles was 20min. 44 2-ssec., an average of 78. G
miles per hour.

In the Vie. Motor Cycle Club’s 25-mile
road race on Saturday—l2J miles out and home
along the north-eastern road from Craigieburn—H.
Russell lowered the State’s record for the distance from
30min. 30sec. to 27min. 58 2-ssec. This looks prom-
ising should Jake de Rosier come out td slither round
the F.S. Gardens new asphalt track.
* * * *
“Billy” Murdoch’s pioneer work for Aus-;
tralian cricket is to be recognised with a memorial
by the N.S.W. Cricket Association ; ■ and the small
outlay will keep green the memory of a sound
cricketer and a good sport.
Maoriland five-mile cross-country cham-
pionship was struggled through at Auckland, when
a bunch of athletes careered from Ellerslie race-
course to Alexandra Park. Throughout the journey,
Hill(Auckland) led an elastic band of athletes, which
stretched more as the distance was traversed, and at
the tape there were 75yds. between Beatson and Not-
man (Otago). Time, 32min. 15sec. The teams
championship fell to Otago, with Auckland and Well-
ington next.
* * * *
Ooraki (N.S.W.) begins to put a bit on
itself as an aquatic centre. As an inducement to the
top-notch ers to float themselves at the local regatta
on November 1, £lOO is offered for an all-comers’
best-and-best event. The village claims this to be
the record regatta prize of the State, Sydney not
barred. Seeing that it contains under 1500 souls,
Coraki is an enthusiastic place, anyway.
* * * *
Vic. Judge Moule, when recently asked to
grant an insolvent Ikey Noses a certificate of dis-
charge, refused to do so, though there were no ob-
jecting creditors, on the ground that insolvent had
wasted his substance in riotous gambling, book-
making being, he decided, a gamble, not a business.
For once Moule was wrong. The essence of all
gambling is that both bettors must have a chance,
whereas, with Ikey’s current odds, the backer has
in the long run no chance whatever. So, if this
Ikey smashed, it was because he went outside his
bookmaking job, or didn’t do the job properly.
The question whether Tarrant, formerly of
Melbourne, now of the Middlesex professionals, should
be included in the English team to play against
Australia and S’Afn'ca, was still being keenly dis-
cussed in London when the last mail left. The
Referee concludes an article: “Lord Harris points
out that the laws of cricket enable Tarrant to play
for this country. No laws can transmogrify an Aus-
tralian into an Englishman.” The Observer says:
“Tarrant is to all intents and purposes an Australian
cricketer, and it is scarcely fair to England’s home-,
bred talent, or to the Australian school of cricket,
to clothe him in the raiment of a cricketer born and
bred in England.” Though that puts Tarrant in an
awkward position, it seems to sum up the general
opinion.

Also at Melbourne (& Brisbane
S. Australia’s most eccentric parson has
a seaside church, where on a hot Sunday
evening last summer he was faced, by only
seven faithful members of the flock. After
ticking them off carefully he startled the little
remnant (which expected to be told that,
dearly beloved, we will now join in singing
hymn number 217) by saying: “Friends,
owing to the smallness of the congregation
there will be no sermon this evening. Those
of you who want to do so may now go down
on the beach —you will find the others, I ex-
pect, on the seaweed.”
The Welshman seized the recent oppor-
tunity to assert himself as the most bubbly
of British subjects. When his bosom swells
with national pride and love of freedom, the
spirit usually moves Taffy to go for all he
can get. It is just that which makes Lloyd
George the most painful Chancellor of the
Exchequer that English Propputy has ever
had to suffer. He isn’t so much the poor
man’s friend as the rich man’s foe; and, if
British Propputy never knew before, it
knows now the real meaning of “giving ’em
Taffy.”

“Maris Stella” : Of course, you know that
the Australian bush breeds clods—just dull
clods, with no aspirations, no anything; the
chaps whose legs the superior city pulls at
Cup and Show time. Well, here’s some evi-
dence in the case. Orbost is a little bush
village in far and very bushy East Gipps-
land. It is two days’ journey from a rail-
way station, and if a muster was made of all
its inhabitants, men, women and children,
600 would cover the total. This village of
Orbost is not content with the ordinary
State school, good as it is, with a line stall’.
It must have a High, or Continuation,
School. A deputation of one interviewed the
Director of Education, and offered to find
£lOO toward the building of a school. Tate
declined. What about £200? No! £300?
No! “Well, what about £500?“ “Bring
me £5OO and the school’s yours,”
laughed the director. The deputation of one
returned to Orbost, called a meeting of
parents and villagers, told the result of the in-
terview, and opened the list with £5O. An-
other donation of £5O came immediately.
Two others of £25 followed. In two hours
they had £550 collected —£550 collected in a
village with less than 100 houses. I reckon
that's the sanest thing I ever heard of in col-
lections. I’ve put my little bit towards
many things in my time—funds for soldiers’
monuments, for burying policemen, for giv-
ing depressed politicians a send-off, for pre-
senting Papuan ladies with pink dresses, but
never have I so willingly tossed in my little
bit, as I did to the Orbost fund.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
J-H.S.: You “put things plainly so that
even the ‘worker’ may understand.” The Bulle-
tin s idea is that 3 r ou put them so crookedly that
even the vyorker may be misled. (1) “Early this
month C.S.R. shares were quoted at £45, and at that
price they were paying a dividend at the rate of
£4 Bs. lOd. p.c. Increased expenses would mean
reduced dividends, and a corresponding slump in the
market value of the shares.” The market price of
the shares has no more bearing on the merits of the
case than the price of mummies or boot-laces. Those
£45 shares are £2O paid. That is to say, the
public pays £4 Bs. lOd. p.c. on £2O of capital, and
the same on £25 of Stock Exchange “water.” Why
should the public pay interest on “water”? You
are a lawyer ; and if some fool of a stationer chose
to pay £lO per stick for sealing-wax, would you feel
yourself bound to pay him £lO, plus his profit for
the goods? As a matter of fact, the C.S.R. Co., on
the capital actually subscribed by shareholders, is
paying over IS p.c. (2) It wasn’t “admitted” that
all the sugar workers could earn £5 or £6 by piece-
work. If every man went on the piece-work jobs,
the whole industry would stop. Mill-work is as
essentially a weekly-wage job as domestic service is,
and the co. never offered piece-work prices for it.
(3) The Bulletin, by cartoon and otherwise, op-
posed the abolition of the Protective sugar duty.
This, however, has nothing to do with the row—ex-
cept to show that you don’t know the facts about
any single phase of it Jaimy: Keep clear
Pinnaroo: No; The Bulletin's cheeks have always
been pink.... Abre: Hordern’s fire was first; don’t
remember date Franky F.: The heathen will
have to do without their toast-racks Con R.:
His son Brown: Neat; but — Unknown:
Sorry unsuitable... .T.G. (Q.): An avalanche of
jagged sentences. Fortunately we side-stepped in
time, and, leaning on our fountain-alpenstocks,
watched the mass go roaring down into the basket.
....Q. Quill: very mild love yarn. No grip....
Hakea: Some grace, but not enough skill... .F.E.D.:
Nancy will not gather posies here. Give her a new
rig-out and implore her to do her hair, and you
might find a place for her in some quiet poet’s
corner... .H.R. (V.) : “Noises” pleasant, but hope-
less. ... .C.A.S.: Rejected without a pang; .F.A.W.:
Fairly good first attempt; but it is some way below
publication standard. Try to score off your own
bat. . . .A.E.G.: Crude work. Forty-one and a-half
verses are a trifle too much. Make an effort to pack
the idea into 39....Zeit: Very well, we won’t send
you any money for it... .Bellerive: Tragedy resumed
at the old address:—
AN INDIAN ASSASSIN.
Having learned of the sins
Of his Asiatic wife,
An Indian vowed to
Put an end to her Life;
While she lay hushed in slumber
In her sleeping-room,
In a diabolical manner
He sealed her doom ;
He emerged from the darkness
And turned the key in the door,
And piteously shrieked the victim
As fierce flames did roar;
For his crime the assassin
Sacrificed his own' life—
He perished in the flames
With his ill-fated Wife.

tune. .. .Doc.: Try another prescription. Patient
much worse.... E. O’B.: Plain bush jingle. Too
rough.... Armis: Rather neatly turned: —
I know a girl that’s in a bar,
So quiet and demure; .
I thought her just a guiding star,
And she loved me, I felt sure.
One day I entered, for a taste;
A young dude with a leer
Had his arm around her waist,
So I had bitter beer.

wall in the old homestead bj' the creek, where the
pines grow so tall and the people speak in hushed

lowed you fairly well until you got to the whited

Though the lamentable ex-Crown Prose-
cutor of N. S. Wales brands as liars sundry
humane school-inspectors, teachers, Scotch
farming commissioners, clergymen, labor or-
ganisers, and travellers of all brands, he
will perhaps admit the evidence of a cow
cocky who butts into an argument on
the matter in Tweed (N.S.W.) Times
(26/7/T1). Says this witness, Robert Gil-
lies :
Why should I blush to announce myself a dairy
farmer? For this reason: The most degraded and
spineless creatures I have ever met were dairymen.
Certainly there are exceptions.... One farmer, near
Brooklet, used to feed his 12s. 6d.-a-week man on
the bull calves; the man left because the calves
were getting ahead of him, and were tied up and
starved till he was ready for them. And I’ve seen
more than one Vernon boy out after the cows at 3
o’clock in the morning, barefooted, with a lantern,
in order to get to the factory and have first dip at
the skim milk tank. Another pioneer and grabber
I saw get out of his buggy on the way to church to
pluck the ears off a half rotten padymelon because
scalps were worth 3d. a time then. These are the
men who are independent to-day, and cry out loudest
about artificial wages—men who have made them-
selves out of some other poor devil’s sweat.
If Mr. Gillies isn’t careful, he will be get-
ting unpopular among his fellow cow-
cpckies.

HIRAM CARPENTER’S
WONDERFUL CURE
or SKIN DISEASE
"I have been afflicted for twenty years
with an obstinate skin disease, called by some
M. D.’s. psoriasis, and others leprosy, com-
mencing on my scalp; and in spite of all I
could do, with the help of the most skilful
doctors, it slowly but surely extended until a
year ago this winter it covered my entire
person in the form of dry scales. For the
last three years I have been unable to do any
labor, and suffering intensely all the time.
Every morning there would be nearly a dust-
nful of scales taken from the sheet on my
, some of them half as large as the enve-
lope containing this letter. In the latter part
of winter my skin commenced cracking open.
I tried everything, almost, that could be
thought of, without any relief. The 12th of
June I started West, in hopes I could reach
the Hot Springs. 1 reached Detroit and was
so low I thought I should have to go to the
hospital, but finally got as far as Lansing,
Mich., where I had a sister living. One
Dr. treated me about two weeks, but did
me no good. All thought I had but a short
time to live. I earnestly prayed to die.
Cracked through the skin all over my back,
across my ribs, arms, hands, limbs; feet badly
swollen; toe-nails came off; finger-nails dead
and hard as a bone; hair dead, dry and lifeless
as old straw. Omy God! how I did suffer.
“My sister wouldn’t give up; said, ‘We
will try Cuticura.’ Some was applied on one
hand and arm. Eurekal there was relief;
stopped the terrible burning sensation from
the word go. They immediately got Cuticura
Resolvent, Ointment and Soap. I commenced
by taking Cuticura Resolvent three times a
day after meals; had a bath once a day, water
about blood heat: used Cuticura Soap freely;
applied Cuticura Ointment morning and even-
ing. Result: returned to my home in just six
weeks from the time I left, and my skin as
smooth as this sheet of paper. Hiram E.
Carpenter, Henderson, N. Y., U. S. A.”
The above remarkable testimonial was
written January 19, 1880, and is republished
because of the permanency of the cure.
Under date of April 22, 1910, Mr. Carpenter
Wrote from his present home, 610 Walnut
St. So., Lansing, Mich., U. S. A.: “I have
never suffered a return of the psoriasis and
although many years have passed I have
not forgotten the terrible suffering I endured
before using the Cuticura Remedies.”

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Klifton” : A long narrative by the auctioneer re-
lative to the horse’s beautiful shape, its sprightly
movements, etc., was followed by a bid of £5. The
auctioneer tried hard to get an advance, but in vain
—-none of the local butchers seemed to be operating
this day. “Well, gentlemen,” he said at last, “1
can’t waste any more time on this lot. Can I obtain
any advance on five? At five; going at five, going,
going—” He was just about to smite the table
when Sid Kidman drifted in, threw a critical glance
at the crock, and exclaimed: “Hold on, I’ll give you
five-and-six for him!”
* * * *
“Button-stick”: I read lately of a procession of
full-grown bulls of assorted ages through the streets
of a Maoriland freezing town. They were on their
I way to the works, to be patted on the head with a
I pole-axe, and shipped to the land where the bands
still play “The Roast Beef of Old England.” I
wouldn’t grumble if the indiarubber beef was to be
planted on the general public, for if the English
’ousewife is chump enough not to know bull-beef
when it’s introduced to her —well, her troubles, to
say nothing of her husband’s ! But the M.L. paper
states calmly that the bull-beef goes to fill army con-
tracts, and that one of the clauses in army meat con-
tracts is that one-third of the supply may be bull-,
beef. Alas, my poor brothers of the button-stick in
1 John Bull-beef land! I remember that a cheer used
to pass round when the day’s rations were drawn and
the word went along that it was “brown bully to-day”
instead of the usual “red bully.” The brown was
Australian and the red was American, and they were
so christened because both the label and the, beef
were brown in the one and red in the other. The
| Australian “bully” had a distinct flavor, with a real
I taste of beef or mutton, and was not too salt. The
Yankee stuff was devoid of the faintest taste of
meat, resembled a boiled-up mass of Strings and fat,
was swamped in spice and pepper to give it some
flavor, and was so salt that unless there was a river
handy it was risking a day’s torture by thirst to eat
it. But if this country and M.L. are going to ring
in their old bust-up bulls, I’m afraid the reputation
of the Australian “brown-bully” will slump.
# ■Jt #
“Boozy Dick”; Re that mouse in the Melbourne
lady’s pie. I called for a feed of steak and oyster in a
Dago’s fish emporium a while ago. When Homer’s
countryman brought the feast I found a cockroach
had wandered into the sauce. When I drew An-
tonioso’s attention to the funeral in my mildest
manner, he promptly whipped the corpse away, and
returned with: “It is alia right now.” But I do wish
he had Wiped his finger first.
“Quidam”: Have invented a new use for the phono-
graph. By taking a three-minute record, the machine
can be converted into a thoroughly practical and
effective scientific apparatus for timing the boiling
of eggs. When the music stops off comes the egg.
Could anything be simpler? Personally, I regard it
as a greater invention than wireless telegraphy or
paper-bag cookery; and, for the greater benefit of
mankind, it is not patented.
* * * *
Maifra (Vic.) beet sugar factory, in Labor member
McLaughlan’s district, promises to be one of the
best of the Socialistic ventures of the Weary Govern-
ment. Ityer, the Californian manager, has set 15
such factories go inf in the Yankee West, and looks
like lifting this local white elephant out of the mud.
Some of the factory’s strong supporters are, as usual,
strenuous Anti-Soshers in theory ; but they like the
Sosh they’ve got so much that they never see a
Minister without biting his ear for some more of the
Curse.
* * * *
“Billy Mug”; “Huffin Guffin” (B. 3/8/’ll) doesn’t
know much about the newsagenev business. He has
worked out a nice little sum to prove that the agent
gets 6s. 3d. for every 50 penny-paper customers, pays
the actual runner 35., and hangs on to 3s. 3d. for
doing nothing, or thereabouts. But he has forgotten
the customer who doesn’t pay at all, and on my
own experience I reckon that that brigand absorbs
one-fifth of the agent’s supposed profit. And when
he has provided for that brigand he has also to
provide for another one, overlooked by “Huffin” —
the one who calls with a rent-book on Monday
mornings. * * *
The outcry against the use of osprey plumes has
led to a ch'eekv fraud. Finding that the sales of
plumes were falling off, British and other European
swindlers announced that a means had been dis-
covered of making an artificial osprey plume so per-
fect that detection was impossible. Here was a
chance for lovely woman to gratify her vanity and
preserve her self-respect. She started wearing the
bogus decorations in large quantities. Then a
number of bird-papers became suspicious, and a
scientific investigation proved that the “imitation”
was the real article.
A TALE OF BILL AND JOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The British Ambassador is conferring' with the
French Foreign Office as to which part of the Congo
country should be given by France to Germany in
exchange for a free hand being allowed to France
in Morocco. —Recent cable.
While Burglar Bill on burgling feet
Was slouching round his nightly beat,
An ancient pal he chanced to meet
In valiant Bludger Joe.
“Wot’s up, my bloke?” said Bill, irate—
“ Wot cranky notion’s in your pate,
That you should dare to wi-o-late
My bizness quarter so?”
The Bludger’s dander promptly rose
(He never feared the hostile blows
Of traps and law-abiding foes —
He gave and took his fill).
“O Bill,” says he, “this bloomin’ street
Will suit, I think, my lovely feet
As well as fit the tootsies neat
Of bullyin’ Burglar Bill.”
As when two fierce hyenas scowl,
With mutual hate and mutual growl,
Who chance to cross in nightly prowl
Each other’s quest of prey,
So did this fierce marauding pair
Upon each other grimly glare,
And stand with threatening jimmies there
Intent on bloody fray;
Until a notion struck B. Bill.
Says he: “I have no wicked will
In foolish fight the blood to spill
Of you, old Bludger Joe;
If you my quarter will wa-cate,
I give you leave to burgle straight
The precious spoons and silver-plate
In Bergman’s Golden Row.
“You need but round the corner go,
And there’s the stuff you’d like to stow” —
“Praise Gawd from whom all blessin’s flow!”
Cried out enraptured Joe;
“To crack that Row I make a start —
You are, O Bill, a gen’rous heart;
You always take a comrade’s part,
And never spile his show!”
And so they parted, well content,
And when the night was nearly spent.
Beneath a load of plunder bent
These heroes met again.
Said they: “It was this statesmanship
As gave us coves a useful tip,
And saved us from a foolish slip
And breaking heads in vain!”
Envoi.
Of course when Deutschland says to France:
“No longer will you proudly prance,
And bid your legions fierce advance
Against the Moorish foe,
Unless you show where / come in,
And grab my share of land and tin”—
One must not think with wicked grin
Of Bill and Bludger Joe.

BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
OR the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
r 1 Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

BRISBANE.
Townsville, Toowoomba and London.
O.T. LIMITED.
Tlie fourth half-yearly meeting of shareholders in
O.T. Limited was held at the company’s offices on
August 23. Mr. J. Dixon, chairman of directors,
presided. The accounts showed a net profit of
£5105 17s. 7d. for the half-year ended June 30,
and a dividend at the rate of 15 per cent, per annum
was declared. The chairman, in moving the adop-
tion of the accounts, said that, owing to the
large increase in trade, it had now become
necessary to manufacture in Sydney as well as in
Melbourne, and that it would be necessary to at once
add to the present Melbourne factory, in order to
cope with the increasing demand for the company’s
lines. The trade for July this year was 92 per cent,
over the sales for July, 1910. In referring to the
new issue of shares, he remarked that in taking over
the world’s rights, the prospects of the company
were practically unbounded, as the company had the
sole right to the name “0.T:,” and the exclusive
manufacture of the beverage. The report and ac-
counts were adopted.*
The King of the Belgians is an ardent and practi-
cal motorist, and displays a close personal interest
in cars and their construction, also in accessories, as
is evidenced by his choice of tyres—the well-known
Avons of British manufacture.*

IN
BANANA LAND
The Brisbane Scotchbyterians are going
to establish a residential college “within” the
Bananaland ’Varsity, and have secured
Charles Lilley’s old home as a site for it.
There is an appropriateness about this; for
no one worked harder than the good Old
democrat to get a ’Varsity for Queensland.
He was chairman of the Commission which
reported in favor of its establishment.
It is not only N.S.W. school inspectors
and teachers who complain of the slavery of
the Cow. Bananaland’s inspector Kennedy
drops this into his official report:—
Home work has to be reduced to a minimum in
the dairying sections of the Brisbane and Gympie
districts on account of parents’ demands on their
children. ... A boy of 10 or 12 years arrived late
at school on inspection-day. He had been helping
at milking since daybreak, and was so tired that he
fell asleep sitting in school.
Poor little slave —too young to have a vote,
and too insignificant, therefore, to have the
attention of office-hungry politicians.
Bananaland, which begins to suffer from
the exhilarated feeling that comes with the
sixth drink or so, is credited with harboring
an idea, on the part of its State legislators,
that £5OO a year isn’t a penny too much for
their services. It certainly wouldn’t be any
too much to pay legislators if they were
w-anted. But when Queensland pays out its
£5OO a nob, it ought to reflect that there is
a National body which could comfortably do
two-thirds of the work, and do it better;
while the other one-third could be much
better done by two or probably three small
local one-llouse bodies representing south-
ern, central and north Bananaland.
The Queensland squatters are up. against
the carriers just now, so they are going to
do away with the bullocky and run motor-
waggons. Two or three cos. to that end
have been formed, so it is reported. But,
even if the whole thing isn’t a bluff, the
motors will want capable drivers, and those
drivers will be more easily organised into a
solid union than the bullockies are. More-
over, when the bullocky goes on strike he
has to feed his bullocks and stand the loss
of earnings; whereas when the big strike of
motor-men comes, there will be a heap of in-
terest accumulating all the time on the wag-
gons which were paid for by the squatters.
If it isn’t bluff, Longwood may yet be heard
wailing for the return of “the good old
days” of Spot and Strawberry.
The Bridal Shower.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out glass, china, furniture and all sorts of useful
and ornamental gifts were showered upon the happy
couple.— Brisbane daily.
The happy couple lightly left the door
Of her paternal (or maternal) mansion;
Biz heart was full —and hers! Nay, beg no more
To hear the story of its glad expansion ;
They left her home, the world was all before them,
When sudden (saith our journal) tumbled o'er them
A novel shower of fans and opera-glasses,
Of Paris gloves and priceless bric-a-brac,
Of carven Buddhas, ornamental brasses
And gauds of ivory carved with cunning k’>ack;
No place Was there for paltry wares and petty
In all this hail of wonderful confetti.
Cut glass came down with many a dainty barrel
Designed to hold preserves or biscuit fine,
And all the splendor of the bride’s apparel
Was dimmed by missiles in the morning shine;
Oh, ne’er a lass, since Eve was A bm’s idol,
Was handled half so madly at l.cr bridal.
What olden myth of orgies Bacc' malian
Was ever half so strange a tale as this
Plain story of a marriage—all Australian
From Wedding March to mother’s parting kiss,
When silver spoons and volumes bound in vellum
Came hurtling on the bridegroom’s cerebellum!
A marble clock, with vases intermingled,
Came chiming through that lovely fusillade,
Aud sundry silver trifles gaily jingled
And lent the shower a sweet, harmonious aid ;
And once a curious inlaid Chinese table
Sped flying like a brick from ancient Babel.
How shall man venture in the marriage medley,
If, on the day he ties the fatal knot,
His shoulders sag beneath the missiles deadly
Of wedding guests who heave their little lot
Of presents on the hapless new-wed mortal,
And drop him like a bullock at the portal?
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 26, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The Statham-Thorn marriage and pre-
liminaries have been eating up what reserve
force we had left after the Carnival-week
frivols. Enthusiastic girl pals rallied around
the bride-elect, and, in her honor, worked
the teapot overtime every day of the week. The
most notable party was that given by
the Taylor cousins, of Belle Vue, which was
attended by the Vision and the pick of the
Very Best. The wedding noose was knotted
firmly by the Archbishop of Brisbane and
Dean Pattinson, the bride bearing up in
ninon and satin, and exquisite Brussels lace.
The Vision was in pale blue foulard, and a
hat with a bird of paradise roosting a-top.
INGRATITUDE.
FRIEND: “Hullo, Isaacs! I hear you haf disowned your son Jacob. Vateffer is der matter?"
ISAACS: “Yes, der foot! I haf no patience with him. I start him in pizness, und he goes
und pays offices mit a swimming bath on der floor upove, und a fire station
over der road."
The Government House clan is still whirl-
ing after meetings, sports gatherings,
Y.M.C.A. objects, dances, and other pagan
pastimes. The other day the Chieftain de-
scended upon the Institution for the Blind,
Deaf and Dumb, talked about life saving
and the wisdom of teaching the young
to swim, and dragged in his pet sub-
ject—the proposed Presbyterian College for
the baby ’Varsity. Principal Harper, of
Sydney, came north to explain how col-
leges should be served up, and Wee Mac-
gregor passed him the hand of welcome and
the word of support.
We haven’t yet decided where the per-
manent University and Government House
are to be, although proposals come thick and
fast, and the Premier turns the open ear to
each. The latest idea is to plant the ’Var-
sity at Yeronga, pack up the Government
House gee-gaws, shift them to Bowen
Bridge, and . allow the Technical College to
spread its growing limbs in the vacated
University buildings, which used to be Gov-
ernment House. Grant, Minister of Educa-
tion, is said to favor Yeronga for the ’Var-
sity, but whether his views will count is
another matter.
Died on Wednesday night, J. G. Ander-
son, 1.5.0., for many years Under-Secretary
for Education, who was as unostentatious
as he was kind, and as kind as he was just.
His family numbers three, the eldest of
whom is the wife of Canon Ashton, of St.
Andrew’s, South Brisbane. Incidentally
Canon Ashton, with wife and five sturdy
little Australians, will leave shortly for St.
Kilda(Vic.), where he has been appointed to
the living, and a bit over. Dr. Arthur
Anderson, who practises in Goondiwindi,
and Miss Edith Anderson, an artist of merit,
who studied for some years in London and
Paris, complete the family circle.
Mrs. John Hicks hostessed two gatherings
during the week —the first, a dance for young
people in honor of the coming marriage of
Mr. Jack Petrie (a scion of the Queens-
land Petries) and Miss Dorothy Ryder; the
second, an afternoon tea, to enable Mrs.
Ball (of Canada) to swap “Howdy” with
local Sassiety dames. The hostess, caught
in the prevailing epidemic of decorations,
passed the damper round amongst scarlet
poppies and wheat.
Mrs. Hutton was another of the week’s
hostesses. She collected nearly 100 young
men and maidens, turned her lovely old
house upside down, and set them dancing
merrily on the floors, and eating heartily in
a marquee. And if young men and maidens
cannot enjoy themselves in those conditions
they must have changed since our time, my
dear. Daughter Lynnie wore a striking
frock of pale grey, over coral pink.
A shock ran through the town on Thurs-
day, when the news of the death of Mrs. Carl
Palmer, the girl wife of the eldest son of
the late Lieut.-Gov. Arthur Palmer, and
eldest daughter of Dr. Lightoller, became
known. She had been married just a year
and, besides husband and friends, leaves a
tiny little daughter a week old. I don’t
know who controls these things, but I’ve got
some strong views about the management.
The Gay Gordons include Mr. Frank
Brookes and Mr. Cecil Houghton, both old
Brisbane boys. Frank Brookes was married
on Monday, at All Saints’, to the charming
little lady with whom he warbles the lovey-
dovey duet. Their romance is one which
might almost have been put down to legerde-
main, for, within eight days of their first
meeting, the two were made one.
Mrs. Frost and her musical offspring
attended to the musicianly soul and the
creature comforts of several of our local
talent recently in honor of our Mabel Wool-
cock, a contralto with a voice to be proud of.
It has had years of Melbourne Conserva-
toire polish, and has taken it like the very-
best Australian timbre. Henry Saint George,
of Trinity College, and Percy Brier, our
home-grown Fellow of the same institution,
played their very own comoositions in their
very own way, and assisted the merry little
hostess in making the party a huge success.
The catmittee of one hospital had its fur
rubbed backways recently as the result of a
lady doctor’s objection to a meal-time regu-
lation. This regulation sets out that the
resident’s tucker shall be taken with the
nursing staff; but the newcomer was alleged
to have told a friend that she was “not going
to feed with nurses,” and naturally it wasn’t
long before the alleged remark had worked
round to the ears of a horrified catmittee
and nursing staff. The ensuing excitement
was only quelled by the hon. medical staff
bringing masculine hard sense to play and
hinting at strike and other rude methods
copied in later years by the coalhumpers'
union. All is peace again, but I can’t say
anything about goodwill.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Brisbane’s Mayoress (Mrs. Diddams)
after her experience of Hospital Saturday"
congratulated Brisbaneites cordially on their
generosity. An analysis of the contents of
the collection boxes reveals the interesting
fact that nearly 20,000 coppers were given
on that eventful day. Generosity? Well
ask of a pub-keeper what he’d think of a har-
vest of copper.
Banan'aland friendly societies are quiver-
ing just now in a fever and ague of appre-
hension. The other day a northern Oddfel-
lows’ lodge discovered a shortage of £2OOO in
its accounts—a deficiency which resulted in
an arrest. A few days later another big
branch of the same society found that it was
about £lO,OOO to the bad, owing apparently
to somebody else’s defalcations. And these
are not the only shortages in the friendly
society atmosphere just now. The epidemic
has arrived. A borrowing boom always
brings it.
The North Coast Railway, which is about
to be started, will have the longest bridge in
Banana land. It will cross the Burdekin
River, and will have 20 spans of 120 ft. each.
Bananaland wants population so badly
that it reckons it had better not take the
risk any longer of grilling its guests in the
old wooden gaol at St. Helena. So a new
hotel is to go up, with all modern comforts.
Among those at the outing down the Bay,
given to the Bocal Authorities’ Conference
delegates by Queensland Home Secretary
Appel the other day, was C. H. Buzacott,
who years ago piloted through the Banana-
land Assembly the State’s first Local Gov-
ernment Bill.
Died at Ipswich on August 24, aged 73.
D. T. Keogh, who had represented Rosewood
in the Bananaland Assembly since 1896, ex-
cept for one small break. The gold fever
brought “Dinny” to Victoria in 1854, and
five years later he passed to Bananaland.
After a couple of years of station life, he
went into business in Ipswich, and had lived
there ever since. He never rose above medi-
ocrity in politics, but his brogue added to
the gaiety of the Assembly.

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
h
OU^Y\,
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they-ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo.” Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder”—real “Flor de Cabbage”
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-faintin' name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story—l’ll tell you about it
another time.)
h
The s torekeeper or tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge 9d. for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they’ll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can’t
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
“ DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they're
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That’s the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from A to Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you'll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the. side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer —
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge’ you 22/6 the box
of too DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22 16 to-day, and get the box of xoo
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN
34 QUEEN STREET, MELBOURNE

The Power of the Great Moqul
Though the power of the Great Mogul was tremendous, :7 furnished a striking
example of misplaced energy—misplaced by reason of it’s being direcled where it did
the least amount of good.
The “1.H.C.” ‘Mogul” Gasolene Tractor has, like its namesake, enormous power,
but unlike him its* power is developed just where the greatest possible benefits accrue
from its energies, i.e., the Draw-bar.
That’s one great reason for the phenomenal and universal success of the “1.H.C.”
Tractor—it wastes less hauling energy than any other internal combustion engine in
the wide world.
No need to take our word for the above, because the “1.H.C.” has proved it
again and again in open competition against the world’s next best engines.
In the last competition at Winnipeg, Canada, its competitors were nine in number.
Three of these engines were withdrawn from Draw-bar test, and the remaining
six developed at the Draw-bar an average of 59 5% of their horse-power as against
75% developed by the “1.H.C.”
Not content with a 15% victory in the matter of Draw-bar power development
the “1.H.C.” in the Fuel test at the same competition consumed 33% less fuel per
acre ploughed than the average amount consumed by its seven competitors.
As compared with other engines, buying an “1.11. C.” Tractor is therefore on a
par with buying several draught horses, that not only haul more than any others,
but cost far less to feed.
Of all the internal combustion Tractors now in use in Australia, 4 out of 5 arc
“lll.C.s”—very significant.

—Fills itself in a flash* from any ink supply.

speedily over the paper.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you arc. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them —the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring- strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
ip
fV
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to tlie natural car-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
ORDER FORM.
STAR NOVELTY CO.,

229 Collins Street, Melbourne (Vic.).

HOME CYCLOPEDIA
EMBRACING
THE POPULAR “PLAIN HOME TALK."
By Dr. E. B. FOOTE.
Plates. 36 Chromos. 400 Illustrations. 1248 Pages.
IT IS FREE FROM TECHNICAL TERMS, and shows up Human
Nature asit exists. Here are answered, in plain language, a thou-
sand questions that occur to the minds of young and old, but about
which they feel a delicacy in consulting their physician. Chapters
are devoted to innumerable interesting subjects concerning mar-
riage, which makes it a superior guide to the action of men and
women both in and out of marriage. No man or woman who

LITTLE BOY SUFFERED NINE MONTHS
WITH PNEUMONIA AND BRONCHITIS.
WAS REDUCED TO A SKELETON—HIS LIFE WAS DESPAIRED OF
A REMARKABLE CURE—THE RESULT SPEAKS FOR ITSELF.
“I am writing to tell you of the good Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery has done my
little baby boy,” writes Mrs. F. de Grosse, 61
O’Connell Street, Newtown, N.S.W. “He
was very toad with Pneumonia and Bron-
chitis for nine months. He was so bad
that he was ordered to the hospital, in-
deed he was in two different hospitals. His
little throat was quite black on both sides
with the constant turning of his head,
which bruised the glands of the throat. At
other times he used to lie as if dead all
day. The doctors did all they could for
him, tout they sent him home from the hos-
pital, not expecting him to live, and really
I thought that would be the last of him, he
was like a little skeleton.
“I was strongly recommended to try Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery, tout I thought, as
he was so far gone, there was little hope
for him. Anyway, my friend brought a
bottle of Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery, and
he was just able to sip a dose of it. After
a few doses he seemed to get brighter, so
I felt this was going to cure him, and he
got all right in no time. I can’t be thank-
ful enough to Dr. Sheldon’s New Dis-
covery, for it saved his life. No one would
believe the difference it made in such a short
time. He is quite cured, and has grown a
fine, fat child. He is only two years and
five months old, but anyone would take
him for five years old.”

The Buccaneers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE hansoms slur through the London
mud, and the Bank of England Jeers
Like a fat old thief where their hoof-
beats thud —all hail to the Buccan-
neers!
A thunder of waves goes swirling aft, and
the punkah swings at noon ;
For a man shall live by his fighting-craft—
not feed from a nurse’s spoon!
And the world cries out for the fighting-men,
for the pirates stout and lean;
Lo, the weaklings toil with a clerkly pen,
dumb cogs in the great machine!
But the pirates sail for the distant lands,
and the liner swings and veers;
Yea, a man shall win with his brains and
hands —all hail to the Buccaneers!
1 heard the chime of the temple-bells, and a
cable came at noon,
And I heard the distant broker’s yells in
London from old Rangoon.
The "wireless” splutters from ship to ship,
with a message of stocks and shares,
And the pirate lignts with a tightened lip in
the battle of bulls and bears !
llis orders flash for a thousand miles, and a
war-shout fierce and strange
Goes forth o’ nights through the star-filled
aisles to the lords of the Stock Ex-
change !
For women may call but men must dare, so
the forthright viking steers ;
And the cable croons in its ocean-lair, "All
hail to the Buccaneers!”
There are code-books thumbed in Mandalay.
and the price of copper and tin
Is conned with care where the punkahs sway
and the grey shark shows his fin.
Slim cables slipping from sea to "sea, long
ships with their, lights aglow ;
And the Morse must chatter with rattling
key when the pirates whisper low !
For wealth means women woman and
wine are part of a man’s desire ;
So the pirate stands to the firing-line with
the earth in his fighting-hire!
The patient drones in the London hive loaf
through the dull brown years,
But they loudly sing who plot and strive —
All hail to the Buccaneers !
The bells may summon to evening prayer,
but there’s death in the priest’s refrain ;
And we bow in desert and city square to the
ancient Gods of Gain!
The clamoring wheels that claw the track
breathe an anthem fierce and new ;
And the pirate’s bags are swift to pack
when the call comes whistling through !
Yea, Solomon's ships from olden Tyre bore
spoils for the kingly feast;
And a man still thrills with the same desire,
and he ravages West and East!
'Tis a thieving world, where a Man must
live no matter who doubts or fears ;
And this is the song that the ages give— All
hail to the Buccaneers!
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
Callaghan Scores.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WAY BACK in the early ’6o’s the
squattages of Avoca, Glenorchy
and Caribee joined each other.
Their owners had acquired nearly all the
water-frontages by the process known to
Parliament as pre-emption, and flocks and
herds browsed and waxed fat on their ver-
dure-clad hills and plains.
It was a blistering day in December, when,
after completing a “bang-tail muster,” all-
hands on Avoca station were drafting and
branding. The stockyard was full of bustle
and blasphemy. The dust rose in dense red
clouds from the swirl and rush of the cattle
as they plunged to and fro, now on the
charge, then on the retreat. There the
owner of Avoca, Michael Callaghan, by the
grace of Arthur Hunter Palmer', justice of
the peace for the State of Queensland, was in
his element, whirling a short stick on the
“curl” of bellicose bullocks; there a jaclc-
eroo would fall flop over the cap-rail in an
agitated attempt to escape the horns of an
aggrieved cow; and above all hung that red
panoply of profanity and dust. Patsy Cal-
laghan, a gaudy youth of 20, was in charge
of the branding, and no calf over six months
old escaped being decorated with the MC in-
signia. The bustle, dust and language were
at high pressure when McFarlane, of Glen-
orchy, rode up.
“Good day to ye, Mac,” called out Calla-
ghan, as he balanced himself on the top rail
out of the reach of a bellowing steer:
“I’ve all yer cattle drafted out in the little
yard.”
“Ah’m real obleeged tae ye, Mick,” re-
plied McFarlane. Then as his eyes roved
over to where the branded calves Avere
yarded, his cheery mood changed, and with
that solemnity with which the “unco guid”
comment on the sins of the backslider, he
said:
“Michael Callaghan, hae ye nae respeck
for the law whatever? Dae ye no see yon
bull calf wi’ my earmark on —an’ yer brand?
Mon, Mick, Ah’m wae tae think ye wad
play sick a plisky on a guid neebor.”
“Oh, ’tis that b’y Patsy’s blunderin’ agin.
Here, Patsy, ye rip av the wurrld, for what
did ye brand Mister McFarlane’s calf?
Run out two unbranded heifer calves and
put them in his mob.” Then to the stern-
visaged Scot he said: “We’ll call that
square now, Mac!”
“Oh, aye!” returned McFarlane; “but.
Ah’ll tak’ a wee bit luk amang the calves.
There might be a wheen mair o’ the Glen-
orchy breed that Patsy blundered aboot.”
The investigation furnishing no further
cause for the deliverance of a homily, the
customary tot of rum was negotiated, and
Avhen McFarlane had vanished among the
timber, the branding was completed, and the
calves were let out to their mothers.
The calf with the Glenorchy earmark and
the Avoca brand started sucking a Caribee
cow.
Michael Callaghan gave a yelp of aston-
ishment and exploded.
“The murtherin’ Scotch robber!” he yelled.
“To think the mealy-mouthed rapparee wud
be takin’ me in Avid his psalm-snuffling, an’
him earmarkin’ Jack Paterson’s calf. And
me two good heifers are gone! The curse o
Grummet on him!”
Then to Patsy he roared:
“Take thot calf an’ drive him down to the
Sugarloaf paddock, an’ lave him there. If
Paterson comes along an’ foinds that calf
here wid its mother, it won’t be that Scawtch
thief will get the blame—it’ll be Mick
Callaghan’s brand that’ll show up first. Devil
burn McFarlane, whatever comes and goes.
And thus began the feud between the
houses of Avoca and Glenorchy. Callaghan
and McFarlane impounded one another s
cattle with zeal and regularity from that day
forward, and communications between the
squattages ceased. The innocent cause, the
Caribee calf, strayed from the Sugarloaf pad-
dock on to the Caribee run, and at the next
yearly muster of that station, did some fur-
ther straying—into its meat-cask. Jack
Paterson, like his neighbors, was no re-
spector of brands, but in this one instance at
least he came to his own —unconsciously.
* * * *
Two years later, in deference to the re-
peated demands of the budding democracy of
the settled districts, the squatters’ leases
were broken, and half the runs resumed for
settlement. Then began a feverish scramble
to add to the remaining areas by every
means within the law, or not too far outside
it. “Lamb’s Act,” as it was called, pro-
vided for the selection of agricultural hold-
ings of G4O acres at 20s. per acre, first-class
pastoral holdings of 1280 acres at 10s. per
acre, and of second-class pastoral land 2560
acres at ss. per acre. Most holdings selected
by the squatters, their sisters, their cousins,
their aunts, and their grandmothers and
grandchildren were second-class pastoral— i
the officials who classified the lands being
amenable to reason if the reason bore her
late Majesty’s superscription. As the supply
of land for the moment exceeded the demand
so far as real honest settlement was con-
cerned, the run-holders ha.d a good time of it.
They placed each of their hands in posses-
sion of a block of land, with the reservation
• that the deeds should be transferred to :the
squatter when the land was acquired in fee
simple. Three 640-acre blocks along the
creek in the Avoca Sugarloaf paddock were
surveyed, and on the day they were to be
thrown open, Callaghan, his brother Dermot,
and Patsy started at daylight for the Lands
Office at the adjacent township to select
them. But owing to the thirst of the eldest
Callaghan, who dallied at a roadside pub,
the office was reached just as McFarlane
and two station-hands issued therefrom, and
it was with a wild wealth of profanity that
Callaghan received the information that por-
tions 17v, 18v, and 19v, parish of Blank,
county of Dash, had been selected by
Walter Thornton, Wilson, and Don-
ald McFarlane.
“Thim two swells what tuk up those selec-
tions are dummies,” Callaghan tried to ex-
plain to the Land Agent, but that official
regarded him with a stony stare, and seemed
as disinterested as though Callaghan had
proffered the information that Mars is in-
habited.
“And who the devil is Donald McFarlane,
annyhow?” blustered Callaghan. “The ould
thief’s name is Malcolm, an’ more, be token
he have a selection of four square miles on !
his own run already.”
The tired-looking official eyed his inter-
locutor now with some show of interest, and
replied languidly:
“My good sir, the Government doesn’t keep
a staff of detectives to ascertain who selects
its lands. Now, for instance, on this same
station we have the names of John Styles,
James Rafferty, William”
But Callaghan had gone. The string of
names recited in an official monotone tallied
with those of his own pet dummies.
Next morning, when the two new selectors
on Avoca rode over to examine their new’
holdings, they found that the pegs were not
within two chains of the creek, and the land i
was wrongly described as water-frontage. |
They reported the matter to McFarlane.
The following day the pegs were again in
the creek bank, and when Callaghan went j
over from the hoinestead to investigate he
found a piece of paper tacked to one of them
on wffiich was painfully scrawled:
“Read Deuteronomy xxvii.-17, ye Irish j
gowk.”
Callaghan rode 10 miles out to 1
a piously-inclined shepherd, and read. Then
• he started to lie low.
Within three months the selections were
fenced, and a mob of bullocks depastured
thereon. They were stores from a station
in the West —in those days referred to as the
Never Never. Then Callaghan rose to the
occasion.
Acting on his complaint that McFarlane
was running his cattle on the newly-acquired
selection —presumptive evidence of dummy-
ing—the Crown Lands Ranger paid a sur-
prise visit, but the two new chums showed
receipts for all the stock running on their
holding, and Callaghan realised that McFar-
lane expected trouble, and had arranged for
contingencies.
Then Callaghan became friendly with his
despoilers. Night after night he could be
seen squatted at the hut fire, swapping yarns
with Thornton and Wilson, and, hearing of
this, McFarlane’s vigilance took but short
sleep.
It was great fattening country, that
Sugarloaf paddock, and in six months the
herd was ready for the Ennogera yards.
McFarlane rode over with two of his stock-
men one morning to drove the bullocks down
the range, but all signs of life had vanished
from the selections. The ashes in the fire-
place were cold, and everything pointed to
an abandonment under the Act of 1868.
Mac swore, then pondered, then swore
again. What did it all mean? Had the
cattle broken out and strayed, and had the
men gone after them ? This was not feasible;
the fence was unbroken. Something had
gone wrong—something beyond McFarlane’s
comprehension—and, leaving the stockmen
there, he rode at a hand gallop to the next
township.
At the first pub he drew rein, and pro-
ceeded to the bar to slacken his drouth. As
he poured out his “wee chillick o’ whusky,”
his glance fell on the form of Wilson, huddled
up on the sofa of the bar parlor. Gulping
down his refreshment, Mac made a bound at
the sleeper, and, shaking him much as a bull-
dog Would a terrier, he roared out:
“Whaur’s ma beasties, ye drunken limb o’
Beelzebub?”
While Wilson was struggling up into a
sitting posture with an incoherent “Don’t
mind if I do. ole fellow!” Thornton stag-
gered into the bar. The man from Glenorchy,

I Right Arm was Distorted and Utterly Useless through Severed and I
Contracted Sinew —Lacerated Wrist Developed Alarming Symptoms— I
