A WONDERFUL VICTORY FOR ZAM-BUK, AFTER
DOCTOR GAVE UP ALL HOPE
FOR sensational features, the following
story of Mrs. C. Jones, residing at
208 Denison Street, Camperdown,
Sydney, would be hard to equal. She says :
“ One day, when cleaning a window, I went
to push it up, and instead of going round a
couch to do so, I leant over. The consequence
was I overbalanced, and my right hand went
through the window-pane, fearfully lacerating
my wrist and severing one of the sinews,
which flew up the arm.
“ 1 went to the hospital, and for an hour
the doctors had me on the operating table,
searching for the contracted sinew. They
finally located and joined the sinew, and put
eight stitches in the wound.
“ The wound itself obstinately refused to
heal, and proud flesh formed, with terrible
inflammation and irritation. 1 could hardly
keep myself from tearing the wound to pieces.
1 went over to the hospital, and after scraping
the wound the doctor wanted to burn and
cauterise the parts, but it was only making
matters worse.

having a free sample pot in the house, I made up my mind to try it.

saturated some wadding with the balm and applied it to the contracted sinew. One night, on re-
tiring to bed, I propped my arm up by the pillow as usual, and on waking in the morning you can

with a glare of battle in his eye, strode up
to the new arrival, and yelled the query
fiercely at hun.
Thornton steadied himself against the bar
with both hands, and, with a look of com-
passion at the figure that was dancing, ges-
ticulating and frothing around him from
every available space in the bar-room, ven-
ded to ask what the trouble was about.

‘.‘Aye, just them. What’s came o’ them?
Deil fry ye for an impudent thief —will ye
no answer?”

the bar. Beaming a joyous, inebriated smile

laghan for 30 bob a head. Cumanavea-

and locked the door. Then he emptied the
raving squatter out into the road, and barri-
caded the pub for a siege.
It came. Amid a torrest of blistering pro-
fanity in two languages came a rain of rocks
on to the hotel windows. Not a piece of
glass the size of a half-crown remained on
the outside of that pub. When the sergeant
and his constable rushed down from the
other end of the town to see who had broken
the crust and let Hell loose, MeFarlane was
bellowing out a final curse, half English, half
Gaelic-—an _ imprecation more blastiferous
even than its predecessors—and was wheel-
ing his horse for a break-neck ride to Glen-
orchy.
He was met at the door of his home by the
nurse, who welcomed him by saying:
“Ye’ll be pleased, Maister MeFarlane, to
ken that a wee stranger has come to bide at
Glenorchy.”
“Aye!” grunted Mac, “an’ what is ut, a
laddie?”
“Na,” smiled the old dame; “just another
bonnie girl.”
“Guid God!” roared MeFarlane. “There
goes portion 19v too. I was sure ’twould be
a laddie. There’s no Donald MeFarlane the
noo.
The three forfeited selections were shortly
afterwards thrown open in one block as nas-
toral land, and that was duly selected by
Timothy Callaghan, who had boen 1 yin ol in
the churchyard in Skibbereen, County Tip-
nerary. for three years.
So Avoca run was consolidated. Callaghan
had scored.
CABER.
The Trapper Trapped.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Did I tell you that little trapdoor
spider experience of mine? No?
Well, I was havin’ a smoke under
a tree once, after a bit of a snack in the
middle of the day, when a hornet—one of
those big yellow-and-black chaps—’lights on
the ground a yard or two in front of me,
walks along on tiptoe a few inches, and then
lifts up—what do you think? Why, the
roof of a trapdoor spider’s diggin’s. Just
lifts it up a little way, quick, and lets it drop
again ; and before you can say knife, he’s
up on a low branch hidin’ behind a leaf.
“Aha!” says I, “there’s goin’ to be some
sport here,” and I takes the pipe out of my
mouth and sits as still as a knot on a log
and waits for the next act.
Quite gently, old Trappie raises the lid a
bit, and peeps out through the crack to see
who’s knockin’.
“No one there,” says he. “Very sing’lar
thing. P’raps it was the wind.” Shuts the
door. Down comes Auld Hornie again.
Opens the door a little wider this time. Pets
her go, whack! and up again behind his leaf
in a. jiffy, laughin’ to himself. Out comes
Spi in a hurry, and stands on his doorstep.
Pears to be a bit wild. Has a look round;
can’t see anyone but me; scratches his head.
Could ve swore I heard someone laugh, and
run away,” says he. “Take me oath it’s
some of that Billybung push. I’ll lay for
em.” Goes in again, slammin’ the door
behind him. Same performance again; and
the lid _goes back with a bang that nearly
shakes it off its hinges.
Ilornie only has time to dodge behind a
tuft of grass before Trappie rushes out, the
maddest spider you ever see, and swearin’
like a sailor’s parrot. “Oho !” says he, shakin’
his fist, “I heard you, y’ variegated young
larrikins! Wait till I catch you! By the
Holy Ancient Matilda, I’ll serve y’ up raw
for tea—that’s what I’ll do to you! I’ll
wait behind the door, and nail ’em next
time,” says he, and he goes inside again.
I can see things are movin’ up to a finish,
and when Hornie comes up smiliif for the
fourth round, he no sooner raises the roof
than Trappie’s out like a shot. Of course,
Ilornie’s been workin’ for this, and he’s up
10ft. in the air like winkin’ with Spide in his
clutches.
It’s “up in a balloon, boys,” for Spide, but
he fights for his natural, and blest if old
I ootball-Jacket can get any further with
him. The pair of ’em just hangs there in
mid-air clawin’ each other; then suddenly
down comes old Yellow-and-Black ivhop-oh!
on the broad of his back, buzzin’ like a
circ’lar-saw, with Spide on top of him.
They’re a bit jolted up, but soon get to work,
rollin’ over and over together, knockin’ the
furniture about, and abusin’ each other some-
thin’ shockin’.
Pretty soon I can see poor old Hornie’s all
out, and weak’nin’ fast. Must’ve jinked his
back that time. Spide gets him under directly,
and sits on his chest, and biffs him left and right
till he sends him to sleep. Then he puts in a
strangle-holt and finishes him. Good old
Spide’s just about done up, and wants a
spell-oh pretty bad; but he ain’t takin’ any
risks of Missus Hornie turnin’ up with a
fire-stick to see if her old man’s dropped into
a pub on his way home from the butcher’s—
not him! He grabs Hornie deceased by one
of his legs right away, and lugs him home,
and dumps him into the sepulchre, fairly
falling in a-top of him.
Then the “curtain” drops.
WILL READE.
The Bay of Rest.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE day was dream-blue—a sun-maid-
| en’s eyes
_l_ Love-litten; the bay was all rippled
with blue,
The green trees dripped pearls, and soft blue
were the skies,
The grass a mad medley of sunbeams and
dew.
A thousand birds warbled —voluptuous
notes!
The spring-tide lapped lazily over the wall;
Near-by a brown boatman stood hosing his
boats;
He winked at me, slyly enjoying it all.
The day was a dream-day. I spied with
delight
A little blue ’rigger, new-painted and trim.
With new-varnished sculls and a gun’l-line
white;
My heart gave a leap, and I winked back at
him.
The day was in raptures!—a sun-maid sur-
prised
In fondling and kissing fresh wet leaves in
play;
I heard a Voice whisper: “That man is de-
spised
That meekly to labor goes townward to-
day!”
By bright harbor-waters, by cool bays of
dream.
The blue sky above me, the blue tide below,
I naddled the blue boat a-down the still
stream,
Past gardens where birds sing and love-
blossoms blow.
The shimmer of sun-gold delighted the sea;
Far-off an impalpable, rosy-pink haze
Veiled ocean and sky-line mysteriously
In vapory purples and radiant greys.
The hills were all dimpled with magical
light,
And down to the water’s edge verdure was
spread
With happy sun-buds, that all summer in-
vite
Glad fairies to dance and to dream when a-
bed.
Now two blue-eyed mermaids lay sunning
themselves
Like beautiful odalisques lullabied there,
’Mid yellow sand-roses and soft-singing elves
And bright rippled waters —luxurious pair!
“Oh, bonny blue boat, ’tis the blue Bay of i
Rest!”
I dallied unseen on the crisp dappled sands,
Till mermaids espying, divined me a guest,
Blew kisses of welcome with slender brown
hands.
Near-come I looked into the dreamiest eyes—
Eyes lustrous and tender, with love-light
aglow.
Eyes tilled with sea-lore, and wondrously
wise,
That only a mermaid may ponder or know.
On warm cheeks there galloped a fugitive
red,
Swift banners of shyness; such ceased to
depart : - - )
When red lips were held up for kisses, and
said
Were glad welcome speeches that sang in my
heart.
Then couched on a sand-dune, bud-spangled
and grassed,
We breathlessly gossiped, sipped afternoon
tea
From murex tea-cups with pearl saucers,
and passed
The merriest hours, a luxurious three. |
They’d w r onderful ornaments, exquisite
gems—
From what deep sea-caverns exultingly
won?
Shell ear-rings and coral with delicate
stems,
Sea-blossoms as red as the summer-red sun;
And lazuli necklaces, bracelets of pearl,
And fluorspar finger-rings; wavy and wet,
And ambergris scented, the hair of each girl
Was coifed with a sea-pink, coquettishly set.
Their soft-throated voices were lute-strings
that filled
The. ambient air with inebriant sound,
Their laughter was wave-wash and sun-
shimmer spilled |
On red-burnished beaches where white pearls
abound. j
Ineffable music! Such sounds have I heard
Whene’er the sea-wind in an ecstasy brings
The shrill eerie cry of a far ocean-bird,
And cunningly blends it with sea-inurmur-
ings.
While sunshine was dusting their brown hair
with gold
A thousand strange questions were heaped
upon me.
A thousand strange tales of the sea-folk were
told,
A thousand wild stories of wind, sky and sea.
The day was as blue as the mermaids’ blue
eyes,
Warm earth an enchantment of blue, gold
and green.
And in the wide sea and the wind-flickered
skies
No sign of distress and no blemish were
seen.
Their lullaby-voices encompassed my heart:
Sweet-thoughted, I passed into passionate
rest,
Dreamed of a new life of entrancements,
apart
From bicker and barter and strenuous quest.
And when I awoke there were no greeting
words,
But dark water frolicked, and in the dark
blue
The stars flickered madly like numberless
birds,
And near me two sea-pinks lay shining with
dew!
The bonny blue boat bears me farward and
near,
The summer-sun scatters his rays on my
quest;
“To-morrow !” I say ; and to-morrow is here,
But lost to me ever the blue Bay of Rest!
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
